O, my sons, since I have completed for you this book, which is the culmination of the science of medicine, and by its completion I have achieved the goal, and through its expositions and its explanations, it seems to me that I must fulfill this to you with this treatise, which is a part of manual operation, that is, surgery.
Because manual operation is neglected in our region and in our time, almost entirely lost, so that the science of it is greatly diminished, and its practice is abandoned; and nothing remains of it but some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which are mostly corrupted and fallen into errors and uncertainty, so that their intentions are obscured and their power and art are weakened.
And it seems to me that I should revive it with the arrangement of this treatise, according to the method of exposition, explanation, and abbreviation; and that it should come with the forms of the cautery instruments and of the other instruments of the work, both by way of explanation and by preparation of them, which is necessary.
But the reason why a skilled craftsman with his hands is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and its practitioner must first be trained in the science of anatomy, which is lacking in Gaul, so that he knows the positions of the limbs and their shapes, and their connections and separations; and he must have knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, and their number and arrangement; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their origin.
And for this reason Hippocrates says that in name it is much, but in practice little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not knowledgeable in what we have said about anatomy, is not exempt from falling into error by which men will be killed, as I have seen many of them, because some are trained in this science and practice it without knowledge and experience.
For I saw a foolish and unskilled physician incise a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he cut some arteries of the neck so that a great quantity of blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who undertook to remove a stone from a man who had it since his youth, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I was called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and a black, crippled man came to him who had a fracture in his thigh; and the physician, through his ignorance,  extracted the fracture, over the wound, with cushions and splints with strong constriction, and did not allow the wound to breathe (i.e., did not allow for proper air circulation).
And then he loosened it according to his desires, and then he bound it, and then he left it for several days and commanded that we not loosen the binding; and it remained thus until the thigh and foot became suppurated, and it came to ruin (i.e., resulted in loss of the limb or even death).
And I was called to him, and I prepared to untie the binding, and tranquility was achieved and thus a lessening of his pains.
But however, corruption was already confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated for several days, until the malignancy of it was magnified with its lord (i.e., until the infection spread extensively).
The reason is that because a hot cancer is pure melancholic humor, then it is not fitting that it be approached with iron at all, but it should be in a limb that is sacrificed (i.e.,  amputated) to prevent its spread.
And therefore, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation with the hand be divided into two parts: that is, in an operation to which health is associated, and in a work with which there is peril in the greatest dispositions (i.e.,  operations with high risk).
And I have already explained this in every part of this book where operations occur which involve error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to observe this and leave that, so that fools do not find a way to speak and dishonor you.
Therefore, take up your instruments with diligence and with God's protection, and treat your patients with happiness and firmness.
And use the best method by the grace of God, leading to health and laudable success, and avoid terrible illnesses of difficult healing, and abstain from those things that make you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name does not overcome you: for this is more lasting to your glory, and more highly regarded in the world and later among your sanctities.
Galen already said in some of his admonitions: Do not treat a bad illness, lest you be called a bad physician".
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter is ordered from head to foot, on the forms of instruments and the tools of cauterization, and on what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of suction and perforation, and of cupping glasses, and of wounds, and of the extraction of arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of the treatment of sprains and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and of the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary that I describe the qualities of the benefits of this cautery and its harms, and in what constitution cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the discourse on the quality of the benefit of cautery and its harm is a long and prolix discourse, and subtle in science, and a hidden secret; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they have disagreed on it, and a small part of their discourses suffices for me, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all complexions universally, whether with matter or without matter, except for two complexions, which are hot complexions without matter.
In the hot and dry complexion with matter there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial to it, and others say the contrary, that cautery is not suitable for a disease which is caused by heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is unsuitable that a hot and dry disease should be healed with that which is hot and dry.
And I refute those who say the contrary, that benefit is done by cautery in every hot and dry disease that comes to the bodies of men, for when you compare the human body and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the human body cold.
And I say, regarding this discourse, that experience has revealed this to me on several occasions.
However, it is not appropriate that this should apply to someone who has already been tried and exercised in the chapter on cautery through previous practice, and who has known the diversity of the complexions of men and the disposition of illnesses in themselves, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the duration of those.
But in other complexions, there is no fear for you, especially in cold and moist diseases, for all physicians have agreed on these, and they are not in disagreement regarding the benefit of cautery in those.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire and its goodness, over cautery with burning medicine, is because fire is simple, the action of which does not reach except the member which is cauterized and does not harm the other members joined to it except with little harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches even those members which are distant from it, and quickly brings to the member a disease of difficult healing and quickly kills.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this except that it supersedes. And it is already known to us by experiment and by long service of study with the art concerning the truth of the causes; and for this I am excused from the length of the speech. And if it were not that it is not written in my book in this place, I would reveal to you a secret concerning fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies and its expulsion by which it expels illnesses, with a philosophical demonstrative speech, which would be sharp in your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree on the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery, placing springtime as the best time.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm caused by the accidental time is submerged in the benefit of that which is achieved with cautery, and especially if the cautery is for necessary pains or strong and urgent pains, which do not permit delay because it is feared that harm may come from those who are slow to act,  a harm greater than the small harm incurred by acting at any time.
And this does not occur to you, O sons, this is only estimated by the vulgar people and foolish physicians, that to that illness which is healed by cautery there is no remission at all times, and they posit that consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they think, for cautery is nothing other than a medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
However, superfluous cautery is performed over and above other medicine because of the speed of its effectiveness and the power of its action and the greatness of its effect.
And it is possible that his illness will return at some time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his illness, and his strength, and that which is prepared in his body from the accumulation of superfluities in him, and that which has reached him through his acquisition of food and similar causes; but if the illness to which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a limb with few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth for pain and similar things, it is possible that the pain will not return, but this is done only in small cases.
And a more common saying is that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and this is a true saying, not in the sense that they understand it, for they believe that it is not a cure that benefits with medicine or with any other means besides cautery.
And the reason is the opposite of that; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is only when we administer methods of cure in certain illnesses, and those medicines do not work, and then at the end, one administers cautery, and it benefits in that case. And therefore, from here it is clear that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the understanding of the common people and most foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the purity of gold and the nobility of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not scar, although this is not entirely true, for I have already proven this and have found that it does not do this except in some bodies and not others, and cautery with this is good and better than with iron, as they said; however, when you do cautery with fire with gold, it is not clear to you, because it is ignited according to the quantity you want, due to the hardness of the gold, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you apply too much heat, the gold melts in the fire, and some sheets fall into the fire so that the artisan is distracted by that.
And therefore, cautery with iron is done more easily and more readily with accuracy of operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those from the head to the feet, so that I may easily give the inquirer what he seeks.
The first chapter.
This cauterization is applied to dampness and coldness affecting the brain; the accidents of which are two causes: that is, the cause of excessive and copious rheumy flux, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and much sleep, and toothache, and throat pain, and universally of every illness that comes from coldness, such as paralysis, lethargy, apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The method of this cauterization is this: first, order the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine, purifying the head, for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient, their age, and their habit.
And then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit between your hands on a low bench and place his hands on his feet.
And then place the root of your palm against the space above the root of the nose between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that spot, mark that place with a probe, and then ignite the olive cautery, of which this is the form:
And then place that [cautery] on the spot marked with the probe, in such a way that it descends, with which your hand should be gradually tightened, and you should rotate it.
And then lift your hand slightly and observe the place, and if you see some quantity of the head of the bone uncovered, like the quantity of a grain of barley, then lift your hand, and if not, return your hand with that iron, or with another like it, if it is cold, until that which I have told you is seen of the bone.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the place and leave it for three days.
And then place on it lint soaked in wine, and leave it there until the burn scar is destroyed. And then treat the place with tetrafarmacon ointment until it is healed.
And it is said that when the wound remains open, it better discharges the arm, because it is better and more helpful.
And some of them say that the elbow should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held there until some part of the bone's substance is charred.
And then scrape away what is burnt from the bone, and then it should be treated.
And others say that with the cautery, reach the back, until it penetrates strongly into the bone, until the bone is burnt, and this is the method for a small ulcer: And they believe that vapors from the head are expelled from that place.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then it should be healed until it is consolidated.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men, as a way of fear; and leave that, it seems to me, better is health when it is; for the head is weakened by the solution of its continuity as we have testified in other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a fragile nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and I use and work with that one for health.
Chapter Two.
Of cautery of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs throughout the head in a patient, and he uses leeches and cupping and purgatives and plasters and poultices and cauterizations and ointments and similar things, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we have recounted and none of those things benefit him, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution by nature; and if it is not fragile and finds much great cold, then cauterize that with another cautery a little below that one. And then cauterize that [area] on the temple of his head with a cautery until the scab is formed. Incise the scab and let the bone be uncovered to the extent we have recounted, and cauterize that with a cautery in the back part of the head in the place which is called the vas – and this is the place where a cupping glass is placed. And lighten your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, for the patient thereby finds much great pain, different from the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head.
And I would say this cautery in its place, for it is necessary that the cautery with which the temples of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter three.
On the cauterization of recent migraine.
When a sharp pain arises in the head, or a spreading pain within the eye, let the head be evacuated with purifying medicines, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have described in the sections on illnesses; and if nothing benefits, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with a sharp burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and peel it, and bind its extremities on both ends. Then incise the place of the pain in the temple with a lancet, until a place is made under the skin, until it is hidden.
Then bind it tightly with a compress for a constant constriction, and leave it for the space of three hours; and then unbind it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then apply a cotton ball soaked in butter until the area becomes soft.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron in this manner:  heat the cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called a nail or peg, because its head is shaped like a clove, in which there is a certain curvature, and in the middle of it there is a small eminence.
And then place it on the location of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the flesh below is not burned — and rotate the iron little by little, with speed.
And if the quantity of flesh burned by the heat of the cautery is about half of it, lift your hand so that the artery below is not burned, so that there is no flow of blood.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place that on the area, and leave it for three days; and then place cotton soaked in oil, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of a knife, as with a cautery, and be careful not to cut an artery in this particular, not-ancient, type of headache.
Chapter four.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic headache.
When you treat a headache with what we have said in the previous treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and sometimes because of the illness, what we said about the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire is insufficient, then it is necessary that you heat the knife cautery until it is white-hot, after which you mark on the painful area with a line whose length is half a finger, or thereabouts; and pause your hand for a moment and press it until you incise the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be wary of the continuation of the jaw, which is moved during mastication, and that the muscle not be injured and the nerve which moves it, and that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the guarding and caution against the flow of blood from the artery that is incised, because in its incision there is danger, and especially to him who ignores what he is doing and is not studious and experienced; and leaves the operation in him at the beginning.
And I will come with a reminder of the treatment of accidental blood flow from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if sometimes this illness is not relieved by this cauterization and the patient's body is tolerable, cauterize it in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the constriction of the artery with a cautery having two blades, and this cauterization is better and more suitable.
Chapter 5.
Of the cauterization of earaches.
When pain in the ear comes from cold and is treated with laxatives and with the other treatments which I have mentioned in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery, and this is called a puncture, of which this is the form:
And then puncture, then ignite, and let it be moved around the whole ear as a wheel is revolved, or in the circumference of each part if the pain is in a specific part. Lengthen the cautery from the root of the ear little by little, after you have marked the spot with a probe.
And if the cautery makes ten punctures in the whole ear, or whichever one it is, then afterwards treat the places until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the cauterization of a cough.
A cough which is cured with cauterization is only one of the types that comes from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of illnesses.
Avoid cauterization of the type that originates from dryness or from a spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of cough with bleeding, purgatives, and gargles, and your treatment is ineffective, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side from the affected part, because softening does not occur except in the part that appears healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize it with the cautery at the very tip of the ear, a little above the corner of the head, and another in the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the little finger, and hold your hand with the cautery until half the thickness of a finger's breadth is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a kind of small knife, the form of which is shown before. However, it is more subtle to do it a little like this; and it is necessary to cut away some of the superfluous thickness.
And then take care of the area with what has been previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
On the cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with what we have said, and your treatment is ineffective, and the patient has no fever, then cauterize them with four cauteries: on all the corners of their head; and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery on the nape of the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as described above.
And it is also cauterized above the stomach; and it is better afterwards; and then let it be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
On cauterization for forgetfulness done by flame.
So that at the beginning large potions and purgative pills are given to the patient.
And then shave the entire head of the patient and apply to their neck a plaster of cetre described in the treatise of plasters.
And apply that several times, for that is a manner of cautery. And do this according to the same order which I have already said.
And therefore if he is healed by those means, it is good; and if not, then make for him three cauteries in the back part of his head, which descend from the uppermost part of the head down to the lowermost part of the neck. And place between each cautery the width of a finger, and then treat the cautery with what has been prescribed.
But if you add more and the patient tolerates it, then apply a cautery in the middle place; and if you want greater addition, then cauterize that over the two horns (possibly referring to areas of the head).
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form previously given.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the whole body.
So that you proceed in the purification of the head with leeches and with what we have said; and then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery over all the horns (possibly referring to the temples) of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries over the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body to find it and the patient tolerates it and the illness is severe, then cauterize it with four cauteries over the spinal vertebrae, and finally with a cautery until it is burned from the tailbone up, and raise your hand. And then treat it until it is healed, and this should be an olive oil cautery.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore it is necessary that you purify his brain at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatment which we have described in the section when the patient is strong and can tolerate the removal of humors.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then administer gargles and masticatories that purify the brain, before that for many days, with improvement of the diet.
Then shave the head of the patient, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery on his occiput, and cauterize on each temple of his head. And if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I have mentioned, having paralysis and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, as was previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small/fine cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humors and gross phlegm, then cauterize that with the cauteries which we have mentioned in the treatment of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is an excess [of humors] tending towards melancholy, and the patient's body is humid, then give him something to drink that cleanses his brain, according to what has been explained previously in the [earlier] section.
Then shave the patient's head, and then make a properly-made [medicinal] plaster for him, and then place that in the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated upright and held securely on all sides.
Then apply a liniment; and it is most worthy [or effective] to use old sheep's fat. And warm that [liniment] over the fire with moderate heat, which the said [patient] will suffer when it is applied to him. Then pour that [liniment] into the middle of his head within the circle, and leave it there until it is cold.
And do this to the patient once a week, with his other good regimen, until he is healed; or if you wish, cauterize that [area] making small punctures according to the need, but hold your hand with a cautery so arranged: this is a type of cautery that moistens the brain evenly.
Then apply that cotton soaked in butter or goose grease.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the cauterization of water descending into the ear.
When the onset of water in the ear is revealed to you by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient a drink that will purify his head, and keep him from all humidity.
And make him sweat in a bath of some kind for several days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries on the two temples if the onset of the water is in both ears, or on one side if the onset of the water is in one ear.
Incise with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the nape of the neck; and let the cauteries be lengthwise in the width of the temples.
And beware of a flow of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see a flow of blood from it, stop it with any medicine which is possible for you.
And I come with the wisdom which is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of them and the caution of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below the two bones with two cauteries placed lengthwise.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the cauterization of old tears (likely referring to long-standing wounds or sores).
When the tears from the eyes are old and persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest location of the head outside, and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold phlegmatic humors, then cauterize that with the cautery which we recounted in the beginning chapter about water falling into the eyes, and with a cautery in the middle of the head and with two cauteries in the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two bones. And if it needs additional treatment, then cauterize it in all the space of the corner of the eye above the end of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the cauterization of nasal odor (or, more likely, a foul smell from the nose).
When you treat this with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not work, then you prepare and give the patient decoctions to drink for three nights; and then shave his head, and cauterize it with a medium-sized cautery, with an olive-shaped cautery. And then cauterize it with a clove-shaped cautery, with two cauteries above the two eyebrows, just below the hair.
And be careful with the cautery that you do not burn it (the skin/area).
And of the clove-shaped cautery, this is the form (or shape).
Chapter 15.
Concerning the cauterization for the softening (or mollification) of the eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize, do so above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries in each part, and let them be elongated towards the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand too much with the cautery, but only so that a third part of the eyelid is hardened/cauterized.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the cauterization of the eyelid when the hairs are turned inward toward the eye and prick the eye.
When the hairs of the eyelid are turned inward so that they prick the eye, cauterization in these cases is done in two ways, for cauterization is either done with fire or with a burning medicine.
If cauterizing with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to shave their eyelashes as one shaves them, until they are lengthened and thinned; and if it pricks near their root, then bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so that they are not harmed until the hairs have grown back.
Then, when they have grown back and thinned, place the patient's head in your lap, and then mark on the eyelid with a probe, with a mark that is shaped like a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
And then apply a pledget soaked in egg white or in mallow mucilage to the eye.
And then ignite the cautery, of which this is the form.
And then cauterize over the figure you have marked, little by little, in several times, until the surface of the cornea, which is like a myrtle leaf, is entirely burned.
And a sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and its hairs raised above the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days, and then apply a pledget with a poultice until the scar is healed. And then treat that with ointment until it is healed.
But if some matter returns through the hairs near their roots and the eyelid is softened, then perform the cautery again on that, just as you did initially.
But if the hairs are below the eyelid, then cauterize that until it is raised to its natural place and is even, and the hairs do not touch the eye.
But cauterize with a burning medicine; this is that you instruct the patient to pull out the hairs until they are long and even, and then a leaf-shaped compress of cloth is made.
Then take known soap and quicklime and oil, or whatever that is, and then mix those ingredients well, and do this quickly so that it does not get cold; and then spread this on the cloth which is made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place that over one eye or both; and place under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, and the patient's head should be in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it a little and do not move it until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like fire.
And then, when you perceive that he feels the bite, then leave the medicine and wipe it with your finger; then, when the bite ceases, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then consider if you see that the eyelid is already raised; if it is not raised, constrict it with fire or with incision, for then it is well; and if not, return to applying the medicine to the place where the medicine has not yet been applied and it does not become dark until your work is even and the eye is contracted.
Then place cotton with a bandage over it until the scab from the cauterization is removed, and then treat it with healing ointment or with something else until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during your operation, to watch with vigilant diligence that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid in a few days is softened and some hair is sticking in the eye, then repeat your medicine on that part of the eyelid which is softened, and exactly as you did at the beginning, and then bandage it until it is healed.
And this is the form/preparation using myrtle leaves.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to their size (magnitude and smallness), and therefore your constriction [of the eyelid] is done accordingly.
And the path to perfect skill will not be hidden from one who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to place their head on your lap, and have a minister hold their head between your hands in such a way that their head is not moved or shaken.
And then place cotton soaked in egg white or gum arabic on their eye.
And then heat the cautery, whose form is as follows:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a vulture's feather, at the end with which the cauterization is performed.
And if you wish, let it be penetrating to the other end. And if you wish, let it be pointed like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
Then place the cautery, which should be well ignited, on it, and hold your hand a little below the cautery, from the eye to the side of the nose, so that your hand does not burn or the patient is disturbed, and then place the cautery on the granulated area of the eye and burn it away.
And so if at the beginning your cautery penetrates to the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place a bandage over it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if eleven days have passed and it is not healed, then place a sharp corrosive medicine on it until the bone is exposed and is scraped as is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula which some ancients described:  Attend to the location of the fistula and open it, and then place in that fissure a thin tube, which is its shape: And pour into it the quantity of one ounce of molten lead; and hold your hand with the tube with good retention, and do not hurt the patient so that the molten lead does not reach the eye; and you must place on the patient's eye a cloth soaked in egg white or water, because the lead will adhere to the fistula location and heal it with a marvelous cure.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said of the cautery and the treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a puncture of the nose and reduce the fistula to the body of the nose as it will come to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
Of the cauterization of the fissure of the wells (likely referring to abscesses or sores).
Many times fissures occur on the lips, and especially on the lips of children.
Therefore, if you treat those fissures with what we have said in the previous sections and your treatment is not effective, then ignite a small, knife-like cautery according to this form which is shown:
And let its concavity be according to the thinness of a knife.
Then carefully place that iron in those fissures until the cautery reaches the bottom of the fissures.
Then treat it with cerates until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth, an abscess develops, then breaks, becomes chronic, and pus flows and a fistula forms, and you treat it but the treatment is ineffective, then you should apply a cautery according to the size of the fistula. Then place this heated cautery into the fistula opening, holding it with your hand until the heated iron reaches the bottom, and do this once or twice while afterwards applying what we have previously described for the treatment until it heals.
And thus, if the wound is healed and healthy, good; but if not, then it is necessary to open the area and remove the corrupted bone, as will be explained in its chapter.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the cauterization of teeth and the softening of gums.
When the gums are softened by humidity and the teeth are loose, and you treat them with medicines without success, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat the cautery, the shape of which will be described below, so that you place the small cup over the tooth and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, holding your hand briefly until the patient feels the heat of the fire reach the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization several times as needed.
And then the patient should fill their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the loose tooth is fixed, the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt humidity is dried out.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is cold or a worm in it, and you cannot effect a cure with medicine, then cauterize it in one of two ways: either cauterize with a cow's bladder, or cauterize with fire.
Cauterization with a bladder involves taking a cow's bladder and boiling it in a pot of iron. Then take cotton and wrap it around the end of a stick, and then dip it in the boiled bladder, and quickly apply it to the painful tooth until it cools.
And then repeat this several times until the power of the heat reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, submerge wool or cotton in the aforementioned bladder, and place that on the aching tooth, and place ignited iron on top of it until the iron reaches the bottom of the tooth's cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is done by heating a small tube of bronze or iron, ensuring that there is some thickness at its core, so the intensity of the heat doesn't reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery to the desired temperature, and place it on the tooth, holding your hand there until the cautery cools; and do this several times, for the pain will be lessened either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, during the application of this cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good wine and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the form of cauterization.
And cauterize with whatever extremity you wish, as is possible for you.
And this is the form of the cannula:
Chapter 22.
Concerning the cauterization of extrophuli (likely referring to a type of skin eruption or lesion).
When extrophuli are caused by phlegm and cold humors and are not responsive to medicinal digestion, and you wish to digest them gently, then you ignite the cautery, the shape of which is as follows (description of shape to follow, implied by the text). It has two open ends so that the smoke escapes from one end of the cautery, and place that ignited instrument upon that abscess once, or more times if needed, until it reaches the core of the abscess.
And if the abscess is small, then apply the cautery according to the size of the abscess, and then leave it for three days and apply a cloth soaked in boiled water over it until that which the fire has burned is softened.
And then treat it with ointment and bandages until it is healed.
Chapter 23.
On cauterization of hoarseness and constriction of the throat.
When humors accumulate in the lung canal, and especially when this is accompanied by coldness of constitution, then the patient should be evacuated initially with laxative medicines, and then cauterize that area below the root of the throat in the constricted place.
And be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor burn the windpipe, but only a little.
And cauterize that area with another cautery near the junction of the neck at the back of his last vertebra. And his cauterization should be done with a nail, following the previous manner.
And then treat it with what has been prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the cauterization of lung disease and cough.
When cough and lung disease are caused by cold humidity, and the patient has no fever or consumption (tuberculosis) and the disease is chronic, then cauterize him with two cauteries on the clavicle in the depressed areas near the shoulders and armpits; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples; and let the cautery be a nail according to the preceding method.
And if you wish, your cauterization may be done with the cautery called a punctum, the form of which precedes in the puncturing for earache.
And let the points be close to those; and then treat it with the same remedies that precede until it is healed.
And let the cautery have three prongs, for cauterization is done with this: with one application you can make three cauteries according to this form:
Chapter 25.
Of the cauterization of the titillic, which is the head of the penis.
When the head of the penis is dislocated due to lubricous humors and is not fixed at the time of its return from its dislocation until it is accustomed to returning, and then by a small movement it is dislocated again, as we have testified, then first you must reduce the dislocation, and then the patient should be turned onto his back or side; and then raise the skin which is on the inside of the penis to the upper parts with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated on the inside.
And then heat the cautery which has two heads; and this is its form:
And then cauterize the skin until it penetrates to the other side; and the cauterization will then be in the form of four cauterized heads.
And sometimes it is adjusted according to this count, and therefore there are eight cauteries.
And then place over the cautery cooked porridge with salt, and then keep the patient calm, and the limb should not be moved for some time until it is healed.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this is done late, then cauterize over the muscle with a nail-shaped cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is strengthened and the moisture is removed and the patient is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and many humors come into the stomach, exceeding its capacity and multiplying in it, and if you continue the methods of treatment and do not cure it, then the patient should lie on his back, extend his arms and legs, and then cauterize it with three cauteries: one cautery below the feet, the size of a finger, with a nail-shaped cautery, and two cauteries below on either side of the cautery, so that a triangular figure of cauterization is created; and space them so they are not touching, to prevent putrefaction.
And if the depth of the cautery is two-thirds the thickness of the finger.
And let the figure of the cautery be according to this form without addition. And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form; and this should be a cautery according to the form of a circle according to this form:
And let the stomach be cauterized, puncturing it where the cautery is feared: and it is that you mark on the stomach points according to the quantity you wish with a probe, and then cauterize those points with a cautery; and then cure it with the previously mentioned treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
Concerning the cauterization of the cold liver.
When pain arises in the liver due to coldness, or humidity, or excessive flatulence, so that it is expelled from its normal composition by superfluous egress, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it does not help, then the patient should be supine on his back, and mark with a probe three cauteries according to this form and this same quantity on the liver under the hypochondria, where the decubitus of a man is perceived.
And let the space which is between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger, and let the cautery be according to the length of a straight finger.
And do not press too much with the cautery.
And if the quantity of what is burned is half the thickness of a goose quill, then no more should be added; and if the patient is standing on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him be lying down, having already extended his legs, and raising his arms.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these areas with a knife-cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And beware that you do not proceed too much in the cauterization, and that the thickness of a whole goose quill be burned, lest the whole body be burned and it reach the intestines, for the quill here is thin; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the perforation of a liver abscess with a cautery.
When there develops a wound or abscess in the liver and you want to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver or in its membrane, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient finds gravity and pain without acuteness.
And if it is in the membrane of the liver, the pain is very acute, and sometimes it fatigues the doctors in its treatment.
Therefore, the patient should be positioned on his side; and then mark the location of the abscess with a probe; and then ignite the cautery, which is shaped like a rake, the form of which was shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until the entire swelling is burned, until it reaches the pus, until blood comes out.
And then treat it with the treatment for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that this form of cautery be used except by one whose study in the art of medicine is extensive, and in whose hands many illnesses have been treated;  but let someone with some experience sometimes dare this operation, but otherwise leave it to us, and it is better so.
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that cautery with the roots of birthwort for cold pleurisy, according to this manner, is effective, and this is how you do it: you take of the long, dried roots of birthwort a root as long as a finger, and let it be of the thickness of a finger.
Then immerse it in oil and heat it in the fire, and then cauterize with a cautery in the place which is between the continuation of the clavicle and the neck; and with two small cauteries under the chin, slightly inclined towards the part below the beard, and with two cauteries in the area that is declining between the fifth and sixth ribs, inclining slightly towards the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and with another on the stomach, and with three cauteries on the back; and with one cautery in the place which is between the two shoulders, and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary that you grasp your hand with the cautery, but rather let it be held in the evident manner by an assistant.
And some ancients said that some men used a cautery of iron resembling a ray and ignited it, and placed it in the area between the ribs, until it reached the abscess and was safely removed, just as we have said in the case of the abscess of the liver.
But this perforation with a cautery is not without danger, because you might quickly kill the patient, creating a fistula which is incurable.
Chapter 30.
On the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the illness of the spleen with what we have said of the treatment in the division and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is appropriate in three ways, all of which are correct. One is to make three or four cauteries along the length of the spleen, following the shape of the liver cauteries, the form of which has been previously described.
And between each cautery should be the width of a finger or a little more, and the manner of cauterization should be the same as that which we described for the liver cautery.
And do not compress with your hand over the cautery; and the patient should lie on their side.
And another way of cauterizing is to ignite the cautery with two points, the reason for which we have mentioned in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and raise the rib which is in the area of the spleen where the patient's left side lies.
And your elevation, with which you raise the rib, should be along the length of the body, so that cauteries are made along the width of the body.
Then insert the two ignited points quickly into the space so that with them you penetrate the rib from the other side.
And then withdraw the cautery; and thus four cauteries are made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three points.
Then you must care for the cautery until it has stopped discharging pus for many days, for this is more important than the other preceding things in the cure.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the cauterization of a dropsical person.
Cauterization is not used for dropsy, but only for certain specific cases.  (The "3" after "alcinte" is likely a scribal error or annotation and doesn't affect the core meaning.)
When you treat a dropsical person with the methods of treatment that we have mentioned in the division and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize them with four cauteries in a circle around the navel, and with one cauterization above the stomach, and with another cauterization above the liver, and with another cauterization above the spleen, and with two cauteries between the vertebrae of the back, and one cauterization on the right foot, and with another cauterization above the stomach. And the depth of the cauterization should be proportionate to the thickness of the skin.
And then leave the cauterized wounds open so that they drain for a long time.
And do not remove the patient from treatment after cauterization with what is necessary, so that two intentions are added to him, and he will be hastened towards healing with assistance. (This implies that the healing process requires sustained care and multiple approaches beyond the cauterization itself.)
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized should be nail-shaped, as its form is described above; and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, as its form is described above.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are inflamed with dropsy and filled with yellowish water, it is necessary to cauterize the feet on the top of the foot, between the little toe and the ring finger.  Raise your hand with the cautery and do not twist it; then raise your hand again, and do not completely withdraw it, for the yellowish water will drain.
The cautery is according to this form: Then make two cauteries on the two thighs, and on each thigh a cautery should be made with the knife-like end of the cautery. And let the cautery be according to the length of the thigh: one below the knee and the other below the middle of the thigh; and on each thigh two cauteries.
And leave the wound open without treatment for a long time so that some water may drain from it.
And then treat it with those things which we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
On the cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is cold and damp, weakening its functions – that is, the digestive and retentive functions which are in the stomach and intestines – and this is treated with methods of cure and does not improve, and sometimes the patient suffers, exhibiting a need for cauterization, then cauterize it with a large cautery over the stomach as previously described in the cauterization of the stomach, with a circular cautery and four small cauteries in a circle around the navel, with a small nail-like cautery; and with a cautery on the flank above the hip, either one large or two smaller cauteries.
And if sometimes the humors are excessive and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize it over the pubic area and over the intestines, and quickly apply two cauteries over the stomach and near the inguinal regions, for it is a perfect cure, a help from which you will not err.
Chapter 34.
On the cauterization of chronic hemorrhoids.
When there are chronic hemorrhoids in the lower nature, or those of thick cold humors or corrupted moisture, and they are treated with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not improve, then cauterize with three cauteries on the lowest part of the spine, small and slightly triangular, and with a cautery above the navel, slightly to the left of the two aforementioned.
And if you suspect that his stomach is cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face inflamed, then cauterize over the stomach with a large cautery as previously described; and with one cautery over the liver, and with another cautery over his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery, and leave the cautery open for some time, and then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the cauterization of swellings near their excision.
When you excise a swelling, then you immediately apply a cautery that resembles a ray. Then insert that burning cautery into the excised swelling, and lastly use your hand until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood comes.
And do this once or twice, for if the swellings are many, then cauterize each of them as we have recounted, and then treat those with ointments until they are healed.
And if you cauterize with a large cautery on someone, it is slower in being helpful.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. Of the cauterization of fistulas that are formed naturally in the private parts.
When nature does not obey the work which we have recounted in its place, and the patient is frightened by it, let it be healed with cautery.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula in the private parts, and it is old, running with pus and corrupted humidity, and then what is most worthy that is needed is that you probe it with a thin probe, and then know the quantity of its depth with the aforementioned probe.
Then heat the cautery which is similar to a ray. (Meaning a cautery iron shaped like a ray/fish). And then place that heated cautery into this fistula, according to the extent to which the probe entered.
And repeat the cautery on that until those corrupted bodies are burned, once or twice or three times, according to the amount of your necessity.
And beware of burning the nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes to the body of the bladder or to the body of the intestine, then beware of all those places; and do not do this unless the fistula is in a fleshy place and you think it is not penetrating.
And then treat the place until it is healed.
And if the place is sealed, and the materials have been excised from it, and remain so for a long time, then know that it is already completely cured.
And if the materials have not been removed from it, then know that it is penetrating or the bone underneath is corrupted, or this comes from those causes of which mention will be made in their place.
Chapter 37.
Concerning the cauterization of ulcers.
When in ulcers pain arises from cold or from excessive swelling, and because of this the patient's flesh is diminished, then it is necessary that you cauterize that over the ulcers with a cautery, over each ulcer with a nail-shaped cautery, of which mention was previously made.
And quickly cauterize again a little lower, so that three linear cauterizations result, and thus they will be more helpful in healing.
Chapter 38.
Concerning the cauterization of the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder due to cold and humidity, such that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize that in the lowest part above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubic region begin, and cauterize with one cautery on the right side and with another on the left.
And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the walnut; and cauterize that with a cautery in the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries if necessary.
And let the cautery be a nail-shaped instrument, as is appropriate/customary.
Chapter 39.
On the cauterization of the breast.
When cold and humidity come to the breast, and because of this conception is prevented, and her menses are corrupted, and their provocation is delayed, and pain occurs before their arrival, then it is necessary that the place be cauterized with three cauteries in the circuit of the navel, as we have said in the cauterization of the bladder; and with a cautery on the place below the back, or with two cauteries, and let the cautery be nail-shaped.
Chapter 40.
On the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
When mucous humors are poured into the area of the hip, and are the cause of its displacement, and the sign of this is that one thigh is longer than the other when compared. And the location of the dislocation is found where it protrudes.
Then it is necessary that you cauterize the patient on the part of their hip with a circular cautery, after you have marked with a probe the circumference of the area as it is swollen, so that the affected part falls within the middle of the circle.
And let the cautery be of the kind described previously in the cauterization of the stomach.
And if this cautery is not at hand, then cauterize it with three cauteries using a large olive-shaped cautery.
And let the cautery be done according to the quantity and thickness of the whole spine; and then cure it until it is healed.
Chapter 41.
On the cauterization of the sciatic vein.
When pain comes in the hip area, and it is caused by cold and dampness, the patient is cured with what we have said and don't hesitate, and this is ancient practice; then you must purge the patient of thick humors with foul-smelling pills or artificial pills, or similar; and then cauterize it.
And the manner of cauterization in that case is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
Now, according to two known methods, one of which is that you cauterize that area on the patient's hip with three triangular cauteries according to this form; and make them of good depth. And let the space which is between each cautery be the amount of the thickness of a finger, whether the cautery is olive-shaped or large.
But sometimes let the cautery be in the middle of the place, on the head of the hip, so that there are four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with the circle whose form precedes in the cauterization of the hip dislocation, so that it is a continuous circle and burns the thickness of the entire hip.
And this is the method of cauterization in sciatica when the pain is adherent to the hip and is not extended to the thigh.
But if it is extended to the thigh, then cauterize that with two cauteries on the thigh, on the place to which the patient points as the location of the pain; and with one cautery on the hip, four fingers slightly below the left side.
And if the cautery is knife-like, let the depth of the cautery be according to the thickness of the hip.
And if the patient indicates that the pain is extended to the area near the toes, then cauterize there where indicated with a cautery in three or four points, or more if needed.
And if the patient indicates the pain to be below the buttock towards the hip, then cauterize there with a knife-like cautery.
And be careful in all cauterizations that you do not go so deep with the cautery as to burn nerves or large arteries, lest this cause the patient harm, numbness or contraction.
And I have seen one and another of those whom I cauterized on the ankle, and went too deep in the cauterization, and the leg swelled until the tumor reached the foot, and the whole foot was perforated and corrupted. And then a strong diarrhea occurred, and then death.
And if the pain is in two places simultaneously, cauterize each according to this manner.
And the wise men say concerning the cauterization of the ankle, that the cautery should be like a circle of iron whose circumference is half a palm; and its opening should be according to the thickness of a date pit, or a little less.
And within that circle let there be another third; and let the space which is between them be the quantity of a hazel nut; and let those circles be open in two parts; and let the length of those irons be a hazelnut or two, and make in those irons a mark decently prepared in the circles; and this is its form:
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, and then let it be placed on the ankle, the patient lying on their side. Therefore cauterize it with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave it for three days; then plaster it with a bandage and leave the wound open for many days; and then cure it with ointment until it is healed.
Albucasis says: O, son, we have used this type of cautery little, because of the fear of looking at it, and because we have found few who will look at it.
But of the good cauteries to him who suffers them, and finds with that its place.  (This could be improved with more context.  A better translation might be: "But among the good cauteries is one for him who suffers [them], and finds with this its [the ailment's] location.")
But cauterization with burning medicines is that you make two circles having perforated handles in which to place the bolt of the iron door; let the length of the walls in the height of them be two nuts or whatever those are. (This is a difficult sentence due to archaic language and possibly a few corrupt words. The meaning is unclear regarding the specifics of the instrument being described.)
And if the space which is between each is the quantity of the thickness of the finger; and let them be open below and above, and let them contain themselves between them according to this form: (This describes a surgical instrument or technique, but the specifics are hard to determine without a diagram or more context.)
Then place [the instrument] over the part of the hip, the patient lying on his side, and press your hand over the part of the hip; and then take warm water and pour it between the two circles, and the quality of that should be with a little fire little by little, and hold that for the quantity of an hour, and let the patient suffer the biting and burning of it—for that produces a burning as of fire—until the burning ceases.
Then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the warm water from the entire hip with fresh water.
Then leave it for three days and place a bandage over it until the blackness of the burn is removed, and leave it for some days, and pus will drain from it; this is the final treatment.
Then cure that with ointment until it is healed.
And if the pain extends to the hip or leg, make for him a bath that contains water, according to how it was done for the hip equally.
And this is the method of warm water, and I have already written about it in the treatise on the preparation of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, an equal part of each, and crush them and place them in a new pot, the bottom part of which is perforated by a small hole, according to the quantity that enters the ray (presumably a type of instrument or container).
And place under the bottom of the pot another inverted pot, and pour over that alkali and quicklime fresh water which covers them by a finger's breadth, and then when you compress them with your hand with good compression, and let the sharp water descend from the top to the inverted pot below.
And then collect all that water, and pour it over more fresh quicklime and alkali, for then it will be a very strong caustic, which is used in many medical operations and in cauterizing other members, because it works like fire.
And some medicines with which it is also cauterized, such as verdigris and honey mixed with quicklime and soap, so that they are mixed together.
And Galien recounted from an ancient man the operation and cure by which pain in the hip and sciatic vein is cured, and he greatly magnified the excellence of it, and said that it had no need of another cure, and that it is healed once as he recounted, because he quickly entered the bath and is already healed of it.
And it is that some fresh rue should be taken, and if fresh is not found, fresh fig should be taken; and it should be pounded well with a little grease and placed on the hip where the pain is, or on the thigh, or on the leg, and bound on.
And let it remain for the amount of three hours or for as long as the patient feels relief from the burning.
Then put him in a bath, and when his body is reddened, expose him to the sun, for the pain ceases and he is healed.
And know that it is not fitting that anyone use cautery with these medicines except after the evacuation of the body.
But already Dioscorides said that when the varicose vein is cauterized with goat's dung, it profits him very well; and let the cautery be applied in this manner: let wool be taken and soaked in old oil, and let it be placed between the toes of the foot and between the ankles.
Then take dried goat dung and burn it with fire until it becomes charcoal; and then place it on the wool and leave it until it is extinguished. Then repeat this, and do not cease doing this until the sensation of the burning reaches the ankle and relieves the pain.
And this manner of cautery is called cautery of embers.  (or "cautery by embers")
Chapter 42.
Of the cauterization of toothache.
Sometimes pain is in the teeth for various reasons, such as a fall or a blow, or excessive evacuation, and for similar reasons, by infusion of cold and humid matter.
And cautery is only suitable for that one type which is caused by the effusion of cold matter.
Therefore, it is necessary, after evacuation of the patient with suitable pills and similar remedies, that cauterizations be made on the tooth where the pain is, three lines according to its width, provided that you mark with ink, on each line, five cauterizations or more, according to the amount that the patient's tolerance and constitution allow.
And let the cauterization be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries using a nail-shaped cautery, according to this form:
Chapter 43.
On cauterization at the beginning of gibbosity (or humpback).
Often this illness happens to small children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it occurs in their spine with elevation and movement.
And you will find in the last vertebra of the back and neck that it begins to rise above the other vertebrae.
And therefore, when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize that area with a cautery that is circular according to this form: so that the cautery takes in the whole part of the vertebra equally.
And if you wish, cauterize in the circumference of the vertebra with a pointed cautery two or three lines. And let the points be close to each other.
And then treat the area until it is healed with what we have said.
But beware that cautery is not administered in a swelling that is caused by a spasm of the nerves.
Chapter 44.
Concerning the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pain is caused by cold humors which are infused into each member of the body, then when pain comes in the feet, it is the custom of physicians to call this properly gout.
And thus, if gout caused by cold [humors] is treated with a method of cure and the pains are not relieved, then cautery relieves them.
And it is so that you cauterize that [area] near the evacuation in the joint's circumference of the foot with many cauteries.
And let it be an olive-shaped cautery, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary that a puncture be made on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if the pains ascend to the knees or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize each knee with three or four cauteries on each side with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if many cauteries are necessary, then cauterize [the area] and do not press too hard with the cautery, but only around the thickness of a finger.
And if the pains rise to both knees, then administer the cautery which we have described in its chapter.
And if the pains are only in the hands, and it is called  *suagra* (likely a type of hand ailment), then puncture in the circumference of the knuckles, as if revolving, puncture in those two lines.
And if the pain remains in the fingers, then puncture in the circumference of the knuckles on each finger and on the palm of the hand.
And if the pains reach the elbows or muscles in a few days, then cauterize each one from all parts of the joints.
And do not neglect the patient's good regimen and reception of medicines, for if a good regimen is followed and the inflammation is evacuated with this cautery, he will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
On the cauterization of fractures.
When a fracture occurs in the knees and the intestines protrude before the patella to the genitalia, and this is beginning to happen, then you should order the patient to cease eating that day and to use emollient remedies so that the rupture descends from his belly.
Then support the patient between your hands on his back, and command him to hold his breath until the patella or intestines are expelled or appear.
Then return that with your fingers and gently manipulate the rupture over the pubic bone, noting that its extremity should resemble a semicircle in the upper parts of the body.
Then heat the cautery, of which this is the form:
until it is very red and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and place your hand over the area so that the intestine does not protrude.
You have already opened the space between the patient's thighs and placed a cushion underneath; and another servant should be at their legs, and another at their feet, holding their hands.
Then place the cautery on that mark with your hand, holding the cautery straight, and hold it there until you reach the bone of the hip through the middle of the swelling; and repeat this another time if you did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And carefully observe the position of the intestine during your cauterization, so that you do not burn the intestine, lest the patient suffer death or other great harm.
And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your operation is not complete.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be small, according to their size and measure.
Then care for the cauterized area for three days with a bandage until the burn scab has healed.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area and wound are sealed/closed.
And it is necessary that you give him nourishment according to the length of his treatment, which will cleanse his belly, and that the intestine does not protrude outwards during the effort of healing.
And then, when he wants to get up after about forty days, apply a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another forty days.
And let him be moderate in work, in the fullness of food and drink, and in vehement speech, for when he uses this regime of healing entirely, he will be healed.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the abdomen; and if, at the beginning, you want them to not protrude, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size.  Let what is excised from the hernia be about a third of it; and then treat it with what we have said, and it is appropriate.
And some earlier authors said that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, according to this shape, as it is marked with a probe:
and a line is placed across the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and place your hand with a nail-shaped cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
On the cauterization of torsion.
When pain develops in some member due to a fall or blow, and that pain persists for a long time and does not yield to treatment with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cauterization of each member be according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the torsion and its pain.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cauterization, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization; for it is customary for these pains to be punctuated from one member to the one adjacent to it, so it is necessary to treat them with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers are sometimes cauterized with great benefit, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so, if you wish to cauterize it, consider if the leprosy is spreading and if it can be cured with what we have said in the division and it does not spread or persist; and if you fear that the corruption will affect the entire body and its constitution, then cauterize it on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and the second below it, towards the forehead at the hairline; and two above the ears, like horns; and one behind, on the nape of the neck.
And finally, with the cautery, press your hand slightly so that it leaves a small impression on the bone, and the scabs are loosened, so that the expulsion of thick vapors is eased; and cauterize over the spleen as needed.
And if the leprosy is already spread over the patient and manifestly apparent, then you should cauterize it with the cauteries we mentioned on the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheekbones, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with one large one on the vertebrae near the elbow, and one on the elbow, and with two on the hips, and one on each hip, and with two on the buttocks and one on each buttock, and with two on the knees, and two on the muscles, and two on the ankles of his feet; and cauterize it on all the joints of the fingers of the hands and feet, and on each heel of his feet, and on the knuckles of his hands.
And be careful of the nerves that are on the heel, so as not to burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone, and another on the stomach opening, and another on the liver.
And know that the more precisely you apply the cauteries, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as they do from wounds, because his body is already numbed/stupefied.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as is prescribed by the types/kinds of instruments.
Then treat the cautery with cooked barley flour mixed with honey and with other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Concerning the cauterization of stupor (or numbness).
When a limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size or smallness of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate the thickness of the skin a little. And afterwards, treat that with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes a numbness is cauterized which extends to the hands and feet, and to the vertebrae of the back, near the origin of the nerves which move that limb, and the numbness is relieved.
But do not presume upon this except one who is skilled in the anatomy of the limbs and in the origin of the nerves moving the whole body.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the cauterization of albaras, which is morphea.
When albaras is chronic and you apply no ointments of medicine to it, then you cauterize it with a cautery iron a little, according to the size of the affected area, until the whiteness and its color are relieved, and its color is changed.
Then treat it with flour of barley and lentils with rose oil and with leaves of arnagrosa (rosemary?), and with pigeon's blood or with lamb's blood; each should be a part and all mixed together; then a cloth should be applied and placed on the area until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you wish to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery iron around its circumference as it is swollen.
And the wise men say that it should be cauterized with the pointed cautery in the middle; but to me this is not advisable, because I fear that it will be ulcerated, and I have seen this happen several times.
And it is more appropriate that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circle, as we have said, or with several cauteries.
Chapter 50.  (Assuming a standard chapter numbering system.  Roman numerals were common in medieval texts.)
Concerning the cauterization of swellings/tumors.  (The word "adubilati" seems to refer to a type of swelling or tumor. The exact translation may depend on the specific medical context.)
When a swelling is slow to mature, and this is due to the excess causing it, or to the age of the patient, for he is old and decrepit, with little blood, or because of the time of year, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the swelling; then cauterize around its circumference with several small, pointed cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.
But if you wish to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place it in the middle of the swelling until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in that place which is lower down, so that the body of the arm may be relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the cauterization of a corrosive [wound/ulcer].
A corrosive is a corruption moving in the limb, which corrodes it like fire consuming dry wood.
Therefore, if you see this corrosive in a place that permits cauterization with fire, then you immediately cauterize with many small nails, or large ones, as is appropriate for the place where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it completely until you eradicate all the corrosion and no cause of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and apply crushed sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scabs are gone and all the corruption is eradicated.
