And then treat it with flesh-generating ointments.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh has grown where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted areas.
And corrosion is sometimes treated with a sharp medicine, for that serves in place of cautery. But however, cautery with fire is of greater benefit.
And I have already described the treatment with sharp medicine in the section, and therefore take that when you need it.
Chapter 53.
On the cauterization of wounds and other similar things.
Many times, a disease called a wound occurs in the parts under the foot, and it is a large, penetrating wound that injures the foot; and cautery in these cases is done in two ways, that is cautery with fire and cautery with hot water.
But cautery with fire is that you heat the concave cautery resembling a vulture's beak, which should be made of iron according to the size that encompasses the entire wound, and it should be of fine edges.
And then place that heated iron on the wound, and then move your hand with the cautery in a circle around the wound until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And leave it there for three days until it begins to shed pus. And then plaster it with crushed wild mallows mixed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it is not healed, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you should apply cautery with water to those areas.  Take a cannula of bronze or iron or a vulture's quill, and place it over the wound; then pour a small quantity of hot water into the cannula, and hold your hand, and you should move it with compression of your hand all around, and press the edge of the cannula into the root of the wound, and the hot water will find its way to the root of the sore; and let the patient endure the sting of the hot water for a while, about an hour, and then remove it, for the wound with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this with those and then the others until you have treated all those that are in the body.
And then treat those, once the roots of the sores are eradicated, with those ointments which generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
On the cauterization of tremor.
When someone suffers from tremor due to cold in the nerves, or from a fever of cold origin, or from a similar cause, then you should cauterize them with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, one cautery between each vertebra; and with a cautery on their feet, and on their stomach, using an olive-shaped cautery.
And their tremor will cease, and hasten the healing of the cold illness.
Chapter 55.
On the cauterization of accidental blemishes on the body.
When blemishes appear on the body, and there are pustules and fissures caused by thick, corrupt matter; then, at the beginning, as they appear, it is necessary that it be cauterized over the head of the fistula with a fine cautery, with myrtle wood whose end is ignited with fire; or with a long root of elecampane; or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes the cautery is also suitable at the beginning of their expulsion, according to this method; and they are not believed, and the superfluity is disrupted by making those, and heals from those. (This line is difficult to translate precisely due to the archaic language and possibly some corruption in the original text. The meaning seems to suggest that sometimes cauterization is used early in the process of removing excess fluids or matter, and that it disrupts the process of their formation, ultimately leading to healing).
However, it is necessary that this be done near evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter 56.
Chapter 56. On the cauterization of bleeding accidents caused by arrow wounds.
Often, there is bleeding from an arrow wound that is incised outside the wound, or from the incision of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similarly, and it is difficult to stop.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then press with your hand on the orifice of the arrow wound, and place your finger on it, pressing it many times until the bleeding stops under your finger, and it does not come out.
Then put small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire, many in number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
Then take one of these, small or large depending on the location and wound where the artery is broken,  applying it to that vein quickly once you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery there until the bleeding is stopped and restrained.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and withdraw the cautery, then take another cautery carefully from among those which are prepared in the fire, and do not cease doing this one after another until the bleeding is restrained.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that no other harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to stop it in those cases, especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or with extraction if it is not extracted, for when it is extracted, the extremities are best cauterized and the bleeding is stopped, or that it be tied with thread or a ligature by strong constriction, or that medicines whose virtue is to stop bleeding be applied to it; and they should be tightened with a cushion with decent constriction.
But that which administers decent restriction with ligature and stricture with cloths or by the application of cauterizing substances and the like, does not bring help except late.
But if this happens to someone and the physician or medicine is not present, then gently press with the index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and apply great pressure to it until the blood is stopped from the main part of the wound, and the finger should not be removed from the artery; and over the artery, cold water of great coldness should be sprinkled until the blood is congealed and thickened; and then consider what need there is of cautery or medicine.
Of incisions and perforations and cupping and of phlebotomy and of wounds and their like.
We have already mentioned in the first chapter every illness in which cautery with fire is suitable and with burning medicine, and its occasions and causes, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the top of the head to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the one seeking this will be relieved, then regarding what he will have requested.
And before I begin this, it is necessary, son, that you learn that in this chapter is what is terrifying in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because it is necessary that there be great caution in it, for in the operation which is in this chapter, many times there is evacuation which is with blood with which life remains, through the opening of a vein or incision on an abscess or opening of a wound or purification of a wound or extraction of an arrow or incision of a stone and the like. And with all of these is accompanied fear and trembling, and in the hands of those who do them, death.
And I warn you that this, in which there is hesitation, should not fall upon you, for sometimes various kinds of men fall to you with many kinds of illnesses. And some of them are angry with their illness and death is light to them, and this is because of the vehemence of what they find in their illness, and because of the length of their illness, and with the illness is a fear which signifies death.
And among them is one who gives you his wealth and says, you hoping for his health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to that which comes to you from which is this manner; and your caution should be greater than covetousness and your greed; and do not presume anything about this except through certain knowledge which verifies this for you, and to which a praiseworthy outcome follows.
And use in the cure of all your patients prognostication and indication of him to whom health is restored, for in this is to you help in acquiring fame and remembrance, praise and glory.
O, sons, may God inspire in you His understanding and not withhold from you guidance and protection, for this is in His hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that this which you ask for and of which you have need may be relieved for you.
Chapter one.
Of the treatment of the water which is contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often happens to children during childbirth and when the midwife squeezes the child's head without skill.
And sometimes it happens for a hidden cause which is unknown.
But I have only seen this illness in children; and all those whom I saw died, and thus it is to me a work abandoned in them. (Or: ...and thus this is seen by me to be a hopeless case in them)
And I have seen that a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was always thus enlarged until he could not support himself because of the size of his head, and moreover the humidity increased until he died.
And this humidity is either collected between the skull and the bone, or it is collected under the bone on the brain.
Because the operation in that case, if the humidity is between the skull and the bone and the abscess is small, then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with a fissure in width, and the length of the fissure should be about two finger-widths so that the humidity can run out, and this is the shape of the spatula:
And therefore if the humidity is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two fissures in this manner:
And if the humidity is under the bone, the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head opened on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you compress it with your hand on the inside parts and this is not hidden from you. It is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with three fissures in this manner:
And then extract all the humidity from the incision; and then tighten the fissures with cloths and lint; and then moisten it with wine and oil until the fifth day, and then untie the bandage and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and bind the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry food with little humidity until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of water appears, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore aim with the incision where the abscess and the collection of water appear, and do it as best you can.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood, and the patient dies from that blood flow with evacuation of humidity.
Chapter Second.  (or Chapter 2)
When a catarrh or rheum occurs in the eyes or nose, and it is chronic, and the cure is not achieved with medicines, then the last resort is incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you wish to incise them, the patient should raise his head, and you should rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear; and the patient's neck should be constricted with a cloth; and observe where the vein pulses; and the location of each, near the ears, is compressed, and thus they are hidden except in some men.
And then mark the artery with a probe, and incise each one with a sharp lancet, making the incision reach the bone, and this should be across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and sever it at the incisions above.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers' breadth, for when a vein is incised, the blood flows out with a pulsatile flow, leaping out frequently.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then you must measure from the ear a space of three fingers' breadth, and then mark it with a probe, and make the incision to the bone.
And the amount of blood that should be let is about six ounces, and quickly let more or less, and all this depends on the strength of the patient and the fullness of his artery.
And then examine the wound, and if any matter of the splinter remains on the bone, then incise it so that a hot abscess does not develop.
Then bind the wound with lint and linen bandages, and treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from chronic migraine or sharp headaches, due to acrid humors and a certain quality in the tissues of the temples, or from an old, violent headache similar to these, and it is treated with medical methods of cure and this does not help, then we must attempt in these illnesses the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or their cauterization, as we have shown.
And the manner of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hairs which are on the temples; and attends to the artery apparent in the temple, for it is shown to you by its pulse, and is hidden only in few men by the intensity of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then let the neck of the patient be constricted with some cloth; and then rub the place with a rough cloth or evaporate the place with hot water until the artery appears to you manifestly. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And then you scrape the skin with that [spatula] easily until you reach the artery; and then put in an ointment and extract it to the superior parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics which are beneath it, from every part.
But if the artery is subtle, then wrap it with the end of the oncii [a surgical instrument], and then incise a part of it according to the quantity by which its ends are elongated and contracted, and there will be no flow of blood, for if they are not incised, the blood will not flow at all.
And then evacuate from one to three ounces of blood.
And if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with strong double silk thread or with cords at length, and do not let putrefaction occur before the consolidation of the wound and the occurrence of blood flow.
And then incise the superfluity which is between the two ligatures; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone with a knife cautery until its ends are incised, and it will be in place of that same work.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or a hot complexion; for cautery is one of those things that helps drying humors, and therefore it is better in youth.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that you fill the place with old linen, and that decent powders are placed on it, and then it should be cured with dry medicines that cause the flesh to grow and with bandages until it is healed.
And if, while you operate another time, a flow of blood from the artery occurs, then you must take care to restrain that blood, either with cautery, or by filling the place with linen, and squeeze your hand until the bleeding is stopped. And if none of those things is at your disposal, then place your finger on the area until the blood is congealed, and apply water of extreme coldness to the area until the sharpness subsides, and it is constricted as necessary.
And what is easiest in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and place each one, very hot, until they reach the bone and incise the vein in two places, so that what is between the extremities is elongated, because they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two knives be equal. But however, if it has less sharpness than two knives, it is better, because if it is as sharp as two knives close to each other, it will not incise the flesh with speed.
Therefore, since in those there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is held in them and the flesh is cut lightly.
And this method is better in every respect and gentler.
And let the space which is between the two knives be the quantity of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter four.
Chapter Four: On the treatment of sharp and acidic tears from the eyes.
When there are acidic tears, and it is the flow from those veins which are on the head, and you find no cause for it in the medication, and sometimes the patient's face reddens and they feel in their forehead a crawling sensation as if it were ants crawling, and their eyes are swollen, moist, so that the hairs around them are wet, and the eyelids are inflamed by the sharpness of the tears, the ancients used various methods in the treatment of this condition, and they called this treatment "spassa".
And it is that you command the patient to shave the hairs which are on their forehead, and then incise three fissures in the forehead, equally distant according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the fissure reaches two fingers: one fissure should be equally distant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little from the movement of the muscles which are in the lacrimal duct, and the third on the other side.
And beware of the incision of the arteries which are on both sides, and keep your hand away from the continuation of both jaws. And if the space of each incision is the amount of three fingers adjusted/joined together.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which to dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of its incision: which is from the part of the temples to the second incision. Excoriate with that [spatula] the whole ridge which is between the two incisions, with the chisel which is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then remove that spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a curette, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that [curette] against the flesh attached to the ridge, and its blunt edge against the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision be continued to that appearance of the ridge, and then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And after a quantity of blood has flowed, press the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a piece of old linen, and place on top of that powders soaked in wine and oil and vinegar, and see that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day untie the bandage and administer a large infusion of warm or tepid water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5: On the treatment of tears caused by cataracts and those descending into the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When someone experiences sharp and persistent cataracts, and sometimes the patient's eyes appear small and shrunken, and their vision is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their hair falls out, and there is sharp, throbbing pain in the forehead and subsequent sneezing, then know from these symptoms that the cataracts and matter originate from many veins proceeding from the inner parts [of the head].
And the best cure in such cases is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then make an incision there in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be to the bone; and extend your hand from the temples' lacerations which are bruised because of chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then fill the space between the two lips of the incision with old cotton or linen cloths. And then bind it above with powders soaked in wine and oil so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And when you unbind it and see that the abscess has diminished, then you must scrape the bone until flesh begins to grow there, and then treat it with a drying regimen which will cause the flesh to grow, thus: if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them, and make a plaster from them, and apply it to these wounds to encourage the growth of flesh.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the treatment of what falls into the ears.
Every cause that falls into the ears is one of four types, for it is either a mineral stone or something similar to a stone, such as iron or glass, or it is from a plant, such as a grain of wheat or a bean, or it is a flowing substance such as water or sour wine and things similar to them, or some animal.
And therefore, when a stone falls into the ear, or another cause similar to those things which do not soften or grow in the ear, and you expose the ear to the sun, and if you see the stone, then instill into it some violet oil or sesame oil, and then attempt its extraction, and cause sneezing with a potion, and block the nostrils before the onset of the sneezing. And then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and pull the ear upwards, for many times it is expelled with these methods.
And if it is not expelled, then attempt its extraction with the instrument called a fine surgical probe, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the probe, good; and if not, then attempt its extraction with a thin hook of little curvature.
And so, if it is not expelled by this, then make a copper cannula and insert the end of the cannula into the opening of the ear and seal around the cannula with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open but the cannula. And then extract it with your breath with strong suction, for many times it is done with what we have recounted.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a small amount of lit tinder or something similar prepared for catching birds, and place that on the end of a probe, and then carefully wrap a suitable cotton around that. And then insert that into the opening of the ear easily, and then dry the ear of its moisture.
And so, if it is not expelled by all the means which we have recounted, then you should consider cutting it before it becomes a hot abscess or spasm.
And the manner of cutting it is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to the patient's strength, and then hold the ear between your hands and turn the ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear above the gray matter, in a well-prepared location. And let the incision be of a lunar shape until the stone is reached, and, the stone found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then close the incision immediately, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause falling into the ear is one of the growths that grows and is inflamed, then administer its treatment and extraction; but if it does not obey you in this way, then take finely powdered litharge according to this method: and administer with that the removal of that growth which has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, and place it on small pieces of cloth, and then remove them with tweezers or with a fine instrument, or with an incision as we have said, for its extraction is thus relieved.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing at the beginning, and fill their ear with some old cotton, and lie down on the ear into which the water has entered; and if it is expelled by this, it is good; and if not, then take some heated pebbles and lentils according to the length of the finger, and warm them a little in the fire, and the patient inserts one of these into the opening of their ear and jumps on one foot on that side and taps with another stone on the stone which is inside the ear. Therefore do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And if the water is not expelled in this way, take some paper or tow, and put one end into the ear and hold the other end to a fire until the middle of it is burnt.
Then put other tow there and do this as many times as necessary until all the water is expelled, and remove it with a cannula, as mentioned above for the stone extraction.
But in the extraction of a creature entering that ear, if it is small, like a fly or similar, treat it as we have said in the division; but if it is large, which is apparent to the senses, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks, and its cause is the easiest of all the other causes which fall into the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your treatment is not effective, then you must expose the ear to the sun.
And then, if any cause of the worms appears, extract it with forceps or with fine hooks; but if no cause appears, then take a cannula that is narrow below and wide above, of which this is the form: And insert the narrow end into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and then suck with it strongly, and do this many times until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not obey you in this way, then you should fill and close the opening of the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already proven in the treatment of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your effusion, with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, be with this instrument, of which this is the form: And make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and wide above.
And so let it be such that it pushes what is in the cavity of the cannula to the place where it will be necessary. And if you wish, take a reed and wrap its end decently with cotton, and then pour oil into the cavity of the ear, or juice of some herb, or whatever you wish of these medicines, into the cannula—and that is in the ear—and then insert the reed with cotton on it and press with your hand, with a moderate compression, until the oil is expelled into the cavity of the ear and the patient feels the oil inside.
And what is poured into the ear should be slightly warmed by fire. And be careful that what you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the cure of ear obstruction.
Sometimes children are born without perforated ears, and this also happens in some adults: obstruction in the ear canal due to an injury or fleshy growth within it.  This obstruction sometimes occurs in the front of the ear opening, which prevents hearing, and sometimes in the outer parts.
And that which occurs at the opening, preventing hearing, is among the most difficult to heal.
And in the case where hearing is possible, you should expose the patient's ear to the sun and examine it. And then, if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a fine spatula, of which this is the form:
If its end is narrow, with one side sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be two fingers wide so that it does not injure the ear.
And then, if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or its meatus, then remove that with fine hooks and incise it vigorously until all the flesh is removed.
And if the obstruction is in the entrance of the ear, then take a thin probe and warm it slightly with fire, and carefully insert it into the ear canal.
And then, if you have felt an obstruction with which the patient experiences difficulty, due to the severity of hearing, ease its pressure gently; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a linen cloth, according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for a few days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or take some wool and dip it in rosewater, and powder it with finely ground [herb/substance], and use it; and if you see the ear is already suppurating, then you must remove the wool and replace it with other wool that you will wrap in a plaster made with rose oil, until the abscess has subsided; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use the other treatment which restricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which develops on the eyelids.
Styes which develop on the eyelids are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold them with forceps or with an awl, and incise them with a spatula at their roots, and afterwards place a poultice on the affected areas.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then cauterize them with a thin, lenticular cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains at their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often there develops in the eyelids of the eyes a certain condition which resembles swelling in its strength and hardness, and because of this swelling it is called [a swelling/tumor].
And it is an accumulation of thick moisture in the eyelid, below and above.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you should consider then if the swelling proceeds in such a way that it appears from the eyelid and is spread to every part; and its cause is light.
Then make an incision on that [swelling] lengthwise, and then peel it away from every part easily until it is removed, and then lift it with forceps and excise it.
And if the wound is large, then you close it with sutures; and care for it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it because its ointment restores it and causes it to heal.
And if the growth is leaning towards the inner parts of the eyelid beneath the inner surface, then lift the eyelid and lift the growth with forceps, even if you need to make an incision, and press it from all sides, and if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, this harms nothing.
Then, after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with a healing agent until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a growth on the eyelid above the eye.
A growth is a grey mass which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and mostly in children, it obscures their eyes and causes them much pain; and they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because of this, for tears flow from their eyes.
And therefore you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and the eyelids below their eyebrows are moist, which already shows swelling, and the appearance of a growth is imminent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the growth beneath.
And a certain method of operation is this: the patient rests their head on your lap, then you place a cloth between you and them, and make a circle of it according to the size which contains the growth from all sides, and then place that over it and press with your fingers so that the growth is gathered in the middle of the circle, and then make a cut in the middle of that swelling with a sharp instrument, the incision being of a width no greater than that made in bloodletting.
But in the incision it is necessary that you cut completely until you reach the growth, for in most cases the growth proceeds by fissure according to the form of a white grease.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped around your fingers at the outer edges and roll it between your right and left hand until it is removed.
And be careful not to push into the incision so that with the instrument you do not reach the eye and injure it.
But if the growth does not appear at the beginning of the incision, then push a little gently into the incision until the growth appears; and then extract it as we have said; and then soak cloths in wine and vinegar and place them on the area and bind them with powders.
And some crush salt and place it in the cavity of the incision so that it draws out the remaining moisture.
And then care for it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the site, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
On the various ways of treating the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes, pustules form under the natural hairs and are hardened, then these harm the eye and cause various illnesses, such as constant tears and softening of the eyelids and membrane, until this causes destruction of the eye, which is called a disease of the eye. It is treated in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with a sharp medicine as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery; or it is treated by incision and suture; or by canulas, as I shall describe.
Therefore, you should place the patient's head on your lap; then, turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it is turned, it is good; and if not, insert a needle into that part where there is pus under the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the pus upwards, and this should be—near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow—near the hairs, and pull the pus upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with a ray. (The "ray" here likely refers to a tool or instrument, possibly a retractor).
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid under the hardened pustules with a sharp spatula from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct; and then extract the pus and place small pieces of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and then bind it over the eyelid with a bandage, and it should be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape should be according to the amount of swelling of the eyelid, for this varies in people, for some require that a greater quantity of the eyelid be incised according to the extent to which the eyelid is softened.
Then, using the spatula, make an incision along the two lines that you have marked, beginning from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct. And if your incision is near the natural hairs, let it be of the thickness of a king's [presumably a reference to a specific measurement or a thick needle/tool].
Then place the hook in one of the two corners of the carbuncle, and then completely excise it, and then join the two lips with glue or fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and adhere any excess thread to the two eyebrows with some viscous substance, if you wish to do so, and if not, do not worry.
Then leave the suture of the threads for three or four days, and then treat it [the wound].
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised by the sealing of the wound and its aggregation; but suture is better.  Therefore, the ancients advised this.
However, let there be diligence on the part of the surgeon, and it is a good operation, and there is no fear in it [for the surgeon].
And there is another way to treat [this], and that is to mark on the eyelid a figure like a myrtle leaf, and then raise the eyelid with three separated and collected hooks according to this form:
But if this is not possible, lacking the hooks—and this is not unusual—then take a needle on which there is thread and place it in the middle of the figure, and insert the other thread near the larger tear duct, and the second thread near the smaller tear duct, and adjust the threads between your fingers with equality, and then with those [threads], lifting your hand with equal elevation, raise the entire fold on which it [the myrtle leaf figure] is marked as we have described.
And then join the two lips of the wound with a suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with a plaster and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are inverted; then it is necessary that you use on that what we have said about incision; but suture in each case is more skillful [or "artificial"].
To cauterize with canes is according to this form: and it is that you turn back the eyelid and incise the growth that is inside as I have recounted; and then skillfully make two canes or two thin splinters whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of the space [i.e., the area to be cauterized]. And you have already incised at the ends of each part, where you have the threads [or sutures].
Then carefully add each [cane] with subtlety over what is superfluous of the eyelid skin, and bind the canes to each part with a strong ligature, and leave it for some days, for the growth, due to the ligature, will die and dry up and will rot until it falls off by itself.
But if it is delayed, then break it with forceps, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Then, once it is solidified, the eyelid is elevated and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of the two canes which you will make according to this form: and have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Chapter 12.  Of the elevation of prickly hairs in the eye with a needle when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough, and prickly hairs in the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine needle, and place on it a fine silk thread, and then adjust the ends carefully,  and knot them together in a very subtle manner. And let the length of the threads be the quantity of a palm's length.
And then you prepare two other fine threads underneath it briefly, and tie their ends if you wish.
And then have the patient place their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that your work is illuminated, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the offending hair, which needs to be removed, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract it from above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair upwards and insert it into the alixati (likely a type of tweezers or forceps), and let there be one or two or three hairs, and no more.
And then pull your hand with that until the hair is extracted with the alixolati (forceps) from the eyelid along with the natural hair.
But if it is pulled with the alixalati and the hair is not removed, you will pull the alixalati downwards with the thread which you have prepared with it, until the alixalati is removed from the upper wound.
And examine it with the vision of the eye, for then you will remove the alixati and the thread, and clean each one and close the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and it is raised with that, or you leave it until it is lengthened in some space, and then you raise it.
Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of ehacratir which appears on the upper eyelid.
When ehacratir appears in the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is contracted upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound or incision or cautery, and it is called harelip, and this ehacrati is either natural or accidental, and is caused by the healing of a wound or incision or cautery, or by similar poorly healed conditions.
The manner of the operation is that you should incise that consolidation and separate its lips, and place between them a small piece of linen cloth, and bind them until they are healed.
And it is not fitting that you use treatments that dry or constrict; for if you do this, the ehacratir will return worse than it ever was before.
Use on it things that soften, such as fenugreek, and apply a poultice with the water in which mallow and linseed have been boiled, or diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some oil, and dip the linen cloth in it, and treat with that.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you should prevent, by every means, consolidation from occurring in a way different from its original form.
And this operation which we have described is nothing but a rectification of the ehalixacrati (likely a misspelling or archaic term, possibly referring to a wound or injury) so that it returns to its former shape.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the treatment of an ehacrati (likely a misspelling or archaic term, possibly referring to a wound or injury) that occurs in the lower eyelid.
This injury (alxacrati, likely a misspelling or archaic term for a wound or lesion) which occurs in the lower eyelid, which is truly called an alxacrati, is sometimes natural, other times accidental.
But accidental ones are caused by a blow, or by a cut, or by a cautery, or by other similar things.
And the method of its operation is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left lacrimal duct to the right lacrimal duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh (of the eyelid).
Then stretch the flesh of the eyelids together with the needle and sew it with the needle, and incise it with a wide spatula (or possibly a broad, flat instrument). And then if the eyelid returns to its proper shape, it is good; and if not, take a splint and place it in the split area, and with it, reshape the eyelid, and close the two fissures in the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two fissures (incisions) be two angles of the incision which you have made so that they meet, and let an angle be formed by them so that when they come together a figure is formed from them.
And this figure is the Greek letter: lambda (Λ).
Then remove the flesh from that area, the amount that is the width of the sharp lower eyelid, so that it may be the eyelid. (This refers to trimming excess flesh to properly shape the eyelid.)
Then join the separated parts and sew them together with wool thread; and then treat them with what we have said of emollient medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if it is raised (i.e., the eyelid is swollen or inflamed) because of a fissure, suture, or cautery, then it is necessary that you make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid according to what has been described previously. Then separate [the wound/lips] with a poultice between two lips, as we have said.
In summary of the discourse on the treatment of an abscess, whether above or below, let the operation proceed as prepared in the manner of an abscess, and this is in the form of various kinds.  (This refers to the various forms or types of abscesses and their treatments.)
And the skilled artisan always prepares instruments in every possible way to restore the figure to its natural form, or near to it, and [adapts the treatment] to the patient according to the operation and instruments that are most suitable.
Chapter 15.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation occurs in many men, such that the eyelid is solidified above with the white of the eye and is prevented from its easy movement and natural functions, then it is necessary that you insert the tip of the instrument under the eyelid and lift it up, or spread it with an unguent (ointment).
And then you incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharply pointed, but rather somewhat blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and move the spatula so that it does not injure the eye with its sharpness.
And let your incision be such that it separates that adhesion easily, until the eyelid is returned to its natural form and the entire adhesion is healed.
And then you put salt water in the eye with which you wash it; or distill into it honey dissolved in water.
And then separate the eyelid from the eye with a small, thin instrument, and place on the eye wool infused in egg white, and then on the third day use consolidative remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the incision of a nail and the fleshy eminence of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two types, for it is either nervous, and resembles hard, fine horn; or it is non-nervous, resembling a congealed white humidity, which when an iron touches it, or it is forced to be removed with an instrument, is cut off and the instrument is not inserted into it.
And both types do not begin except from the major tear duct until it grows little by little, covering the sight and preventing light and movement of the eye.
But the manner of operating in its incision is that the patient places his head in your lap, and then you open his eyes, and raise the eyelid with your hand, and then you grasp the nail with an instrument of little thickness and extend it to the upper parts. And then take a needle and insert into it a horsehair, or ox hair, or cow hair, or similar, or strong thread, or double the end of the needle little by little and insert it into the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it to the upper parts, and scrape with the hair the edge of the nail which covers the pupil. And thus you draw it with the hair into the back of it.
And then you incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula or with small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct so that by the incision of it there is no continuous flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail, with the instrument alone extended or with thread, as we have said, and then it is scraped with a lion spatula, of which this is its form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and that a rupture occurs in it, and that the pupil is perforated.
Then, into the wound, put a little crushed salt or a poultice made of hellebore, and leave it there until the next day; and then treat it with what is necessary until it heals.
And if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert the needle, nor is it fixed with the instrument, then many times this type of nail is cured with medicines, and is healed as I have recounted in the division.
And if you wish to incise it, then you open the wound of the patient and take a thin lion spatula of polished steel, according to this form: with subtlety.  (or: delicately; skillfully) And if you see that it is rarefied and has gathered and rooted itself there, then distill from your hour into the wound a poultice made of hellebore or with rue or with crushed salt, and plug the wound until the next day.
Then repeat the operation on it until all that is there is removed, unless on the contrary a hot abscess forms in the wound, for then you leave that, and treat the abscess initially until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it reasonably until it is healed.
The eminence of the lachrymal flesh, if it is noticeable in the wound as a great hindrance, should be suspended with hooks; and incise it in part, and do not extend the incision so that you do not cut the flow of tears.
Then distill into the lachrymal duct a poultice of rue and hellebore until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the incision of a fistula and that which is born of flesh adhering to the wound.
Sometimes in the eyes of some, red flesh is born and grows until it covers the pupil, or approaches or presses upon the eyelids, strongly constricting the eyelids at the edges and resembling a balustrade rose (a type of fleshy growth).
But the operation is that the patient rests his head on your lap; and then open the eyes of that one and gather all that flesh with appropriate forceps for this, and hold it with forceps or a pair of tweezers; and then incise the flesh at the beginning until all is finished with the lancet, and be careful of the eye that you do not injure it during your operation.
And let your incision, with which you incise those things, be with a spatula, which we have recommended in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which hair is collected, as the form of it will suggest in this chapter.
Then, when your incision is complete and all that flesh is removed, fill the eye with crushed salt, or instill into it a red powder similar to that of corrosive medicines, and place a bandage over the outside of the eye with egg white until it is secured from a hot abscess.
And if some cause of inflammation remains and hot blood is secreted, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and treat that abscess with whatever reduces that abscess, and then repeat the operation on it until it is healed, and similarly do in the case of fleshy growths which occur in the eye of this type.
But however, you must avoid any treatment of the eye by natural or accidental means, working with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that that member can tolerate that operation.
Chapter 18.
Concerning the collection of pus in the eye.
Pus is a thick, red vein which forms on the eye and hinders its function, and weakens the eye over many days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which there is pus is strong and there is no other illness besides the pus; then you may collect that pus.
And it is that you command the patient to place his head in your lap, and then suspend those veins with an onch or two, according to your judgment; and let that onch be of subtle doubling according to this form:
or two onches gradually joined in one piece according to this form:
And then let those veins be collected with fine forceps, with subtlety, and wipe away the blood little by little, until you see those veins being removed from the eye and are retracted.
And be careful that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the spatula; and if your operation is in the midday before the sun, and you are very firm in your operation that you incise only those veins.
And when you have finished, then you distill into the eye verdigris or [a] red [substance] so that with its sharpness it corrodes what is left of the splinter.
But if this is not possible for you, collect everything at that time, and then you plaster everything with something that alleviates his pain, and leave it for a few days until his pain is soothed and the risk of a hot abscess is avoided.
And then repeat on him according to the same manner until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
Concerning the reduction of a nasal fracture.
When you treat a fracture with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, as previously described, and it is not healed, then there is no pus within it, but rather fissures over the abscess before its maturation.  And extract all the moisture that is within it and the pus until the bone is exposed.
And thus, when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is named a rasp, and let it be made of Indian iron, and let its head be round like a button, which should be pointed by filing or sharpened by subtle filing (as it is in the following form).
And place that [rasp] upon the corrupted place of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that that corruption is scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and then restore the place with drying and styptic substances.
Then, if the place is consolidated and good flesh has grown there and the body is separated from the healthy part and remains so for forty days, and is not renewed [i.e., the corruption does not return] and is not suppurated and no accident befalls it, then you know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is nothing to do but reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, and according to this manner:  that is, you uncover the second bone with an iron or with a sharp medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a rasp according to this form:
Let the extremity of the iron rasp be triangular, and its shaft be thin and tapered to the extremity, like this.  (The phrase "like this" implies a diagram would accompany the text).
And then place that [rasp] on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal bone; and move your hand a little from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the rasp until it penetrates all the bone, and the patient will feel it because he will feel the wind escaping as you move your hand.
Then restore the place with styptic and drying medicines as has been shown to you.
Then, when the flesh grows and the place is hardened, the matter which flowed to the outside parts returns to the nose, therefore this is easier for the patient.
Chapter 20.
Of the reduction of the protrusion of the eye.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety and there is no accompanying damage, diminution, or weakening, then you should leave the patient alone and closely observe them.  Apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification and suck [the blood] for good evacuation; then apply to the eye an ointment made from  acacia, aloe, incense, and sarcocolla, and firmly fasten the plaster to the eye with plenty of powder.
And the manner of the powders is that you take many linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded into four layers; and make [a poultice] from those.
And then place those [cloths] one on top of the other according to the amount the eye needs. And then bind those [cloths] with a firm cloth with strong compression; and repeat the cupping with suction without scarification; and then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye bound both day and night.
But if meanwhile the ligament is softened, then it should be bound very tightly.
And then remove the binding both day and night, as we have said.
And if sometimes the eye is already reduced, it is good, and if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders and the binding and the cupping until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the incision of the eminence of the uvea. (or Concerning the incision of the prominence of the uveal tunic)
When there is a rupture in the uvea, and it is prominent and protrudes beyond the eyelids like a grape, and the appearance of a man is marred by it, and you wish to incise it, then the operation is as we have recounted, that you insert the needle into the root of the uvea from below to above, and then place another needle with double thread from the lacrimal side, and make it penetrate and leave the first needle according to its disposition; and then incise the place of the double thread and lift with it the part of the uvea in the circuit of the needle, and tighten it greatly.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool infused in egg white and leave it until the thread with the uvea falls away.
And then treat the eye with that which comforts it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes gather from all sides with a circle of cloth around the eye a great prominent swelling, and then it should be perforated with a fine spatula that reaches to the pupil of the eye, and the albuminous moisture will flow out, and the eye will descend and enter immediately.
And then tighten it until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Concerning the treatment of alconati (likely a type of eye affliction or injury).
This illness, which is called alconati, is nothing but a swelling which is attached to the eye, resembling descending water, but it is not that.
But the method of working on it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared and straight seat, and then take his head in your hands on both sides and gently pull it until you see the swelling descend to the lower parts, and then it should be dried and not descend, and light should be avoided, and the patient should see things as he used to see.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the swelling is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a fine spatula and use it on the pannicle, carefully around the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the crown (near the empty space), until the swelling is expelled.
Then, when it is expelled, you instill warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then it should be treated with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Concerning the treatment of the descending water of the eye.  (Or: Concerning the treatment of the watery discharge from the eye.)
We have already discussed the types of watery discharge in the division and which ones should be perforated, as stated above; therefore, this [treatment] according to the health [of the patient]. (A more literal but less clear translation might be:  "We have already said the species of the water in the division and which ones suit to perforate with sermo declaring, then that one according to the health.")
Then it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands on a chair and bind his/her healthy eye, tying it tightly.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of his/her eye, if the eye in which the water is located is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is the left one; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then place the end of the almond near the cornea, along the edge of the iris in the white of the eye on the side of the smaller tear duct, and then press the almond gently and roll your hand with it.
And then penetrate the white of the eye and feel that the almond has already reached the empty space (or, the source of the problem).
And it should be that the distance of the almond to the pupil is the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness (iris), which is the cornea of the eye; for you will see with that pupil through the clarity of the corneal tunic (cornea).
And then place the almond into the place where the water (fluid) is, and then you draw it down one time and then another, and then if the water descends immediately, then the patient sees what is above him immediately, and the almond in his eye.
And then rest a little; then if the water has descended, then you remove it another time before the almond is discarded.
And then when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond easily, and gently roll it with your hand, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in the water and wash the eye inside with it; and then place on the outside of the eye cotton or tow or wool infused in rose oil and egg white and bind the eye with this.
But we and those of our time put on that [wound] crushed comfrey with egg white.
And if the almond does not obey you upon entering the eye because of its hardness — for there are some men whose eyes are very hard — then it is necessary that you take the instrument which is called an alberid, of which this is the form:
Then perforate that joint with it, and do not thrust your hand into the hole, but only make a subtle passage for the almond.
And then introduce the almond as we have said.
And for the completion of your work, a suitable sleeping place should be prepared for the patient, where he may sleep on his back in a dark room, and all movement should be prohibited.
And position him in such a way that his body is aligned, and he does not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And let the dressing remain according to its disposition until the third day.
Then unbind it in that dark room and expose the affected area to view. Show him certain things, and then reapply the dressing by the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that you do this during the hour of the treatments or immediately after the infusion. Having said that, you must avoid this because the water may leak slightly due to great and vehement movement.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you must unbind the wound before the seventh day, and treat it with something that reduces the abscess.
Then you wash his face and place a cloth on his face through which his vision will be obscured for a few days while he remains in the dark room.
And then let him be removed from that dark room gradually and he should talk about his condition.
And know that in this case the pupil is not excused from some visions, and then you should be bold about the operation.
And it has come to us from some of those who are in Artaix, and someone said that a fire was made in Ayrac, a perforated oven with which water was drawn.
However, I have not seen anyone in our land who has perfected this, nor have I read about it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is new.
And these are the forms of the types of oven for those who are ignorant of them:
And let them be made of bronze or iron, properly, and let their extremities be of this triangulated subtlety, the pointed extremity thus, as that is the form.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of raw flesh in the nose.
When diverse growths of flesh are born in the nose, some of which resemble a very small scorpion, or a polyp fish; and this sickness is called polyp because it resembles that fish in appearance. And from it is cancerous flesh, patchy, of dark color; and from it is flesh like a lion, not of dark color.
Therefore, in that which is in these fleshy growths, neither fraudulent nor cancerous, you should make the patient sit between your hands and placed in the sun, and open their nostrils, and prick your fingernail in those growths. Then extract that to the outside parts, and then incise what you have grasped of those with a sharp, thin spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed; that if any part remains from which excision is possible, then you scrape that with one of the fine instruments easily until nothing remains of it. But if it bleeds profusely or a hot abscess forms, then you apply to it what is necessary; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then you incise and cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the cavities are cleared.
Then let there be poured into the nose near the incision, strong wine and water or wine. But if the nose is open and humidity proceeds from it into the palate, then know that it is now healed.
But if the humidity does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is rotten flesh in the deeper bones of the nostrils to which excision has not reached.
Therefore, it is necessary that you introduce a linen thread into which some thickness has been added, and tie knots in it, and place between each knot the quantity of a finger or less, and then guide the patient and introduce one end of the thread into the nostrils with a rod or with any other instrument that is available.
And after this is done, let him draw in its scent until it reaches the nasal passages and is expelled through his throat, for often children do something similar at school, and it is an easy thing for one who wishes to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end emerging from the nose and the other remaining inside the nose, and then administer sawdust of meat with the knots that are on the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then remove the thread, and place near the nose, close to the extraction of blood, a poultice, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the rotten flesh.
Finally, at the end of the treatment, place a lead cannula in the nose for a few days until it is healed.
But if you need a more drying cure, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument, a nasal purge-maker, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make it of silver or bronze, which should resemble a small open lamp, like a small bowl, and the spout similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the nasal purge-making instrument should be flat and open; to which there should be a handle at its rear, with which you hold it when you heat the oil or water, or whatever you wish of the juices and extractable substances.
Chapter 25.
Of nasal polyps in the extremities of the nose.
Many times a polyp grows in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in some days, until it is very noticeable to the observer.
And for this it is necessary that you incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all of it. And then apply to the location either cautery or a burning medicine which will stay in its place.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is solidified, hard, of dark color and with little sensation; then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess; for many times he who incises this abscess suffers great harm from it, on account of the malignancy of the one who had it.
And if the abscess is of a light touch and not of dark color, and sometimes incision is possible in all of it, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and cure the place with that which dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Concerning the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when their continuity is broken by a wound or similar injury.
Know that when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, you should place little confidence in that operation except in certain men.
Therefore, when it happens to someone with any of these conditions, you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its blood, and then you should join the two edges of the wound with a suture, and then cure it until it is healed.
And if the edges of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each is healed, then it is necessary that you scrape each part of its apparent surface until blood comes out. Then join the two edges with sutures and tighten them, and over them sprinkle dragon's blood and finely crushed incense, and place over the powder a piece of diachylon plaster, or of any other of the consolidating plasters, and leave it bound for two or three days, and then unbind it and change the plaster and leave it until the stitches are naturally shed, and then cure it with ointment until it is healed.
But the manner of the suture is that you close the solution of continuity either with needles, as we have recounted in the suture of the abdomen, or close the solution of continuity with thread, as has been shown to you.
Chapter 27.
Concerning the extraction of those things which have entered between the two lips in the inner parts.
Many times there appear in men in the inner parts of their lips certain hard abscesses resembling kernels, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Then it is necessary that you evert the lips and separate each of those hardnesses from every part, and afterwards fill the place of the excision with crushed sage until the blood is restrained, and afterwards let it be washed with sharp wine and the place be treated with something that is styptic, until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the incision of the flesh adhering to the gums.
Very often there grows upon the gum flesh that adheres, which the ancients called an albulus; then it is necessary that you lift that up with a hook or that you hold that with forceps and incise that to its root, and allow the pus or the blood to flow.
And afterwards place on the area crushed sage or other styptic or drying powders.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it, for afterwards the cautery will not return.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the reason for teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both the inner and outer parts, and between the gums, rough, sticky, and sometimes blackened scabs are attached, and they become citrine or green where they touch the gum, causing corruption and making the teeth sticky.
And therefore it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and rest their head on your lap, and scrape the teeth on which those scabs and similar things appear, like sand, until nothing remains of them, and this removes the blackness, the yellowness, and the others, until they are cleaned.
Therefore, if what was in those at the beginning of the treatment is removed, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the treatment on those on the next day, and the second, and the third until you arrive at the desired end.
And know that the teeth require many scrapers of various shapes and forms, according to what is prepared for your operation. And because the scraper with which the tooth is scraped on the inner part is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth according to other forms, and these forms of scrapers should be prepared by you, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of teeth.
That you cure the tooth of its pain with all diligence and delay its extraction, for there is no cause that compensates for its loss once it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance whose extraction is excused.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is diseased, for many times the pain deceives the patient and he believes it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, and then the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operations of barbers.
Therefore, once you are certain which tooth is diseased, and you know it well, then you must scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is loosened from the gum on all sides, and then remove it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, initially little by little until it is completely loose.
Then apply strong, large forceps for good traction or firm hold, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold it well and it does not move, and then extract the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause the patient greater pain.
And then, if it is not dislodged, then take one of those instruments, and place it under it on all sides with ease and force its removal in the same way as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or eroded, then you must fill that crack with cloth and tighten it with the end of a fine thread so that it is not broken at the time of your tightening with the forceps.
And you must open carefully with scarification around all parts of the gum and watch your strength so as not to break it and leave a part of it behind, and return to the patient because the harm may be greater than the first pain he felt.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and audacity concerning extraction, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great illnesses in men which are more difficult to cure, and their roots remain embedded, all or some of them, or that you extract it with some bones of the jaw, as we have often witnessed.
And then, after its extraction, the mouth should be washed with warm wine or soured wine with salt.
And if bleeding ensues from this, as often happens, then crush some quantity of sage and sprinkle it on the wound, and if this sage does not stop the bleeding, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and the delicate tongs with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be long in the gripping part, short and thick in the handle, so that they do not bend when you work with them.
And let them be, as it were, of thick handle so that when you squeeze with them they are not bent; [let them have] short gripping ends.
And let them be made of Indian iron, preferably made with straight ends, and at the ends of these let there be teeth, of which some interlock with others and tighten by strong constriction; and sometimes the ends of these should be in the shape of the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* (that is, a stork), and are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
Of the extraction of teeth and the removal of broken jawbones.
When you must extract a tooth which is broken, and some cause remains, then it is necessary that you apply a poultice to the area for a day or two until the area is softened; and then introduce pincers or forceps, the ends of which resemble the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* or stork, the shape of forceps:
Their ends should be made inside like a file or rasp.
And if it does not yield to you in this way with these forceps or pincers, then it is necessary that you excavate around the root and clear away all the flesh and introduce the instrument which is called *atali*, that is, a small lever whose shape is this:
And let it be of short, somewhat thick extremity, and not be bent so that it is not broken.
Therefore, if by this the root is extracted, it is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is of a triangular extremity in which there is some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it should be triangular and of a more subtle extremity.
And now you are aided by this instrument having two ramps, of which this is the form, approximately the following: and with other instruments which precede in the scraping of teeth; for help is given with this instrument which resembles a large hook, and this is its form:
of the triangular, curved or short extremity, which should have some thickness, and should not be bent so that it is not broken.
And also know that the instruments for loose teeth are similarly made to the other instruments.
But the wise and subtle artificer in the art finds for himself, according to what the operation and the diverse illnesses signify to him; for there are illnesses to which the ancients did not give or devise a proper instrument, because of the forms of diverse species.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is broken, or if it is possible, then it should be cured in that place with what is convenient to it from among the instruments and forceps which we have mentioned in the extraction of the roots of the teeth, and help yourself with a splint of which this is the form:
And let there be some thickness to it little by little, and let it be tightened or held with the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until the bone is completely set; and then restore the place with medicines suitable for this.
But if in the bone there is a small rottenness, then scrape away that rottenness and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of teeth that protrude above the others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural alignment, their shape is unsightly, and this is especially true for women, and especially for venerable old women; therefore you must consider: if a tooth is next to another tooth, and neither extraction nor filing is possible, then extract it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you incise it with an instrument of this form; it is similar to a boar's tusk, and is made of Indian iron, with a very sharp point.
And let your incision, with which you incise it, be done over several days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that no other teeth are damaged.
And if the protruding tooth is suitable for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of this shape:
And let the entire file be of Indian iron and very smooth; and let it have very fine points, like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. Then smooth it at the end, and scrape it with some scrapers.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and injures the tongue, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and is filled so that the tongue is not injured nor the speech impaired.
Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver wire.
When the front teeth are damaged by concussion and loosening due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats, and you have treated it with styptic medicines, and your treatment has not been effective, then so that they do not fall out, let those teeth be fastened with gold wire and not silver, because silver turns green and soon becomes powdery after a few days, but gold is never changed.
And let the wire be of such fineness and thickness according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the manner of binding is that you take the wire and pass a double strand between two healthy teeth, and then let it increase with two turns of the wire between the damaged ones, whether one or many, until the wire reaches a healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the weaving back to the point where you began, and tighten with your hand gently and skillfully until it is firm.
And let your tightening, with which you fasten the wire, be such that it does not slip from the roots of the teeth.
And then cut off the superfluous ends of the wire with forceps or scissors, and smooth those ends in each place, and bend them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightly bound.
But if it is loosened or broken, tighten it or them with another wire in the same manner.
And this is the form of the teeth and the manner of the binding of two healthy teeth and two damaged teeth, as you see these bound:
And when it is done that one or two teeth return to their proper places, and are grown back as we have recounted, and that they are healthy again.
And only a good and skilled craftsman does this.
And sometimes some material from cow bones is suitable, and it is made from them in the form of a tooth, and is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and is fastened as we have said, and remains and serves in that place for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the incision of the ligament which is in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which is under the tongue, is sometimes natural, with which one is born, or accidental and occurs due to a wound which has already healed.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, their head being in your lap; and lift their tongue, and then incise that ligament, following its width, until the tongue is freed from its constraint.
But if in that area there is some hardness or knotting, and this is due to the healing of a wound, then you should use an unguent and dissect it across its width until you have incised the ligament and the knotting is resolved.
And be careful that no fissure is made in the depth of the flesh and that you do not cut too deeply and cause a flow of blood.
And then wash the site of the incision with warm water or with sour wine or with cold water; and then place under the tongue a linen cloth; that is, a piece of cloth that the patient should keep under their tongue each night, so that it does not heal again.
