But if bleeding occurs, place crushed vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a lancet-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess similar to a small tumor occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and it is quickly enlarged until it fills the mouth.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, sitting facing the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is dark or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancerous.
And if it is tending towards white, and there is moisture in it, then you place the hook there and incise it with a thin spatula, and incise it completely; and if blood flows during your operation, then place crushed vitriol on it until the bleeding is stopped.
Then return to your work until you have completely removed it, until it is washed with sharp wine and salt; and treat it with the other remedies for this until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36.  Concerning the extraction of an abscess from both ears and from what is festering in the throat and other abscesses.
When a swelling occurs in the throat, these are glands that are gathered together like cotton, which protrude outwards and are called tonsils; and if you treat them with what we have said in the previous section and it is not healed, then consider if the abscess is hard, dark in color, and insensitive; if so, do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red and the root is large, then do not touch it with iron because of the flow of blood; rather, let it mature, and then either you will extract it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is white, soft, and its root is thin, then it is something that should be incised.
And the operation is that you should consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already matured with complete suppuration or if it has diminished in any way; then make the patient sit before the sun, with his head in your lap, and open his mouth, and the assistant, taking it between his hands, should press down the patient's tongue with a very fine instrument, which is of this form:
And let it be made of silver or bronze, and let it be as thin as a knife.
Then, as you press the tongue with this and the abscess is shown to you, and you have observed it carefully, then take an oncia [ounce, possibly referring to a type of surgical instrument or a quantity of medicine] and put it in the tonsil, and draw it outwards as much as you can. Further, if you pull out some matter from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Further, if you pull out some matter from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it resembles forceps, however its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is opposite the other, very sharp, which should be made of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then incise it with a spatula of which this is the form: And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one side, incise the other in the same manner of incision, and immediately after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, gargle with a decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves and similar styptic substances until the flow of blood is restrained, and immediately treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear in the throat besides those two [near the ears], and incise those according to what I have said in the incision of two sides equally.
And I myself once treated a woman with an abscess which had formed in her throat, which abscess was tending towards a dark color, with little feeling, which had already closed her throat, and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her eating and drinking water, and she was on the verge of death if she had lasted another day or two.
And the abscess had swollen so much that it had created two openings from which pus emerged.
Then I treated it with a fistula (incision), and I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and I pulled it, and a large portion was drawn out, and immediately I incised it from where I understood from the other opening, and immediately I opened its mouth, and compressed its tongue, and immediately I inserted the probe into that abscess.
And immediately I excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Then the throat of that woman was completely clear, and she immediately began to drink water, and soon afterwards she took food.  Then I did not cease to incise that abscess many times over a long period, and another abscess formed in the place of that one, which I incised until it was eliminated.
Then I cauterized her throat, therefore I desisted from further treatment; and immediately I left, and I did not know what God would do about it afterwards.
Chapter 37.
On the incision of a boil when it is abscessed.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and it is abscessed and elongated, then it is called a perpendicular; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a buba (a type of swelling).
But when it is cured with what we have said in the division, and your treatment is not successful, and you see that the abscess is already detached from it and is subtle, then you should incise that which is round and aggregated; and if it is of such a length, and is bloody, of a dark color or black, and it is not sensitive to the patient, then you should attempt its incision, for in that there is danger for the patient.
And when you see it according to the manner I have described, its whiteness and length, make the patient sit and press their tongue with an instrument which follows its shape, and then insert the hook into the uvula, and pull it down and incise it with one of the two instruments which are mentioned in the incision of two boils (or, possibly, 'two humors').
And you should not incise it except what is appropriate to the natural limit without addition, for if you incise more of it, you will injure his speech.
And after the incision, use what we have recounted in the incision of two boils (or, possibly, 'two humors'), and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by its incision, you should use ingenuity in its cauterization without fear, and the manner of cauterization is only with sharp water (vinegar?), and it is that you place the patient's head in your lap, and then press their tongue with the instrument which we have mentioned, and then take the water which we have mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with that live coal, and do not apply it thickly or thinly, and with it fill the concavity of the instrument of which this is the form:
Let its extremity be concave, in which the medicine is placed, like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and let the patient lie on their side so that saliva or mucus runs from their mouth because of the internal medicine, so that nothing descends from it onto their throat and harms it.
And then hold your hand with the medicine, and press it on the uvula for half an hour until you see it become blackened and dried by the action of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and submerge the cotton in the medicine, and insert the spoon with the cotton into a cannula from above so that the cotton adheres to that cannula, and do this as many times as necessary until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it as it will dry and fall off in about three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it, and after the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in wine, and clean with it what remains around it from the medicine, and wash it with cold water, and treat it externally with an embrocation, and internally with gargles until it is healed.
And this type of treatment is safer than incision and less frightening.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with a method more subtle than cautery or incision, for it is cured with fumigation in this manner: take calamint, hyssop, origanum, rue, dried arabic gum, chamomile,  and similar herbs, and mix all these ingredients, or some of them, in a pot, and submerge them in sharp wine, and boil; and the pot should be well sealed with suitable clay, and in the middle of the pot there should be a hole, over which a concave brass instrument is placed in this way; it should be made of silver or brass.
And insert the end into the patient's mouth so that the vapor reaches the uvula through the cannula, until the uvula is thoroughly fumigated.
And then repeat this many times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the development of an abscess, for it would increase the abscess too much, and you should not do that, but rather cauterize the evident abscess.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a reed and attach an eggshell to the end of it, so that the patient's mouth is protected, because the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and that which adheres in the throat and other causes.
Many times something adheres to the throat, a fish bone, or some other cause.
Then it is necessary that you extract that which is apparent of those things, upon which your sight falls, after you have drawn the tongue forward with an instrument, in the sunlight, so that that which is in the throat may be revealed to you.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for very quickly the cause attached to the throat will be expelled by vomit; or let the patient swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or let him take a piece of dried sea sponge, strongly bound with thread, and let the patient swallow it, for when it arrives at the place where the thorns are, it will extract them, and then they will be expelled. And further, take an eel, bound with thread, and let it be inserted and quickly withdrawn; or take a leek and quickly insert it and quickly withdraw it.
But if they are not expelled by those means which we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument according to this form:
And let it be somewhat thicker than a finger, and at its extremity let there be a concavity which you may easily introduce into his throat.
And let him have his head raised upwards, and be careful not to touch his throat bone, lest it cause him pain, and with that push out the bone or spine, or let the physician do it with his hand.
But however, it is better that the patient himself introduce it, because of his knowledge of the location of the affected area, and push downwards, and pull his hand up with the instrument, all this according to the amount that is prepared for him, until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is medicated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and it doesn't work, then consider placing it on the patient's throat in the sun, after you have compressed his tongue with the instrument which we have recommended to you.
Therefore, if you see the leech, extract it with a small hook or with gentle, careful movements.
And if it is not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a glowing iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this as many times (until the leech is dead and detaches from the throat), and abstain the patient from all water that day.
Then take a vial full of cold water, and open his mouth into it, and wash his mouth with it; and do not swallow any of it, and stir the water every now and then with your hand, for as soon as the leech feels the water, it will fall off.
But if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with cyperus or asafoetida, using the instrument which we have recommended for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall out.
And another method of operation in the fumigation of this is that you take a pot in which there are burning coals, and the pot is covered with a lid in the middle of which there is a hole, and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole; and then begin the fumigation; and the patient puts his mouth to the end of the cannula, and closes his mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will immediately fall.
And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and abstain the patient from drinking, and eating salty things and garlic, and do not drink water, and thus it is necessary that with this regimen it will be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is drawn from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps like these, except that there is some curvature in it: which is placed in the throat, and its end is similar to a stork's beak, which is hollow, in which there is a roughness of filing, so that, when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the sum of the discourse on the opening of abscesses and on their incision.
Abscesses are of many kinds, of many manners, as you will find a remembrance of them, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in perforation and incision of them by two manners, of which one is by the kind of abscess in itself; and by that which we understand of the humors.
And the second manner is according to the places in which they happen in the body, for an abscess happening in the head is different from an abscess happening in the buttock; and an abscess in the fleshy part is different from an abscess happening in a joint; and to each of them is its judgment for operation.
And moreover, of abscesses, there are some which it is necessary should not be perforated except near maturation of the pus in them or completion of them.
There are other abscesses which it is necessary should be opened when they are raw, and are not matured according to their completion, thus as abscesses which are near the joints, for when an abscess is near a joint, its being there is prolonged until that which is in its circuit is consumed, and very quickly the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, wherefore a cause of contraction of that member is made; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay its perforation until it is matured it will harm the principal member; or it is near the buttock, therefore perforate that which is not matured; and it is not necessary that you perforate it raw, of incomplete digestion, except that which is near the buttock, so that its matter may not be consumed and be elongated to the inner parts of the buttock, and a fistula be made, or that it be made in such a way that it may not be healed. Moreover, it is necessary that you know the time of the perforation of the abscess, and this is when it is matured.
And you will know that it is matured when there is a lessening of the pain and the cessation of the fever, and also the diminution of the redness and of the pulsation, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because it is necessary that you know that the perforation falls in the place lowest of the abscess and from which comes the healing of the protrusion.
And the perforation should always be along the length of the flesh or of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or of the feet, and away from the tendons, the cords of the nerves, the arteries; and, finally, in all equal places in which there is no reflection; for in those which are reflected, use perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you leave the perforation of it until its digestion is confirmed according to the completion, as we have said; for if you perforate it before that, the body of the pus is prolonged and much filth remains, and quickly its lips are hardened and its depth increased.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or of the body because of necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and in large abscesses a large and wide perforation or several incisions according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses in which it is necessary that the skin be incised and curved when it is made like bread and is ripe, like that which happens in cases of buboes and swellings.
And some of those are incised with a three-angled incision; and others are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the armpits.
And there are those in which a round incision and a lunar incision are administered, and similar to those.
But that which is not like the aforementioned superficial abscesses, you must perforate it with only a superficial or flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you must perforate it and not worry about extracting all the pus at that time; having extracted a part of it, then dress the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger part, and do this many times according to the degree until it is completely evacuated.
And especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person in decrepitude, because the animal spirit is often dissolved with the exit of the pus, and the patient dies very quickly, and you open them.
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution, and concerning your perforation with which you perforate these abscesses, you must clean the wounds and consider whether the wound of the abscess is small, or if it is a flat incision, then you apply lint or old linen to it; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you must place a wick in each opening until they join together.
And if a part of the skin is cut away in the abscess, or you have hollowed it out in the circumference, then you must fill it with old linen or thread made from linen cloth, as when you have drawn out splinters or roots, without moisture; and bind it tightly until the third day; and then proceed and treat it with the ointments that are necessary until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and sour wine in which you soak a linen cloth, and apply this to the place of the bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must apply the regimen and treatment and powders which I have recounted in many places in this our book and division.
But if it is in winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you must mix powders in warm wine and oil, and apply them to the area.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders mixed with water and oil or with wine and oil, and all this should be kept cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you should open the abscess and cleanse it and administer to its treatment what is suitable for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally, but according to the method of distinction, I have already spoken of each abscess and how to treat it in particular chapters.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which appear in the back of the head.
In the back of the head, small abscesses appear, a kind of nodes which contain membranes that are attached to them; and their types are many.
And some of them are purulent, and those contain a humidity similar to lime (or possibly, referring to a viscous, slimy consistency). And some of them contain a humidity similar to dust, and resembling them; and some of them are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, since the extraction of them does not cause contrary harm.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is similar to this, which is called an entromes (or similar sounding surgical tool), until you know what it contains.
For if that which it contains is humidity, then incise it with a cut along its length, with a superficial incision according to this form:
And begin the incision from line B to line G.  Then, when the humidity is evacuated, you should scrape that skin where the humidity was contained, and do not leave any of it at all, because often what is removed returns if any of it remains.
Then submerge cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is available, and if not, in salt water; and fill the wound with it and leave it there until the next day, for with this you will corrode all that remains of that film.
And then iterate on that cotton with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if needed, until you are confident that there is no remaining part of that film; then heal the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodules, then you should incise it according to this form: with an incision in the form of a cross, and insert forceps between the two lips of the incision and scrape it from every part, and exert your strength in extracting the tunic which contains it.
But if the wound resists you, then do what I recounted to you first.
And similarly proceed with a lapified abscess in incision and cure as we have told you; and incision on a lapified abscess is easier, for it has little blood and little humidity.
And sometimes some of these abscesses are found in the head of some men, in which there is no humidity at all; the reason for this is that I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if anyone were struck with it, it would hurt them.
But in other abscesses occurring in the head other than this, this is not the chapter, such as the abscesses that appear on the heads of children and over the roots of the ears; but the incision of all of these should be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation of those in the lowest part of them so that the body may be relieved of the pus in the lower parts.
And then treat those with what is appropriate for that treatment.
Chapter 42.
Chapter 42: On the incision of scrophulas which occur in the neck.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck, armpits, and groin; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck, many are generated among themselves, and every scrofula of these is within its own membrane, just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the types of these scrophulas are many, for some are stony, some contain humidity, and some are malignant which do not yield to treatment.
Therefore, in that which you see to be of good disposition on touching, the color of which is clearly close to the color of the flesh, and is soft in every part, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not deep, you should incise it with a superficial incision from the upper to the lower part of the body, from line G to line B, and scrape it from every part, and extend the lips of the flesh with hooks, or with many hooks if you have need of them, as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is most important that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the instrument not be too sharp, lest it cause you to injure yourself with the incision, or disturb the patient, and that you incise what is not necessary to incise; for if you incise a vein or artery and you are prohibited from your operation, then place on the wound crushed vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides and the wound is softened and begins to fester. When the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then investigate with your index finger if there is any cause of other small scrofulas, then incise those and clean them.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, then you must not incise that scrofula at its root. Afterwards, it is necessary that you tie it with strong doubled thread and that you leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old linen which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then cure it.
And if you incise the whole scrofula, then you must adjust the two lips of the wound and sew it as soon as there is no remaining superfluity.
But if you see that there remains superfluity because of the size of the scrofula, you must understand how to incise its root, and extract it, and administer suture and what has been said.
And as for those scrofulas that contain humidity, perforate it with a superficial perforation where the place of its maturation appears to you, and place the perforation so that it is at the lowest part of the body, as we have said.
Then administer a perforation, perhaps with ointment and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound to be already cleansed, then you cure it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Concerning the incision of the abscess that accidentally occurs within the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the throat, but no one in our land has been seen to have performed it.
And this is the description/writing of the speech/discourse of those (people/authors).
But in men having quinsy, the extraction from the throat must be avoided, because no benefit is gained thereby, since all the veins and the largest ones of the patient are there.
But in those who have abscesses located in the mouth, throat, or ears, when the illness is not in the windpipe (trachea), it is necessary to use the incision of the throat to avoid the harm which is caused by perforation/suppuration.
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the throat below three circles of the circles of the throat, or four, with a small incision in width between two circles; such that the incision is in the fleshy part, not in the cartilage.  This location is suitable for incision because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant.
And if the physician is timid/fearful, then he should extend the skin of the throat with a hook, and then split the skin until he reaches the abscess, but he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any of them.
And then incise the fleshy part as we have recommended; and the incision of the windpipe is indicated by the phlegm which emerges from it along with what is broken up from it, and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Then, when the time for healing has arrived in which perforation is feared, bring together the two lips of the skin of the wound and stitch it up, leaving out the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that promote the growth of flesh until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the sum of the discourse which we have recounted, the explanation is nothing other than that you see that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the throat of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and his breath is cut off, and some go to incise the epiglottis so that the patient breathes above the site of the wound, through some opening for breathing, and is saved from death.
And for this reason, I order the wound to be left open until the malignancy of the disease is consumed; and its malignancy lasts for three days; and then I order the wound to be sewn up, and cure it until it is healed.
And what I have seen with my own eyes is that a servant took a knife and put it in her throat and incised with it a part of the windpipe, and I was called to her cure, and I found the mucus as it is found in the dead, and I uncovered the wound and found the blood which had come from the small wound.
Therefore, I was confessed that she had not incised a vein or artery, and the wind (breath) was coming out of the wound, and I cured it until it was healed.
And nothing happened to the servant except hoarseness in her voice, and after a few days she returned to a better state of health.
And now I say that in the incision of the tonsil there is no fear (or danger).
Chapter 44.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the throat externally, and is called a throat hernia (or possibly a goiter).
This abscess, called a throat hernia, is a large abscess according to the complexion of the body, and is very common in women.
And it is of two types, for it is either natural or accidental.
And the natural one is not capable of being cured.
And the accidental one is of two kinds, of which one resembles fatty nodes, and the other kind resembles an abscess which is caused by the knotting of an artery, and in this one there is danger. And one should not presume to use iron on it except in that which is small. And you probe that and investigate it with the finger, and if you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not connected to any of the veins, then you incise it like the nodes, and extract it with what contains it from the skin and the blister; and if not, then its whole being is explored, and then treat the area with what is needed for healing until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
Of the incision of the types of nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already described all their types in the division.
And it is necessary that I recount thus the difference between a node and an abscess, lest there be doubt.
Therefore I say that with an abscess there is heat and fever and vehement pain until the boiling of the superfluity is sedated; and putrefaction is complete, at which point the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a node there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that lymphatic fluid which is in its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a swelling and is made like a bladder, either larger or smaller, and there are two types, for they are either fatty and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, as I said in the division.
Therefore it is necessary, when you arrive at the cure of a node, that you draw out that [fluid] and inquire into it at the beginning with an instrument which is called a probe, according to what its form will be in the chapter which is near this.
And the manner of inquiry into abscesses and all nodes is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will turn your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has penetrated the core.
Then place your hand according to the measure of the size of the abscess, and then remove the probe and consider what has come out in its wake.
And if the flowing humidity, of whatever color it may be, is expelled, then you incise it with a superficial incision as I have said in the other abscesses.
And if you find no humidity in the wake of the probe, then know that it is fatty.
And incise it in the form of a cross, according to this shape: Thus as I have taught you, and suspend it with hooks, and easily peel the skin from all sides, and keep the skin or sac, if you can, over it and extract that pus with the node.
But if it is broken during the operation, it is not possible to extract all the pus, and this often happens, and then you extract it in pieces until nothing remains.
Because if anything remains, little or much, the node will return to its former place.
And if you see that a small part remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and bind the wound, and place a heated cautery over it, and treat it with other cures until it is healed.
And if it is a large node, then you sew the two lips of it and treat it with that which causes it to grow flesh (i.e., promotes granulation and healing).
But if a pulsating vein is found near it, or a non-pulsating one, then you consider and fill the area with crushed vitriol and let it remain there for two or three days until the wound dries and the boiling of the blood subsides; and then return to incise that which is the remaining part of the node.

Chapter 47.
Concerning the care of men's breasts which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men become inflamed due to sleeping habits,  and then the breasts become swollen and resemble those of women, and remain suppurating, and white.
And for someone who has this, it is necessary that you incise those with a lunate incision, according to this form, from line B to line G, and then scrape away all the thickness, and then fill the wound with incarnative medicine, and then approximate the two lips of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the breast sags downwards and is softened by its size, as happens in women, then it should be cut in its two upper parts, with two incisions similar to a lunar shape, each contained within the other at their ends, so that the larger line contains the smaller, according to this form, from line B to line G.  And then scrape the skin which is between the two lips of the wound, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said about the suture and the powders which are necessary in the treatment until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of what needs to be incised because of the patient's discomfort and bleeding, then you should fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes the remaining fat, and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Of the perforation of abscesses that come below the nipple.
These abscesses that appear below the nipple are a kind of scrofula, hard, and containing a sac-like skin.
And some of these contain humidity; and those that contain humidity should be incised with a lunar section according to this figure: from line G to line B. And when all the pus in the abscess has been evacuated, then you should fill it with old linen and leave it there until the next day.
And then the linen should be removed and the wound treated with a suitable ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the type of nodes and scrofula, then you should incise it as we have said in the section on scrofula.
But if the wound is so extensive that it causes putrefaction, then administer cautery as we have previously described.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur from arrow wounds or from bloodletting.
When an arrow wound is broken and the scab which is over it is consolidated, then very often an abscess occurs.  The signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are due to an artery or vein are these: because when the abscess is due to an arrow wound, it is long and deeply seated in the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel a kind of creaking or grating sound within it.
And that which is from a vein is round and superficial on the body.
And incision of this abscess is dangerous, especially in that which is under the armpits, and in the groin, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very large, therefore its treatment with iron should be avoided.  And that which is in the extremities, or on the head, should be avoided [incised].
But concerning that which is from them [abscesses] by inflammation of the opening of the arrow wound, make a longitudinal incision in the scab; and then open the incision with forceps and approach the arrow, and carefully separate the layers of tissue until it is uncovered.
And then place a needle there and make it penetrate to the other side, and tighten the arrow with doubled thread in two places—the method of which I have shown you in the extraction of two arrows which were in the temples.
And then prick the place which is between two ligaments with a lancet until all the blood which is there comes out, and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer treatment to where pus is generated until the ligaments fall away; and then treat that with ointments suitable for this purpose until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess from a severed vein, then you should hold with your hand as much of the abscess as you can with the scab, and then insert the needle under the place which you have held under your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be double thread in it so that you extract it from the other side, and then tie the abscess with good thread, as I have told you in the ligature of a grape [vein] in two parts with two ends of thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert the thread with that needle under the entire abscess below the incision of the first needle, and tighten the threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it until, when what is in it has come out, then you incise the superfluity of the scab, and leave what is tied there.
Then place on it a powder which you have soaked in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment which is done with poultices and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess which occurs due to the twisting of nerves.
Just as abscesses occur in arteries and veins, similarly abscesses occur in the nerves when they are struck or when they suffer excessive labor, and similarly.
And it is made worse in the locations of the joints or the heel, or in any place where the joints are weakened.
And the abscess is serious, and it resembles the color of the other body, and it is in the worst conditions when it is constricted with vehemence, causing in the patient a similar stupor.
And the abscess is not aggregated in the front of the body, and therefore it is below the ribs, and it is weakened in every part and does not go to the front or back parts.
Therefore, that which is in the joints should not be invaded with iron, for contraction will quickly result.
But that which is in the head or forehead, treat with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then grasp it with forceps and excise it from the roots.
And if it is large, then you suspend it with a hook, and scrape it, and then remove it; and close the wound with suture, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 51.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the area that occurs in the abdomen.
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies, those things which are called fungals by similarity to fungi, of which the root is subtle and the head is large; and now the lips of those are turned, and it is of those that is small, and it is of those that is greatly magnified.
And I have already seen one, and in his belly were two things which were most like fungi, and there was no difference between those white things, of slender root.
And now the lips of those were turned inward and split open, and humidity flowed from it all the time.
Therefore I incised those and found in one about eighteen ounces, and in the other about six ounces.
And the operation in the incision of them is that you consider then if the patient is humid, and the color of the thing is white, humid, of subtle root; then you incise it with a broad spatula, and let there be in your presence cauteries in the fire, for many times after the section of it much blood is expelled; and if the blood overcomes you, cauterize it.
And if you see the patient fearful and timid, beware of incision with iron; but then take freshly made lead plaster, and bind it to that thing, in the manner which we have said; and leave it for two days.
And then adjust the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not cease to tighten the lead greatly over the area, until it is severed and falls off by itself without effort.
But if the thing is of thick root, then in its incision there is some cause for fear and dread, and especially that which is in the belly.
But it is possible that you incise half of it, and then cauterize it so that it does not return.
And then treat it until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not presume to incise that which is of dark color, little feeling, and slow growth, for that is cancerous.
And I come with the reminder of cancer concerning this.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the treatment of umbilical hernia.
Umbilical hernia is caused by many things; either by fissures of the membrane which is over the belly, and from it protrudes the intestine or bowel as happens in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery as previously mentioned, or by constricted wind within it.
But if it is due to a fissure of the membrane and a protrusion of the intestine, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the bowel, then its location is, as we have recounted, of greater variability, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and returns easily; and it is quickly large, and is much increased by the intake of baths and strenuous work.
But if it is due to humidity, then it is soft and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it grow.
And if it is due to blood, then the swelling appears with these signs, tending towards blackness.
But if it is due to raw flesh, then the swelling is heavy, large and hard, and is located in a specific area.
But if it is due to flatulence, then the touch of it is soft.
And the operation in this case is that you consider then if the swelling of the umbilicus is due to arterial blood, or venous blood, or due to flatulence; then you must be prohibited from its treatment, because in that case there is fear and danger, as I have taught you in the chapter in which I discussed swellings due to artery and vein.
And if the swelling of the umbilicus is due to the intestine or hernia, then you must order the patient to hold their breath and lie stretched out; and then mark with a probe around the entire umbilicus, and then order them to lie between your hands on their back, and then encircle with a broad spatula around the umbilicus over the area which you have marked with the probe; and then extend half of the swelling to the upper parts with a large inch; and then tie around it with strong thread or linen, in a proper manner of tying.
And then open the middle of the extended swelling over the ligature and insert your finger into that opening and search for the intestine; and if you have found it and secured it by the ligature, then you carefully extract and push the intestine back into the inner parts of the abdomen.
And if you have found the hernia, then grasp it with forceps; and incise its excess parts.
But if it is against your [better judgment because of an] artery or vein, then circle that area, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and thread with strong threads and insert the two needles into the circle which you have made around the swelling in the manner of a cross, which you make penetrate; and then tighten the swelling into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the extended flesh dries and falls off by itself; or incise it when it is thinned and dried, and then treat it with whatever ointments are necessary until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the navel is from flesh growing there or from humidity, then it is necessary that you concavity the swelling as we have said and extract the blood or humidity which you find there, and then treat it with what is necessary and which will consolidate the area.
Chapter 53.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already discussed, in the division, the types of cancer and in what manner it is possible to treat it with medicines, and that it is necessary to avoid treating it with iron so that it does not become ulcerated; and we have spoken of cancer, generated in the manner and caution of its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as cancer which is in the breasts or in the groin and in similar members, to extract all of it, and especially when it is starting small.
But when it is old and large, it is not advisable to touch it, for I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone else who has been able to.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is that you proceed and purge the patient and remove some black bile, and then bleed them, if a manifest repletion is found.
And then prepare the patient with a preparation in which the area to be operated on is situated, and then apply to the wound ointments which are suitable for it; and then scrape from all sides with a good, sharp curette until no part of its roots remains. And let the blood flow, and do not stop it lightly, and press the areas, and wipe away the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instrument among your instruments is possible for you.
But if a large flow of blood troubles you, from an incision of a vein or artery, then cauterize it until the flow of blood has ceased, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 44.  (Assuming a standard chapter numbering system)
On the treatment of dropsy.
We have already recounted, in the division, the types of dropsy of water, and the signs of each type, and their treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the species of ascites, and iron is not applied to the species of tympanites or the species of anasarca, for that is deadly.
And when you have treated this type of tympanitic dropsy with medicines and your treatment is not effective, then consider: for if the patient has already acquired debility, or has another disease along with this dropsy, such as a cough or abdominal pain and similar ailments, then be careful not to treat him with iron, for there is danger in that.
But if you see the patient with exuberant strength, and he has no other illness but dropsy alone, and is neither a child nor an old person, then the manner of his operation is that you raise the patient between your hands, and the assistant then presses his belly with his hand, driving the water toward the lower part of the pubis, and then take a sharp lancet according to this form:
Let it be of two sharp points, long, with a sharp end like a lancet; but nevertheless let there be some small shortness in it, so that in the operation with it you do not pass into the intestine, and injure it.
And then consider if the generation of the dropsy is from the intestines, for then it is necessary that you, with the incision, distance yourself from the navel by the amount of three fingers towards the lower parts, to the right of it, above the pubis.
But if the generation of the dropsy is due to a liver disease, then your incision should be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is due to the spleen, then your incision should be on the right.
And it is not fitting that your incision be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie, lest the superfluities run to that weak place.
And then perforate the entire peritoneum with the instrument, and then introduce it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the peritoneum and the omentum, and you should scrape that; and if [it is] the amount of a fingernail or thereabouts.
And then perforate the omentum until the spatula reaches the empty place, which is the location of the water. And then extract that and introduce an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to a fine silver cannula; it is made of silver or bronze or tin, to which in its lower part there is a small hole, and on its sides there are three holes: two on one side and one on the other; and its incised extremity is shaped like a feather, thus:
And at its uppermost extremity is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the water, then that immediately rests on the instrument, therefore evacuating the water in a moderate amount, for if you evacuate more than is appropriate at that time, very quickly the patient dies from the resolution of their animal spirit, or you cause a collapse which is a sign of approaching death.
But evacuate according to the quantity of his strength and according to what the patient's disposition, the strength of his pulse, and his general condition signify to you.
And then extract the instrument, and retain the water, the reason being that it is immediately retained by the chain which covers the hole which is on the cup, of which I have already told you that you should pierce it in this manner.
And then repeat the procedure with the instrument on another day if you see the patient tolerating it, and extract a small quantity of water, and do this for several days cautiously, and beware of error so that only a little water remains.
But if you are afraid for the patient and leave a large quantity of water, then treat him with a burial in warm sand, and induce sweating in a bath, and exposure to the sun for a long time; and treat him with drying medicines until he is healed.
And cauterize the stomach, the spleen, and the liver after the extraction of the water, as I have recounted in what precedes.
Chapter 45.
Concerning the treatment of children born with imperforate urinary tracts.
Sometimes a child is born from its mother's womb and the opening of its urethra is not perforated (or is narrow or not in its proper place).
Therefore, it is necessary to treat it with perforation immediately after birth, using a very fine instrument, according to this method:
Then place a thin lead pin in the opening and leave it there for three or four days.
And when it wants to urinate, it should be stimulated and will urinate; then return the pin.
And if you don't put in the lead, it is possible that the urine, which goes over the area, will not allow it to close.
But the one whose opening is narrow should be treated with a lead pin, as we have said, for several days until it is widened.
But those in whom the opening is not in its proper place—that is to say, those whose natural opening is located at the end of the penis, so that they cannot urinate in the normal way unless they lift the penis with their hand—this is not a generational issue because the sperm cannot reach the womb in a straight line; it is a very long-lasting illness.
And the manner of the operation is that the patient should be positioned on his back, and then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with a forceful pull, and incise the head of the penis at the location of the foreskin with a sharp scalpel, making an incision like a feather, or shave a little portion, so that the middle of it resembles the head of the penis and the opening falls into the middle as it should.
And be careful in your operation to avoid excessive bleeding, for that often happens; and stop the bleeding with something that absorbs blood, and care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
Concerning phimosis, which occurs in the foreskin at the head of the penis, and of corruption, blackness, and adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the penis.
This phimosis often occurs on the penis and is a swelling of fleshy matter.
And of that, one is malignant and another is not malignant.
If it is not malignant, you should suspend it with a fine thread and excise it until you have completely removed it; and then place on that wound something dipped in Egyptian ointment; and then treat it with diafinicon ointment until it is healed.
And if the phimosis is malignant, of a foul color, then you should apply cautery near its incision and its root.
But if the phimosis is on the prepuce of the head of the uncircumcised penis, and one part of the phimosis is inside the prepuce and the other part is outside, then you should remove the phimosis which is inside first, until it is consolidated; and then you treat the outside part, because if you treat both parts at the same time, it is not certain that the prepuce will not be damaged.
And if you find in the genitals and prepuce blackness and corruption, then you should remove everything that is already blackened and is beginning to corrupt or is already corrupt; and then apply to that place a poultice of honey, mixed with flour from pomegranate rind and crushed and sifted herbs.
And then treat it with another [suitable] treatment until it is healed.
But if there is a flow of blood, then apply lunar cautery according to this method:
Because cautery is beneficial for two conditions at once, that is, for the flow of blood and for the wound when it is corrupted.
And if the head of the penis is corroded and completely destroyed, then you must insert a lead cannula into the meatus of the prepuce so that the patient can urinate through it.
In the adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis (and this adhesion occurs only in one whose prepuce is healthy, and circumcision is not required; and when adhesion occurs due to a wound or abscess) it is necessary that you scrape with a blunt spatula until the adhesion is dissolved and the head of the penis is freed from all parts.
And if it is difficult to separate those exquisitely, then you must scrape with a spatula some part of the head of the penis and of the prepuce, and this is because the prepuce, when it is thin, is easily perforated by its slight thinness.
Then separate the prepuce from the head of the penis with a very thin linen cloth, which has been moistened in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
And then let it be treated with astringent wine until it is consolidated (healed).
Chapter 57.
Concerning the cleansing of children and the treatment of what happens to those circumcised.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds; however, since it is of our operation and our will, and since it is done properly on infants, it is necessary that we assign to it the best operation and the easiest way leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not discuss circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws, and it is only because we have acquired it through experience, which is why I have found most practitioners and barbers use circumcision and cleansing with a lancet and with scissors; and they use ligature with thread and incision with the nail.
And I have tried all these methods and I find no better cleansing with scissors, with ligature with thread, than cleansing with the nail, because many times the skin peels off from above, because the foreskin has two layers, and the lower layer remains, and it is easily forced to another incision and certain pain. And with cleansing which is done with an awl, and is digital, which shoemakers use, the incision of the tip of the penis is not safe, because it easily enters the hole.
But in the operation with a nail, it easily causes suppuration and corrupts your operation. And the foreskin of a child is short by nature, and many times it is born in such a way that it has no need of cleansing, and I have seen this.
But of the cleansing that is done with scissors and ligatures with thread, the goodness, experience reveals to me, because the scissors are of proportional incision, for this reason that the sharpness which is above is the same as the sharpness which is below; and therefore as you press your hand, two sharpnesses are proportioned, and you incise according to a comparison at one time; and compression of the thread is made with a shape containing the foreskin of the penis in every part, with which no error at all is made.
And the manner of the first operation is that you make the child stay still, especially if it is one of those who understands a little, that you do nothing but tie the thread around its penis only, and that you leave it until the next day.
And then make it happy in every way that is possible for you, given its understanding. And then make the child lie still between your hands, prepared, and not fidgeting; and hide the scissors in your hand or under your foot, so that the child does not see them or any other instruments.
And then put your hand to its penis and blow on the foreskin, and pull it back until the head of the penis is exposed, and then cleanse it of whatever dirt is attached to it.
The shape of the scissors for circumcision, this is the shape:
Let one end be non-cutting and the other cutting, so that the nail does not come out, and let the length of the two sharpened parts be equal to the length of two fingers.
But the error in circumcision is that you very quickly remove the whole inner foreskin, or part of it, below the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you extend that [foreskin] with your fingernail before the suppuration of the area and that you incise it evenly.
And if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with forceps and incise it.
But if three days have passed, and what is swollen remains under the inflamed prepuce, then you leave it until the hot abscess subsides, and then carefully incise it and cut it as you see fit.
And be careful of the glans of the penis, for that will not harm him.
And then treat it with that which consolidates the wound, from the powders which we have recommended in the treatise on powders.
And if it is incised beyond measure, and obeys the parts above, that will not harm him.
And moreover, treat it with what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
On the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction due to a stone or congealed blood, or by pus or flesh, and similar to those of which we have spoken of the methods of treatment in the division.
And if it is not dissolved by what we have said and the cause is vehement in the patient, then it is necessary to administer extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be of silver and subtly hollowed like a bird's quill, with the slenderness of a long ray, similar to a palm and a half in length, which has a subtle tube at its tip.
And the manner of drawing out the urine with this is that you take a doubled bladder and tie wool or cotton to its extremity with a good ligature; and introduce the extremities of the bladder into the part below the syringe, and cut with scissors if there is any superfluous wool, but insert it into the cannula with force. Then anoint the syringe with butter and egg white.
And make the patient sit on a chair, and rub their bladder and penis with moist oils, olive oil and rosewater.
Then gently introduce the syringe until it reaches the root of the penis; then bend the penis upwards towards the navel; then push the syringe inside that; and then push it until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels that it has reached the void cause, and this is not done according to this order, but the meatus, through which it goes, is tortuous.
Then slowly extend the bladder with the wool, because the urine will wet the wool.
Then extract it and do it for urine cure, and use the syringe; do not cease doing this and using the syringe until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.
Chapter 59.
Concerning the quality of bladder clysterization with a catheter and with similar instruments suitable for this purpose.
When an ulceration or abscess occurs in the bladder, or blood congeals there, or putrefaction is constricted, and you wish to instill waters and medicines into it, then it should be done with an instrument that is called a catheter, and this is its form:
except that at its extremity it is blunt, that is, closed. And in it are three holes: two on one side, thus arranged.
In the beginning, it is a concave place, in which is that which is pushed in, and it should be according to the quantity that fills it, without addition, except when any substance is drawn from the humidities with it, it should be drawn, and when it is expelled, with that it should be drawn, and when it is expelled lengthwise, as a catheter does with which naphtha is poured in sea battles.
Therefore, when you want to draw out humors from the bladder, insert the end of the catheter into the humor, and draw with it that which is pushed to the upper parts, because the humor is drawn into the concavity of the catheter.
And then insert the end of the catheter into the urethra as we have described in the syringe; and then expel the humor with that which you expel, because that humor arrives at the bladder immediately, and the patient feels it.
The form of the subtle catheter with which the bladder is catheterized, and this is its form:
And let it be made of silver or tin, of which the upper end should resemble a small feather, and below it a slender tube.
Then take a goat's bladder, and place in it the humor that you wish to instill into the bladder, and then tie it tightly between two ends with a double thread, and warm that humor over the fire.
And then insert the end of the catheter into the penis (urethra).
And then squeeze your hand on the bladder with the liquid with gentle pressure until the patient says that the liquid has reached the bladder.

Then tie that to the instrument and proceed with it as you did with the bladder, and express it with your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
On the extraction of a stone.
We have already discussed in the division the types of stone and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between stones formed in the kidneys and stones formed in the bladder, and their symptoms.
And we have discussed the stone in which [one performs] an operation with the hand, and incision.
And the stone properly generated in the bladder is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I am narrating that with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore I say that stones generated in the bladder mostly affect children; and among its symptoms is that the urine that comes from the bladder is similar to water in its thinness, and sand appears in it; and the patient feels a burning sensation in his urethra, and often suffers pain, and is frequently in a bent posture; and with that he often expels feces.
And the healing of children from this is easy until they reach fourteen years; and it is difficult to cure in the elderly. But in young people it is moderate because it is between those [extremes, i.e., easier than in the elderly, harder than in young children].
And of that [disease] in which the stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is the opposite.
Therefore, to arrive at the cure of this, it is necessary at the beginning that we purify the patient with a clyster which extracts all the excrement that is in his bowels; for this excrement sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone during its examination.
Then let the patient be taken with his feet up and shaken and massaged gently so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or that it sometimes leaps from its place.
Then make that [the patient] sit between your legs, prepared, and his hands should be below his thighs, so that the whole bladder is inclined downwards.
And then search for it [the stone] and everything else outside [the bladder].
Then if you feel the stone in the space [bladder], immediately consider making an incision upon it.
And if it does not descend completely from your touch, then it is necessary that you anoint your index finger of your left hand with oil, if the patient is a child, or your middle finger if he is a fully grown youth, and introduce it into his anus. And search for the stone until it is under your finger. And bring it back little by little towards the neck of the bladder.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it outwards towards the place where you will make the incision.
And instruct the assistant to press on the bladder with her hand; and instruct another assistant with her right hand to stretch the genitals upwards, and with her other hand to hold back the skin which is below the genitals, near the place where the incision is to be made.
And then introduce a well-sharpened spatula, of which this is the form: And incise what is between the anus and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be above the stone, and your finger in the anus, and let the incision be transverse, so that the external incision is wide and the internal narrow, according to the size that the extraction of the stone allows, and no larger, because easily by compressing with your finger which is in the anus, the stone will follow the incision, and be expelled without difficulty.
And know that some stones are angular and jagged, which makes their removal difficult because of this.
And of those that are like a gland, and are round, the extraction is easily done.
Therefore, if it is one that has angles and edges, adjust it in the fissure.
And if it is not thus expelled, then you must push on it, or press on it with a suitable instrument, the end of which is like a file with which you press on the stone, and do not let it escape; or introduce under it a slender instrument with a curved end.
But if you cannot [reach/work on] it, then you enlarge the opening a little, and if you see a flow of blood, staunch it by pressing on it with crushed vitriol.
But if the stone is more than one, then at the beginning you place your hand at the opening of the bladder, and then incise it; and then gently push it out; and similarly do if there are more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is unwise to incise it with a large incision, for it causes one of two things to the patient: either death or a continuous dripping of urine, because the place is not completely healed.
But administer its expulsion until it is expelled, or work on the fracture of it with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
Then, when you have completed your operation, fill the wound with shavings of aloe and dragon's blood, and bind it tightly. And place over that a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be cooled.
Then lay the patient supine on their neck, and do not untie the bandage until the third day.
And when you have untied it, wash the area with water and plenty of oil, and then treat it with diachylon ointment and basilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound there occurs a hot, burning, and corrosive abscess, and of a similar appearance to blood frozen in the bladder, and because of it urine is prevented (and this is known by the emission of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains it provokes corruption of the bladder and putrefaction of it.
Then wash the wound with sharp wine, water, and salt, and with a poultice of whatever is suitable for its healing until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the entire treatment that you bind both thighs and adjust the medicines which are placed on the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the meatus of the penis, and is fixed there, and the flow of urine is obstructed, then treat it with what I have recommended before resorting to incision, for many times because of this treatment incision is accused; and I have already proven this, and it is that you take a sharp iron perforator, of which this is the form:
of triangular end, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread, and tie the penis below the stone with it so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the iron into the penis, perforating easily until the perforator reaches the stone. And turn the perforator with your hand on that stone little by little, and you will enlarge the perforation until you make it penetrate from the other side; for the urine will immediately be released.
Then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the outer side of the penis, because it is broken, and it is expelled with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this cure is not suitable for you due to some reason that prohibits it, then tie one thread below the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then incise the stone in that penis between the two ligatures. And then extract it. And then untie the ligature and clean the congealed blood which is formed in the wound.
And the ligature of thread below the stone is not necessary except so that it does not return to the bladder, and the other ligature above only because, when the thread is untied after the extraction of the stone, the stone returns to its place and closes the wound.
And for this reason it is necessary for you, when you tie the thread above, that you elevate the stone to the upper parts, or that it return to its proper place, and close the wound as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of a stone from women.
Stones are rarely generated in women.
But if a stone is found in one of them, then its cure is difficult, and is forbidden in many ways.
One reason is that a woman is easily made a virgin again.
And the second is that you will not find a chaste woman, or one who is married, who willingly reveals herself to the physician.
The third is that you will not find a woman who knows this art well, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is that the place of incision for a stone in women is far from the stone itself, so a deep incision is needed, and there is fear in that.
But if necessity compels you to this, then you must take a female physician who is knowledgeable, and they are rarely found.
But if you are deprived of that, then seek a skilled and subtle physician, or present a matron well-versed in women's matters, or a woman who has some knowledge in this art.
Then present her, and command her to do all that you instruct, from the initial examination for the stone; the reason for which is that if she is a virgin, she should insert her finger into the woman's rectum and search for the stone.
Then, if she finds it, she should grasp it with her finger, and then you should instruct her to incise it.
But if she is not a virgin, then instruct the matron to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, while placing her left hand on the bladder, and compress it with good pressure.
Then, if she finds it, she should gradually draw it out from the opening of the bladder to the lower parts with the sum of her strength until it reaches the root of the thing.
And then incise it near the position of the middle of the vagina below the root of the thing, wherever it is found stopped, and center the stone in that part, and her finger should not be removed from the stone while grasping it.
And the incision should be small at first; and then insert a probe into that incision, and if the stone is felt, then adjust the incision according to the size that you know the stone will be extracted from.
And know that the types of stones are many: for some are small and large, and smooth and rough, and long and round, and having branches.
Therefore you should know how many types there are of them so that you can signify what you desire.
And if you see a flow of blood, apply crushed vitriol to the place and keep it there for an hour until the bleeding stops, and then return to your operation until the stone is removed.
And make sure you prepare with you the instruments which are mentioned in the extraction of stones in men, so that you are aided by them in your operation.
And if you see a flow of blood, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been incised, then place powder on the area and compress it with powders and a descending bandage, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone and do not extract it, for the patient will quickly die. And then treat the wound.
Then, when the sharpness of the bleeding has subsided after a few days, and the area is dry, then you return to your operation until the stone is removed.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision of a strangulated hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but an accumulation of humidity in the white sheath which is below the seam of the genitals containing the testicle, and it is called the sheath.
And sometimes it is in the proper membrane which nature prepared for it in the part of the testicle, so that it is as if there were another testicle; and this is the seam of the genital between the white sheath which we have mentioned, and this is nothing but a later development.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which affects the genitals, so that this matter is poured into them, and sometimes it happens due to a blow inflicted on the genitals.
And this humidity has many colours, for its colour is either inclined to yellow or to blood-red, or red, or it is black and foul-smelling, or it is a clear white, and this is what is most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white sheath which we have mentioned, then the abscess is round, inclining slightly to length, like the shape of an egg, and it surrounds the testicle, for the humidity contains it on all sides.
And if the humidity is in the proper membrane, then the abscess is round near the part of the testicle and for this reason one believes it to be another testicle.
And if the humidity is between the tunic of the genitals and the white sheath, then it falls below the feeling/sensation.
And if you want to know the colour of the humidity, then you test the colour of the humidity in the abscess with a lancet, whose shape precedes the testing; and according to what comes out on the surface of the lancet, judge what is inside.
Therefore, when we come to its cure with iron, then it is necessary that we order the patient to be bled if that is possible for you, and you see his body full (of blood).
And then let him lie supine on his back on something a little raised, and place many cloths under him.
And then sit on his left side, and command the assistant to stand on his right, who will extend his rod to one of the two sides to the tunic of the genitals and to the part of the navel of the belly.
And then take a broad spatula and split the tunic of the genitals in the middle lengthwise almost to the perineum.
And let the incision be made straight and evenly distant from the line that divides the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the included white fatty tissue (cyphax).
And scrape that, and observe, and beware that you do not incise it; and your scraping with which you scrape it, let it be done lightly and subtly, examining with the scraping as much as possible.
And then perforate the water-filled fatty tissue with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
And then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the fatty tissue to the upper parts; and do not touch the tunic of the genitals underneath, and incise the fatty tissue in whatever way is possible for you. And incise it in its entirety or in pieces, and especially its thin layer, for if you do not carefully examine its incision, there is no guarantee that the water will not return.
But if the water flows from outside its tunic during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the fatty tissue, return it inside, and then join the two lips of the tunic of the genitals with a suture, and then care for it like other wounds until it is healed.
And if you find the swelling corrupted by another disease, then it is necessary that you tie the vessels which are on the suspensory ligament, and extract the swelling, and then care for it with what we have said.
But if the water is collected in two parts together, then know that there are two hernias; therefore, incise that part in the same way that you did the other; and if it seems to you that it is one operation, then you do it.
And then you wipe away the blood, and insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose oil; and place on the outside other wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the genitals and the miracle of the belly; and place on top folded cloths and powders, and bind those on top with a bandage which is according to this form:
And then let two cloths be taken and filled with carded wool in this manner; and let the ends be made with which it is bound of cotton or damp wool, and let them be attached with a band to the waistband of the breeches, and one of the ends should reach from the buttocks to the thighs, and the second should reach to the groin area, and two ends should be on the two thighs and two other ends on the two knees, and the whole binding should be fastened in the waistband of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of incision with iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery and that you incise with it the skin of the genitals, and it is ignited as we have recounted until the white cyst containing the water is uncovered, and then you take a cautery of this form: and it resembles a Greek letter.
And then pierce the cyst with that [cautery], and ignite it until all the moisture is expelled.
And then spread the cyst with unguents, and scrape with unguents, and scrape with a thin, ignited cautery; and incise it as much as you can until you eradicate all of it; and be careful that the fire does not touch the egg.
And know that this operation is safer and less prone to blood flow, and better than operation with incision.
But if an abscess causes you difficulty during incision or cauterization, or bleeding, then you must treat whatever difficulty it causes you, with the treatment that is appropriate for it, as has been said.
But if the patient is fearful and does not expect a second operation because of its horror, then administer perforation as I have recommended; and this is when you are certain, because of what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is watery.
Then you should make the patient sit on some raised seat, then perforate the abscess in its lowest part with a broad spatula, and position the perforation according to the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide enough that all the water drains out.
And then place a cloth over the area and bind it, and leave it, so that the remaining water drains out. And then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is an obstruction to the drainage of the water, at that time you perforate it: and this is done only because the white panniculus in the orifice of the wound creates an obstruction and prevents the exit of the water; then you must put into the wound a probe or instrument with which the water is extracted from dropsies, the form of which is described above; or adjust a small opening in the wound's obstruction.
And know that the water will return, and it will be accumulated again within six months or thereabouts; and when it is accumulated, then it should be perforated in the same manner, for the patient will thus spend their days.
Chapter 63.
Concerning the incision of a fleshy hernia and its treatment.
Know that in the incision of this hernia there is a danger leading to death in the most favorable dispositions; therefore, in my opinion, its removal and salvation by that means is better.
And I am recalling the operation on that and the types of abscesses.
