Therefore, I say that abscesses arise in the genitals which are composed of several things, and this is caused by many things: either by an acute, acrid superfluity which is poured into the genitals, or by a blow.
And the color of the abscess follows the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And very quickly is a petrified abscess, of which the color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by a knotting of the arteries, and by inflammation of them, so that its swelling is perceived, or by inflammation of the veins.
But that which is from inflammation of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the abscess when we compress it with our fingers; and it is not fitting that this should be touched.
But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein is not dispersed by any means through compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that an incision is made on this kind and on the other parts; and it is so that you incise the skin of the genitals, and then extend the testicle to the upper parts, and extract it from the white sheath, and free the suspensory ligament, and ligate it, and incise the suspensory ligament after you free it from all parts of the testicle.
Therefore, if the testicle is already consolidated with those inflamed tissues, then you must extract the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between some part of the sheath, or between that which is between the vessels, then you must free all that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh in the place of the adhesion which is behind, then you must incise all that which is there, and extract the testicle as we have said.
Therefore, when your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; and then treat it with other treatments until it is healed.
Chapter 64.
On the treatment of a hernia that is of the scrotum (or, a scrotal hernia).
A scrotal hernia is an abscess enveloped by a certain covering similar to dough, with softening of the testicles, and it makes exercise and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, it is important to remember what preceded it, for fear of what caused it.  (Alternatively:  And the treatment of this illness requires recalling what preceded it, to avoid recurrence.)
However, we must describe the operation in the way the first physicians did.
And I say that you should make the patient sit on a raised chair; and then push the suspensory muscle of the testicles downwards; and then hold the scrotum with your fingers and thumbs, along with the vessels that are proven to be near the penis; and have another assistant help you, and extend these with strong extension.
And then incise with a wide, sharp scalpel, with a transverse incision, to the right of the vessels until the vessels are exposed.
And then, scrape away all the parts as we have told you in the extraction of arrowheads which are in the temples.
And then place in those a needle with double thread, and tie it at the first of the places where you saw blood, and tie it another time at the end of them. And then incise those in the middle place with a straight incision according to the length of the body, and extract what is accumulated of fetid and corrupted humors.
And then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds in which you wish healing to be engendered so that the vessels which nourish one of the two genitalia fall, for it is not harmful on account of that.
But if blood flows from all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed or that the testicles be tied off with the vessels, so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the vessels, and that they are atrophied, or that they dry up so that there is no help with them.
Chapter 65.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture that happens in the peritoneum extended over the abdomen on the side of the inguinal rings of the abdominal muscle, so that the intestines descend from that rupture into one of the sacs of the intestines.
And this rupture is either of the peritoneum or an extension of it, and these two types occur from many causes: percussion, jumping, shouting, lifting something heavy, or similar actions.
And the sign of this, when it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs gradually over a long time, and not suddenly, and it is an inguinal abscess towards the groin because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum, is that at the beginning it causes sudden, intense pain; and it is an abscess appearing differently below the groin, close to the appearance, and this is due to the protrusion of the intestine, and its body in the parts outside the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum is expelled along with the intestine, and this hernia is called an intestinal zirbal hernia.  (Note: "zirbal" appears to be a variant or possibly a mis-spelling related to the type of hernia; a precise modern equivalent is difficult to ascertain without further context.)
And sometimes it is formed with flatulence, and sometimes a tear occurs in the intestine, and it is retained there, and this is a great danger for the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when it is pressed.
And in the treatment of this type of illness with iron (cautery) there is danger, therefore the cauterization should be avoided in that case.
And the method of operating on this is that you instruct the patient to reduce the intestine with his hand to the inside of its cavity if it obeys its return, and then he should be placed on his side between your hands, and raise his legs.
Then hold the spermatic cord, which is the cord of the testicles, upwards, and incise the spermatic cord lengthwise; and then insert into the two lips of the incision hooks, as you would need to open the incision with them.
And if the incision, according to the amount that is possible for you, allows the egg (testicle) to be expelled.
And then scrape away the sheath which is under the spermatic cord, until the white, hard tunic is uncovered in each part.
Then you insert your finger into what is the testicle, into that which is between the white tunic, which is under the cord of the testicle, and between the second tunic, and resolve with this the adhesion that is in the posterior part of the testicle.
Then gently manipulate the spermatic cord upwards with your hand, and with this, stretch the white tunic upwards with your left hand, and elevate the testicle with the tunic to the site of the incision; and instruct the assistant to hold the testicle upwards while you dissolve the adhesions at the back with complete resolution.
And search with your fingers that there is no bowel enveloped in the hard white tunic. But if you find any such thing, then you should push it back into the abdomen below.
And then incise in the part below the spermatic cord, through the incision of which blood and pus will flow, just as we have recounted in what we have said.
Then apply wool soaked in oil and place it on the wound; and apply the ligature which we have recounted (and sometimes the white tunic is cauterized, which we have said is done near the incision to prevent blood flow, because this often occurs) and leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be bathed with warm water, and place it on until it falls off as we have said.
Then treat the wound with the other treatment for wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Of a ventosity hernia.  (Or: Concerning a windy hernia)
In this hernia, I have not seen anyone who has dared to treat it with iron.
And the first [doctors] said that it is done as we have recounted in the hernia that is with wine; which is that you tie the vessels near the incision on that [hernia] with ease; and then dry the middle place around the abscess with something that opens it, until the vessels fall [away/collapse].
And cure the wound as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the disruption that is made in the inguinal region.
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the groin as we have said, and it swells the area.
And do not allow the genitalia to be a cause of the intestines in the genitalia; and if they do, it is little; and it returns at all times.  (This is a difficult sentence, the meaning is somewhat unclear due to archaic phrasing and possible medical terminology that is now obsolete.  A possible more modern interpretation is:  And do not allow the intestines to protrude into the scrotum (or genitalia); and if some do, it is a minor protrusion; and it returns at all times (or frequently).)
But if the time with this is prolonged, a fissure is made in the inguinal canal until the intestine descends into the inguinal canal; and this happens because of the extension of the inguinal canal, which is in the groin as we have said, which is the cause because the inguinal canal is stretched, and then it is softened, and the area is suppurated and swollen.
And its cure is with cautery, as its description has preceded.
And sometimes it is cured with iron according to this manner: and it is that the patient lies on his back between your hands, and then dry the area of the rupture of the swollen abscesses with incision across the width according to the quantity of three fingers.
And then tighten the white inguinal canal that is under the testicle until it is uncovered (or revealed).
And then take the ray (presumably a surgical instrument) and place that on the prominent area of the inguinal canal, and press that to the front of the abdomen.
And then sew the two prominent places on the end of the inguinal canal's ray (instrument), and join one of those to the other with a suture.
And then remove the end of the ray (instrument), and do not incise the inguinal canal completely, nor extend the wound, nor any other cause, as I will let you know in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat that with the treatment for wounds, and as you incise the fibers, clean it and repair the wound until it is consolidated; because the inguinal canal is contracted and the prominence of the rupture is not set (or reduced).
And cautery is better in this location, because it is closer to health (i.e., promotes healing).
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the scrotum.
Many times the scrotum in some men is softened because the flesh that is between them is softened, and its appearance is damaged.
Then it is necessary for the one who is charged with its cure to make the patient lie on his back, and to sew up all the scrotum which is softened over the natural scrotum; and then to join the two lips with a suture.
And if you wish, sew in the beginning the superfluity of the softened scrotum with three or four stitches and with a firm suture.
And then incise between the sutures; and then treat it with the treatment for other wounds until it is healed and the threads fall out.
Chapter 69.
Concerning castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law, but because it is fitting for me to mention it in this book, and its mention is necessary only in two ways: one is so that it is in the knowledge of the physician when questioned about it, that he knows the treatment for one who has suffered it; and the other way is that we often have need of the castration of other animals for our benefit from them, such as horses, goats, pigs, and similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing, or by incision.
But in that which is done by crushing, the method of operation is that we cause the animal to be immersed in hot water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. And then take them with your hand until they are resolved and do not appear under the touch.
But in castration done by incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the scrotum of its testicles with your left hand.
And then tie the suspensory ligament, and incise over each testicle with an incision until both testicles protrude.
And then incise them after you have skinned each one. And do not put any dressing on those of the cyphus, except the fine cyphus which is on the vessels.
And this manner of castration is better which is done by crushing, because strong crushing leaves no cause of the testicles whereby the beast desires copulation.
And then care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men is of two kinds: one is where it appears that there is a space in the scrotum, in that which is between two testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva in which there is hair.
And sometimes urine flows from that which is in the scrotum.
But in women there is one type: and it is that the vulva above the mons pubis is like two testicles of a man, somewhat smaller on the outside part, like a man's prepuce, and two testicles.
And the cure is of three types: two for men and one for women. And it is necessary that you incise those adhering flesh until the impression of it disappears, and then you should treat those with the cure for other wounds.
And in the second type in men, from which urine flows that is in the scrotum, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Of swelling and prominent flesh due to causes in women.
A slight swelling is usually caused by natural causes until it is pressed and examined; and sometimes it is magnified in some women to such an extent that it spreads as in men and is visible during intercourse.
Then it is necessary that you hold the excess swelling with your hand or with a bandage and incise it.
And do not go too deep in the incision, especially near the root, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
Then cure it with the treatment for wounds until it is healed.
But a fleshy growth is flesh that grows in the womb of the mother until it fills it, and it quickly comes out of the parts outside like a tail (and because of this some first called this "tailed disease"); therefore we must incise it as we incise a swelling, and cure it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
On the treatment of sciatica.
Sciatica is when the vulva of the woman is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to a preceding illness, and that is caused either by improperly formed flesh or by a thin or thick membrane.
And it is formed either in the front of the vulva, or on its sides, or in its upper part, or in its lower part, and it prohibits coitus and conception and childbirth, and strongly inhibits menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by examination of the vulva; if the obstruction is apparent, it is straightforward; but if it is not apparent, then you should search for it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin membrane, and is close to the two labia, then you should incise and break it up.
And you should place powders on the two labia, and then place your index fingers of both hands together.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs spread, and then spread the two labia forcefully until that thin membrane is broken and the obstruction is opened.
And then take wool and soak it in oil and place it on the area, and keep it moist daily so that the incised area does not consolidate (heal too quickly).
But if the membrane is thick and dense, then you should incise it with a broad spatula or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to an abscess, then suspend it with a hook, and incise it.
And have with you medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and  plantain (enses), mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not close up too quickly.
And keep it there for some days and administer a decoction of dry herbs.
And then it should be treated with the other consolidation cure until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the mother's birth of other flesh [likely referring to a difficult childbirth or postpartum complication], and then it is necessary to incise according to this manner. But if this is due to a cranial abscess, to the cranial abscess which is in the mother, no harm should be done with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids and of a boil and of a red tumor that occurs in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the openings of many veins from which much blood continuously flows; and when they are old hemorrhoids and boils are formed.
And I have already mentioned in the division the types of these and their signs, and I will say thus which ones receive treatment and which ones do not receive treatment.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids and boils, when they are in the womb of the mother and do not appear externally, then in those cases no treatment should be done with iron (cautery).
But those which are at the orifice of the womb, upon which sensation falls (i.e., which are externally palpable), those are the ones that are cured.
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a cold place and then extend the boil with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and incise them at their roots.
And then sprinkle on those (hemorrhoids/boils) to stop the flow of blood, one of the incisive powders without biting (i.e., causing further irritation), such as acacia and similar things.
And then bathe her feet for an hour, and then place them in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then have her sit in a decoction of pomegranate leaves and bark, and walnuts, and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a plaster with this and apply it to his back.
And if the blood is stopped, it is good; and if not, apply two cupping glasses, to the breasts without scarification.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice, or in plantain juice, or in rue juice with old wine, and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointment until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of the rectum occurring in the mother.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the mother, such as cancer, and stony abscesses, and ulcerative abscesses, and  abscesses (possibly meaning buboes or similar), and herpes, and hemorrhoidal fistulas, and fissures, and boils, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these diseases and their types and their signs, and their cures, in the previous section.
And it is necessary that I speak in this treatise of a hot abscess which occurs in the mother, when it is among the abscesses that collect pus, and if you wish to know in what manner the operation is performed with iron.
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute with throbbing and phlegm and fever, and sometimes it reddens and if you feel it, then do not attempt perforation, and treat it with what assists maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms subsides.
And then you must make the woman sit on a stool that has two legs; and she should recline on her back and adjust her feet to the lower part of her belly, and her thighs should be spread, and place her arms under her knees, and tie each one with a suitable bandage to this.
And then the midwife follows from the right side and administers the instrument with which the orifice of the womb is opened, the form of which will be shown in those things that will be done next.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument that you place with it the neck of the woman's womb, and that which of the instrument is entered into the womb should not be more than the neck of the womb and hurt the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the neck of the womb, then it is necessary that you place powders on the texture of the neck of the womb so that the entry of any instrument into the neck of the womb is prevented.
And it is necessary that you place the turning instrument on the upper side and that the person administering it holds the instrument and turns the instrument until the orifice of the womb is opened.
And when the wound appears, and is touched with the hand and is soft and moist, then it is necessary that you dry that with a linen cloth from the head downwards.
And when the blood has been evacuated, then it is necessary that you place on the wound a piece of cloth soaked in rose oil or green oil in which there is some astringency; and place the caul on the section in the neck of the womb.
And place outside the womb and on the vulva clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then let it be treated for about two days with an ointment which is suitable for this, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed in such a way that it is rinsed with water and honey, and then with water in which the root of lilies or of long birthwort has been boiled, and then returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is located inside the closed womb, then you must be prohibited from treating it with iron,  taking care of it with what we have said in the previous section.
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives in what manner live embryos should be treated when they are expelled in an unnatural form.
It is necessary that the midwife know at the beginning the form of natural childbirth by its signs, and it is that when you see the woman straining in the lower parts and desires to smell the air, and because of that which is upon her during childbirth, she hastens the expulsion of the embryo, then you should know that this birth is according to the natural form, and it is the expulsion with its head first, and the afterbirth with that or suspended or hanging from the umbilical cord.
Therefore, when you see those signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends easily because when it descends headfirst, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed of those superfluities with good purification.
But that which is other than this birth is unnatural and unfavorable, for when the embryo is expelled feet first and its body is expelled before its head and feet, and one of its hands, or its foot, or its head and hand, or its belly, and quickly it is turned upon its neck, and similar to those unfavorable forms.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and skillful in all these matters, and to guard against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what skill is needed in them so that it may be signified by this and may be established upon this.
When the fetus is expelled headfirst by natural exertion, and this is strenuous for the woman and the birth is difficult, and sometimes her strength is already weakened, then have her sit, or have the women hold her, and evaporate her vagina with cooked fenugreek and moist oils.
Then the midwife inserts a small, thin spatula between her fingers and tears the secundines with it, or scrapes it with her fingernail until she removes the moisture from it. And press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman should be irrigated with a mucilage of ptils and a mucilage of fenugreek with a syringe.
Then instruct the woman, during the irrigation, to be compressed and make her sneeze with a sternutatory; and hold her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
And if the two hands of the fetus are expelled at the beginning, then you should reduce them carefully, little by little. But if it does not obey the treatment, then place the woman on a stool and raise her legs, and then shake the stool on the ground; and the woman should be held so that she does not fall from the shaking.
And if the hands are not returned and the fetus dies, then incise them and extract the remains of the fetus, or tie the body to its hand with a cloth and extract it gently, for it will be expelled.
When the fetus is positioned on its feet, then you should push them upwards, and gently turn the fetus, and gently grasp it, and gently take one foot and extend it little by little.
But when it has come out between the thighs, then you command her to strain, and make her sneeze with a concoction, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled with what we have recounted, then you return the fetus little by little until you place it in its natural position, for then it will be expelled with ease.
And if it is hindered by all that we have recounted, then take marshmallow mucilage and dissolved gum arabic, and pound it all well in a mortar, and gently apply this to the woman's vulva, at the lowest part of her abdomen. And gently make her bathe in lukewarm water, up to her hypochondria (the region below the ribs).
And when you see that the lower parts of her are softened, then make a fumigation of myrtle for her and place it under her. And when she has had the fumigation for an hour, then make her sit on a seat, and gently make her sneeze and hold her mouth, and lift her up, and press the lower part of her abdomen with gentle compression, for the fetus will immediately be expelled.
Thus [support] the fetus laterally, holding one of its arms. Try to reduce [the dislocation] with your hand; but if you cannot reduce it, then lift the woman and make her walk.
And if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her hips with a vehement shaking, and then administer bandages which I have recommended, made of mucilage.
And if the labor has begun, then make her sit on a stool, and then work on the reduction of the arm, and grasp the fetus according to its natural position, and make the woman sneeze and command her to strain until it is expelled.
Thus [support] the fetus on the neck, spreading your hands, and its face is towards its mother. Let the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then turn it little by little, and let that happen easily.
And when it is grasped, then place on the neck of the child that which we have recommended of oil and mucilages.
And then command her to cough little by little, and when you have done that, then make her sit on a stool and command her to be inclined on her right side, and then command her to strain; and make her sneeze with a [sneezing] potion. The prepared fetus exits on the side.
When the horror of the mother decreases according to this manner, consider, and then you incise the secundine with your fingernail until it is evacuated of its moisture.
And if the fetus is suspended over its face and neck, then you incise it at the umbilicus, so that it may not be retained and die.
And then push the fetus into the inner parts until you place it as needed, and instruct the woman to strain several times, and it will be expelled easily in this way, if God wills it.
The exit of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four are born, but this is rare and seldom happens; but there are natural causes for it, and they do not live.
And count those, even if they are multiplied, as one birth, and they are separated by a membrane; distinguish them by the umbilical cord of each of them. And sometimes they are expelled as if it were one fetus by easy natural expulsion, and sometimes they are expelled by an undesirable expulsion as we have recounted.
And if some of them are difficult to deliver during childbirth and reach the point of what we have said about unnatural presentations, then you treat them as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and skill, and with patience, and is ingenious according to each presentation, as she is prepared by her intelligence leading to health.
Concerning that which is formed in the womb from embryos and is aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten are formed in the mother, and it has been verified by me that one woman aborted and expelled six, and another ten, all formed, which cause was explained by the measurement of glorious wise men.
And all this must be in the knowledge of the midwife, because it easily appears similar to that, and therefore oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
On the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat a dead fetus with what we have said in the division of medication and it is not expelled, and you are forced to use a hand operation, then you must consider if the woman is of sound constitution and is not afflicted by any of the accidents that cause danger to her.
Then the woman should be placed on the bed on her side, her head inclined downwards and her thighs raised.
And the attendant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mother's vulva with warm oil and with a mucilage of mallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then let the midwife anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the location where the umbilical cord is attached to the fetus.
And convenient places for this: then you consider if the fetus is positioned to descend head-first, and then you grasp the umbilical cord at its eyes, or at the neck, or at the mouth, or at the palate, or under the chin, or at the clavicle, or in the appropriate places near the ribs, or under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is feet-first, then at the perineum, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary to hold the umbilical cord with the right hand, and place its curvature in the space between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and grasp the cord in one of those places which we have mentioned, until reaching the empty cavity.
And then place and attach another umbilical cord, or a third if necessary, so that the traction is even, and there is not extension only along its straight length.
Rather, it should be manipulated in every part, so that its expulsion is eased, just as is done in tooth extraction.
And it is necessary in this [context] that the extension be softened; and if some part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife lubricate some of her fingers with oil and expertly insert them and manipulate with them that which is retained.
Then, if the part of the fetus is expelled, then the cords should be moved to a higher position; and do this until the whole fetus is expelled.
But if the hand emerges before the other, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then extract it until all is expelled, and incise it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus you should proceed with the other hand and with the two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or a constriction occurs during its expulsion, or there is accumulated water on its head, then it is necessary to insert between the fingers expectorant saliva and split the head and extract the water, or to open it with an instrument which is named “alundat”, the form of which will be described with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is near this one.
And similarly proceed if the fetus has a naturally large head: and then it is necessary to incise it with a scalpel, or to grasp it with forceps as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is expelled but constricted below the clavicle, then it should be incised until the moisture which is in the feet is drained.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it obey expulsion, then you should incise the head immediately as much as is possible for you.
And if the lower part of the belly is abscessed or dropsical, then it is necessary that you perforate it until the moisture that is within it runs out.
But if the fetus is on its feet, then its traction is easy and its direction to the mother's mouth is easy.
But if it is constricted around the belly and feet, then it is necessary that you extract it with a cloth which you will have wrapped around your hand, and that you incise the belly and feet until that which is within them flows out.
But if the other limbs are obstructed and the head returns, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mouth of the mother is open, then she inserts her hand into the womb, and searches with it for the head, and draws it with her fingers towards the mouth of the mother, and then inserts into it one or two fingers, and draws it out with them.
And if the mouth of the mother is constricted by a hot abscess which affects her, then it is necessary that it not be disturbed; beforehand it is necessary to administer an infusion of moist, oily substances. And administer plasters; and bathe the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, then, if possible, it should be turned; and that which we have said for a living fetus should be administered.
And if that is not possible, then let it be incised in pieces, and extracted.
And it is necessary that you leave no part of the afterbirth inside.
And when you have completed all your work, then administer what we have said concerning the treatment of the hot abscesses which may occur in the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then bathe in styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb there was a dead fetus, and shortly after that fire [presumably meaning pregnancy] again she became pregnant, and again the fetus died. It happened to him that for a long time there was an abscess in his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and it began to discharge pus.
And I was called to his care, and I treated him for a long time, and the wound has not healed.
Then I applied to him plasters of strong attraction, and I extracted a bone from him; and after a few days passed, I extracted another bone; and I was very amazed, since there should not be bones in the abdomen.
And then I considered that they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I cleansed the wound, and extracted many bones from him, the woman being in good condition, and she lived in this way for a long time, with a small amount of pus being discharged from her.
And I did not consider this to be strange, but because in this there is skill and help, that the skillful physician administers with his hand for healing.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of incision with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a constrictor in which the books are bound, which binding has two incisions at the two ends of two sticks. However, this incision should be finer than the constrictor, and be made of ivory or boxwood.
And let the width of each stick be about two fingers, and the thickness of each about one finger, and the length of each is a palm and a half; and in the middle space between the two sticks are two fastenings of the same wood which are already fixed, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the mother so that she is opened with them when you turn the two aforementioned incisions.
Another form similar to that one, but more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of ivory or boxwood, in the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity are two additions, thus shaped like V's, and the length of these additions is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you wish to open the mother with them, then you make the woman lie down on the bed with her legs hanging down, having opened that which is between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, just as you do with forceps, gradually according to the amount you want to widen the opening of the mother's womb, until the womb does what you want.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:
The shape of the instrument with which the fetus is expelled:
The shape of the instrument with which a large head is grasped.  (The "2" likely represents a superscript or annotation in the original text, perhaps indicating a variant or clarification.)
And it resembles forceps which have teeth at the end, like tongs, and sometimes it is made long like forceps according to this shape, like tongs, which have teeth like saw teeth, with which it is incised and grasped. The shape of a small instrument:
The shape of a hook having a spine (or point).
The shape of a hook having two spines (or points).
The shape of a spatula having two ends with which the head is severed (or cut).
The shape of two broad spatulas.
All those instruments are exercised, and when the types of those are more multiplied and prepared by the artisans, the operation is easier and more valued by men because of its quantity (or abundance).
And do not neglect those instruments, whatever they may be, that you have prepared, for the necessity of them is not excusable.
Of the extraction of the secondine (afterbirth).
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should instruct the patient to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze forcefully.  Squeeze your hand over her mouth and nostrils, and if it is expelled by this means, it is good.
And if not, then take a pot and pierce a hole in its lid. Place herbs inside, opening the mother's mouth [to inhale the steam], such as rue,  mint, chamomile, dried arabic gum, cassia lignea, and holy thistle (centaury). All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the pot, and place that over the fire.
And then place a reed cannula in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's womb.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains ineffective and it is not expelled, then instruct the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or mallow poultice, and then introduce it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it.  And when she has found it, then take it and extract it little by little.  But if it is attached to the womb of the mother, then she should insert her hand as we have recommended, and when she finds the afterbirth, extract it little by little as instructed, so that the mother does not faint from the violent traction. Afterwards, it should be moved to the right and left sides easily.
And if the mother's mouth (cervix) is constricted, we have already recommended treatment with sneezing and medication with herbal oil.
But if it is not expelled by all the methods we have recommended, then be careful not to tire yourself with repeated attempts at extraction. However, what has emerged from the mother's womb, attached to the woman's genitalia, should be cauterized with ointment and tetrapharmacon; for this will cause it to rot within a few days, and it will dissolve and be expelled.
But when it rots, a foul odor rises to the head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and then fumigations suitable for this should be administered.
But some of the Ancients already tried fumigation with henbane and dried figs.
Method of instrumentation with which a woman is fumigated because of retained menses and afterbirth:
Let it be made of glass or brass.
And let the thin end be placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation placed on the embers of the fire.
And let her hold that until that fumigation is consumed and then another.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus in a child.
Many children are born and their anuses are not perforated, which are very narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that narrow passage with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the rectum.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil on it; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And sometimes anal obstruction occurs due to the consolidation of a wound or abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then it should be treated with what we have said about the treatment and the lead [likely referring to lead-based ointments].
Concerning the treatment of fistulas that develop below [likely referring to anal fistulas].
The fistulas that develop in the lower part are knotty and thick, which develop near the anus externally or in the space between the parts.
And it is one fistula or several.
And when that knot is old and discharges a watery white moisture or subtle pus, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known because something comes out of them through emission and by flatulence; and sometimes the patient suffers from emission, and worms readily come out of them.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the space, entering the bladder and into the meatus of the penis; and some penetrate into the joint of the thigh and into the buttock.
And what distinguishes a fistula penetrating into the anus from one that is not penetrating, is that you insert your finger into the anus, and insert a thin probe of bronze or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you do not feel it at all, nor does any matter of emission, worms, or flatulence proceed from the opening, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then attend to its cure.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the meatus of the penis, then its indication is the presence of urine from it and the prohibition of it so that the place may be consolidated with medicine.
But if it penetrates into the joint of the hip or into the bone and causes pain in the circumference, then its sign is that the probe penetrates there if there is no tortuosity in the bone, and pain in the circumference of the part; and that pus always issues from it; and that medication is of no use, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all, and the cure of those is the labor and vanity of foolish physicians, on which they are fixed.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is hoped for, according to what I shall recommend to you, and this I have already proven.
And it is that the patient lie between your hands on his back and raise his legs upwards, his thighs being inclined towards his belly, and then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.
And if the patient feels it around the anus, then it is necessary that you introduce your finger into the anus.
And if you feel with your finger the probe already discovered by itself penetrating, besides what you feel between your finger and that sac or flesh, then you know certainly that it is penetrating; and then do not work on that, for there is no healing in that, as we have said.
However, some say that it is healed in some men late.
And of the medication by which help is hoped for him is that you ignite a fine cautery according to the width of the fistula, as has been previously described, and introduce that ignited cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those fleshy parts attached to the fistula, which resemble the quill of a bird's feather, are cauterized.
And then treat that with poultices wrapped in linen until all those cauterized tissues slough off, and then treat that with flesh-generating and consolidating medicines, and if it is healed, good; and if not, then it is not healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, and does not penetrate beyond your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a thick layer of flesh or a sac, and you see the fistula in such a way that it is the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will cut with the probe, and it will be in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the end of the probe arrives, and through the probe it is freed and exits.
And then cleanse those fistulous tissues which resemble the quill of a bird's feather and do not leave any of them behind.
But if you see a flow of blood come between you and your operation when you cauterize those tissues, then the best and easiest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire achieves two good things: that is, cauterization of those attached tissues, and the stemming of the blood and drying of the humors.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen or crushed flax until the place is free of pus, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate hard flesh and promote healing.
And these are medicines in which there is stypticity and drying; when it is restored, it is healed and you do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula penetrates to the rectum and is distant from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and explore it with that.
And so if you feel the fistula and between you and it there is a covering of fat or flesh, and it is suitable for penetration, then there is no operation there except by way of trust and hope.
Which requires one of three methods: either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you reach its depth so that you can insert the poultices and the appropriate treatment; and do not proceed too much with incision lest you cut the muscle containing the rectum, and cause harm to the patient and thus death without his consent; and then treat it with what we have said, for it will be healed quickly; or lengthen that covering and clean it of pus after drying it near the rectum, as we have said, either with a probe or another instrument with a sharp tip, and then clean the fistulous flesh which is in the incision as far as you can with scraping; and then strive to effect the restoration of the entire wound with incision, and if the orifice above is not consolidated, and the fistula remains open in the part of the rectum, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula ends in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the side of the rectum near the surface of the body with the bend and at the margin of the rectum, then take a probe with a perforated tip, which is like a needle for preparing skins in this manner:
and insert it into that [fistula] wrapped in five threads or around them; and then insert a probe with thread into the fistula until you reach its depth.
But if it penetrates into the margin of the rectum inwards, then you pull the thread from that hole so that you insert your finger into the rectum; and pull out the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each, and leave it for one or two days, and when you cut the thread into the flesh, tighten it well until those parts of flesh are cut which are between the two ends of the thread and fall away.
And then treat the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way is prepared for you, except that it should be very gently, because it is not for you to do this so that you do not cut the muscle; and then treat it with this until it is healed.
The form of the pointed probe with which the fistula is probed: its curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that you do not cut what is unnecessary, as sometimes happens.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows during their incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, for either they are between the buttocks similar to charcoal, or they are like bunches of grapes. And of these, some are small and some are large, and blood flows from them constantly and it is one, and there are many, and they are outside the anus and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are, according to the months, of little humidity, from which flows a citrine liquid or a little blood with continuous discharge, and [the discharge is] according to the complexion of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you command the patient to wash himself and to strain until the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you should carefully lift and suspend them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail, and then incise them at their roots.
But if the hooks cannot hold them because of their humidity and softness, then grasp them with rough cloth or cotton or a pledget of wool, and extract them with your fingers, and then incise them and sprinkle on them some hot powders at the incision, so that they act in place of a cautery; or cauterize them as is described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatments until they are healed.
And if the anus does not readily allow for protrusion, then  stimulate the patient with a small, mildly irritating enema, and let it be washed with that water which is in the anus, so that it may slightly protrude when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and it is that you should grasp them with your fingernail, or grasp them with a hook, and incise them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And that which you have incised, you must suture in this manner: take waxed thread and insert it into a needle and then pull the hemorrhoids upward from the parts below, and make it penetrate with the needle to the root on the other side, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lowest part around the needle, and this should be done horizontally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a firm ligature, and then tie off the thread and remove the needle.
And do this to all of them, and leave one of them uncut, so that the excess blood may flow from it.
And then place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerate; and place that upon the anus and command the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat it with ointment or another cure until it is healed.
And since they are old and that medicine is not effective, then you must scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are moist and the outer scab is removed, which prevents their consolidation; and then treat them until they are healed.
Then remove them with a more forceful scraping than the first, until they bleed much and are excoriated; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that a felon, that is, a whitlow, is nothing but a red, round swelling, similar in color to the body, resembling the head of a pin.  It is found throughout the body, but most often in the parts below the feet and fingers, and causes pain according to its growth.
And then it is necessary that what is in the circumference of the whitlow be incised, and held with forceps or a hook, and eradicated from its root; and then treat that.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is described in its chapter.
Moreover, a wart is a small, rough, fistulated, round eminence on the surface of the body, and often appears on the hands of children.
And its cure is that you stretch and incise it, or that you bind it tightly with thread or a hair so that it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize that, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine (caustic substance).
Moreover, a mole is a small, hairy or fistulated, thick eminence on the surface of the body, often appearing on the face; and when friction encounters it, a sensation similar to the bite of an ant is felt, and it is found all over the body.
And its cure is that you take a cannula of goose or vulture quill, and let it be strong, and place that over the mole so that the circle of the cannula contains its parts; and then rotate your hand with the cannula so that you incise what is in the circumference of the mole, and place it in the depth of the flesh, and eradicate the mole from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a brass or iron cannula, according to this method:
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn out to a thinness, blunt (that is, non-perforated), covered so that the sensation of it on the finger is dulled and its rotation facilitated.
And if you wish, cauterize the mole as previously described in the cauterization of a boil in the chapter on cautery.
Concerning the instruments which are used to give a clyster (enema).
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or of tin, or of molten or hammered brass; and sometimes it is made of these instruments, small and large, according to those to whom they are administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose anuses are fragile or painful, the clysters for them should be small.
The form of a large and a small clyster:
The head of the cannula above, to which the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner; and let there be a distinction where the bag is tied, thus as you see.
And the lower extremity, which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt (that is, not open) very smooth, tending towards subtlety; and on one side of it let there be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and let the width of the hole be according to the thickness of the fingers and a little larger.
And let the bag in which the medicines are placed be of animal bladder or sheepskin, and its length be one palm [width].
And let it be perforated with many holes, so that it is perforated; and let the space which is between the holes be the thickness of a finger.
And then insert into those holes strong threads of raw silk, about ten threads, or thereabouts; and fasten the bag with them; and when the bag is placed in position, bind it tightly at the top of the clyster with two distinctions with strong constriction, and then cauterize each hole with that: But the method of cauterizing with this is that you fill the bag with the medicine, which should be heated according to the patient's tolerance; and take care that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
And then let the patient lie supine on his back, and raise his legs and place under him a cushion on which there are already cloths or a sheet; and then lubricate the end of the clyster with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or similar; and then insert it into the anus easily, and then the attendant takes the bag and squeezes it with two hands and with force until all that is in the clyster is evacuated into the bowel; and then remove the clyster and the patient lowers his legs.
And if the medicine forces him thus, let him keep it as long as he can; and if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.
But if you use this clyster in cases of diarrhea and the evacuation bothers him, then you repeat the clyster until he is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very thin and delicate, like those with which infants are clystered.
Concerning the treatment of wounds.
I have already said in the division on wound treatment what was appropriate for the book, and I say thus the treatment of those wounds with hand and with medicines according to their nature.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to what causes the wounds and according to the location where the wounds occur.
And the causes by which wounds are often made are such as a stone throw, or incision by a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or with a club, or with an arrow, or similar to those from many causes.
But the wounds that are in the various locations of the body are such as a wound that falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the chest, and similar to those of other limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of certain wounds so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, for the discussion of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from a stone throw or similar, and only the skin is wounded, and it is a large wound, and you fear the occurrence of a hot abscess in that patient, then immediately bleed him and do not delay; and extract blood from him according to his strength unless some cause prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that has issued from the wound is little.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side from the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and from heavy foods.
But if some cause prohibits bloodletting, then let his food be lessened, and let him not approach meat nor wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs in him, a pledget soaked in plain rose oil or in wine in which something styptic has been dissolved.
But if it is certain that there is a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is recent, its own blood and the powder which I shall shortly describe, if the air has not changed it.
And if the air has already changed it, then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large, or is from a sword cut or similar, and its lips are not joined together by themselves, then join them with sutures according to what I shall describe concerning sutures.
But if the wound has already laid bare the bone and hangs, and there is no retention of it but a little suspension, then incise it and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh properly in place of the bone, and order the patient to be fed on foods which contain thickness: heads and strong broths and the like, until it is composed.
And if you find putrefaction in the bone and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that which is rotten and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood can occur, and it is not stopped by medicines, then you should investigate the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you incise it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity compels you that what we have said does not suffice, then you cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is light; thus you should dust these with this powder, for it consolidates them before the air changes them.
And the manner of the powder is this: take oil of roses one part, dragon's blood two parts, unquenched lime three parts; let all be well ground and sifted, and with it let the wound be filled, and be bound with a good bandage until it adheres well to it, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it above with powders and leave it, and do not unbind it while it is thus, that it is not festering, for the medicine is not eradicated from it until it is healed.
And sometimes this alone suffices, when you do not have roses and dragon's blood at hand.
And do this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary that it be placed on them and they are fresh, and recent wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially when it is in summer time, then this powder is not intended for application, but rather treat those with what we have said,  so that you apply to them some digestive ointments, or that you apply to them powders of barley flour with water and honey until they purge the pus; and then treat them with the other cure until they are healed.
But if a fracture in a bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then you extract it with tweezers.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of the wounds of the neck.
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in medication between it and a wound of the head when it is simple.
But if the nerves of the neck or an artery are incised, then if it is a nerve, there is no other way than to avoid treating it with something that causes strong stypticity, such as vitriol and things similar to it, because it harms the nerves and contracts them. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold, and their continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is cured with light medicines, such as being washed with sweet water, sometimes infused with rose oil or non-rose oil, and  honey when washed with sweet water and other healing ointments according to this manner, and moist unguents.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or draw together its lips, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or an abscess forms in the lowest part of it, then perforate it in the most depressed place.
But if an artery has already been incised in the wound and a flow of blood results, then separate or cut it, or tie it, or cauterize it if necessity compels you to do so.
But if you have already incised the vertebrae of the throat or all, and the veins of the neck are saved, then you adjust the edges of the wound with sutures over the neck's bone and do not touch that; first equalize it and restore it to its natural shape, and then tighten it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for some days; and then treat it with ointments which have astringent and stimulating properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has caused damage to the bone, then clean it, and then if there are bone fragments in it, extract all those that you can; and what you are not able to remove, leave it until the wound suppurates, and is separated and the damaged area is relieved.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle on it the powder which has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with that of which the recollection will come later in its chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider further if there is a rush of air from the wound when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is recent with its own blood, then you apply powder to it in the first hour, and do not bind it tightly so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but apply an attractive ointment, and loosen it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not at hand, then you place old linen on the opening of the wound so that it draws out the humidity; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that what is contained therein may flow out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and it has not caused the patient spasms or tremors or a bad heart or stricture of the throat, and you see his other conditions are good, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with cauterization and other treatments until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it remains open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from an incision of a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then treat it as previously described for suturing, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has caused damage to the bone, and pieces have been detached, then you cleanse the wound and attempt to extract those pieces, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound becomes suppurative, so that their extraction is easier.
And for other accidental wounds in other limbs, the treatment is similar to what we have described.
Of wounds of the abdomen.
The indisposition which occurs in the abdomen is either great, or small, or moderate.
And a small rupture is made difficult in another way, insomuch that their reduction is more difficult if you don't take care of their replacement immediately, because it opens and swells, making its introduction difficult.
Therefore, the best rupture is moderate, because in that case the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in the two previously mentioned types.
And know that the suturing of the abdomen is according to four methods, but two sutures are common that are also suitable for sutures of other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures peculiar to the abdomen are also used for other wounds, but however, they are more proper in the suturing of the abdomen.
And I recount to you each one with explanation and clarification.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has emerged through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either because of the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore when this is so, then it is necessary that you warm it in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone, or that you boil in water with squill, cyperus, and spikenard, and bathe the intestine with this until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes it is done with a liquid that has astringent properties, and it is better than water alone in resolving inflammation.
And so, when you see the inflammation to be resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which mallow and marshmallow have been boiled, for by this the entry of it is relieved with little effort.
But if the re-entry is hindered despite this treatment, then incise the rupture a little with an instrument with which haemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is appropriate that there is no incision, and there is no need for drying and weakening.
Regarding the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not sharp, and the end is thin, and not with the thinness of a needle, rather it should be a little blunt.
And thus, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine protrudes, then its reduction should follow its natural form, to its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
But when the rupture is large, and is in the lower part of the belly, then you should make the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you should raise their head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the belly, then always direct your attention and consideration to ensuring that the side where the wound is is higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this treatment in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, apply what you have prepared, and then have your assistant, with gentle hands, hold the entire rupture with their hand and adjust its lips, and then prevent anything else from entering the suture.
And this is the common method of suturing:  first, take a needle, or several needles according to the size of the wound; then, leaving the width of an annular finger from the edge of the rupture,  thread the needle, ensuring that you do not knot the two ends of the thread, using two ends of a small piece of material (or a smaller thread) beneath the thread's ends, until you have passed it through that part; and you have now joined two ends of the thread and two ends of this small piece of material, making four points of attachment.
Then tighten with a double knot around the needle, sometimes from both sides together, and then approximate the two lips of the wound with a decent approximation; then leave the width of a finger and insert another needle into the wound, and tie this one with thread in the same way as the first needle; and continue doing this with needles as needed until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
And let the needles be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin, for thin needles cut the flesh too easily; and thick ones are difficult to thread, therefore they should be of medium thickness.
And the needle should not be inserted too deeply into the two ends of the thread, lest the flesh be too easily cut and the wound reopen before it heals.
And the sutures should not be too long, lest the wound be hindered from healing.
Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient during their sleep; and place expanded powders over the suture on all sides, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave these in place until you know that the wound has healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most convenient for small wounds, since one or two needles, or thereabouts, are sufficient for their closure.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you stitch the four ends, two of the thread and two of the wound edges, at once, with a needle on which is wound thread of even thickness and fineness.
Then, when you have passed the needle through these four ends, withdraw the needle from the same side you began, so that the drawn thread falls from the highest part to the wound, so that the suture is like a cloth seam with which cloths are sewn together.
And place between each stitch the width of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men. (or, And this is a suture known to men.)
And with such a suture I sewed up a wound which had happened to a man in his belly, which had been wounded with a knife, and the rupture of the wound was more than a palm in length, and two palms of the intestine had come out from the middle of the wound, and the rupture was in the middle of the abdomen; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I cured him and he is healed; and moreover he lived for many years afterwards, and remained in good health.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and it was by a miracle that he was, for I did not cure him with ointment, for it was in a place where ointment was not suitable nor any medicine, but I placed old linen on the wound twice a day and washed it frequently with honey water until it was healed.
But I will describe two kinds of sutures proper according to the account of Paul (of Aegina, presumably).
And it is that you begin with the suture of the gut, and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And so when the needle penetrates the gut and that same laceration, both straight and along the length of the whole body, leave the end of the thread where you inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate at the other end from the inside to the outside at the other end of the wound.
And then, when you have made that penetration, then you make it penetrate again at that same end of the wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is at this point, and make the needle penetrate at the other end from the inside to the outside.
And make the penetration with that [needle] with which you make that penetration in the thread at the end of the wound which is on the side of him [the patient], until all of it penetrates.
And then begin from the same side and sew that with the end of the thread on the other side, and remove the needle from the gut which it penetrated, and then return it into that gut and sew the end of the thread which is on the other side with this end of the wound; and remove it from the gut which is on his side.
And do this once and again, and do this once then another time until you sew all that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suture is that you sew with a purse-string suture with which you sew skins, so that you sew the thread once from the side of the end of the gut, and that you leave it another time until you have completed it.
But the second type of suture, which is properly called, and this is spoken of by him and recounted by him, is that you sew it according to the similarity with which certain medics sew it, so that they join each part to its corresponding part conveniently by nature; and they apply the end of one to the other end of him, and the end of the wound to the other end of him, and this is done as I have recounted to you.
So that you place the needle in the end of the wound nearest to you from the outside part, and make it penetrate to the inside part in that sole place, and leave two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside through the two ends of the thread.
And then turn them each, and make each penetrate from the inside part to the outside part through two other ends of the wound that are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common, lighter suture, and it is the suture of which I previously made mention.
This is the speech/account of Galien (Galen).
And others say to me, the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, that is, that you introduce into each of these a thread, and begin with the introduction of the needle from the side that is toward you, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who sew skins equally.
And know that when the disruption is in the middle of the belly, then the suture of it is more difficult than in other places of the belly.
But the judgment of the medication of a wound is the judgment of other wounds.
The reason is that when you clean the fresh wound with its own blood before the air alters it, and reduce the intestine and sew it and dress it properly, then place incarnative powder on it.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then place on it some ointments which are at your disposal until they form a hard skin and the threads fall out, and are consolidated with cypress and myrrh; and then cure it in the same way as the cure of other wounds until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines at hand, then immediately place on it, as soon as it begins to throw off pus, old lint, and change that twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, for you do not need other medication in many instances when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will spread to the principal members, then you should submerge lion's wool in good quality soaked oil, or in rose oil, and place it in the area around the armpits and under the arms.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in his intestines, because this often happens, then cauterize it with warm black styptic wine, especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and an opening has been made penetrating into its cavity.
And know that a wound in the large intestine is easier to heal than one in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not readily heal from a wound; and this is because of the great multitude of veins that are in it and because of their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and because of its proximity to the nature of nerves.
But if what proceeds from the wound is the web (likely referring to a membrane or tissue), and you obtain that fresh, reduce it according to your usual method with which you reduce the intestines similarly.
But if it has passed into the space and has already turned green or black, then bind it with gall on the blackened area, so that a flow of blood may come, for there are veins and arteries in the web (membrane).
And then incise that which is beyond that ligament, and place the two ends of the gall hanging down from the part below the wound outside it, so that the attraction of it towards the falling of the web and the emission of the pus from the wound may be relieved for you.
A reminder concerning wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When a disruption occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some men, for this reason because I saw one who had been wounded in his belly by a lance blow and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and had formed a fistula, and from it issued pus and flatulence.
Then I pursued his cure, although I was not confident in his recovery, but I continued to treat it subtly until it was healed and the area was consolidated.
And I, after I saw the area consolidated, feared for the patient that an accident might befall him in his belly; and no such accident happened to him, and he resumed his normal habits of eating and drinking, and then entered a bath and was exercised in his sayings [presumably referring to speech therapy or vocal exercises].
And some experienced [people] have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be tied according to this manner:  that you take ants with large heads; and then bring together the two lips of the wound and place one of those ants, which has its mouth open, on the two lips of the wound; then when it bites onto them and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it will adhere and not be dissolved.
And then place another ant next to the first, and do not cease to do this with ants, one next to another, according to the size of the entire wound.
And then reduce [the intestine] and sew the wound; and let those attached heads remain in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and let no harm befall the patient.
And it is also possible that the intestine be tied with a fine thread which is made from the intestine of an animal, after it is threaded through a needle; and it is that you take the end of this intestinal thread, and it should be very clean [de-fleshed], and then tie its end to the thread of itself, wrapped [around the intestine], and then thread that thread into needles and sew with it the intestine, and reduce it into the disruption [the damaged area].
And this method of suturing with ants is only a matter of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, because then it is only a reduction [of the protrusion], and not a complete healing of it.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas and ulcers.
Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and becomes an ulcer, and is not consolidated and discharges pus constantly and is not excised, then, in whichever member it may be, is universally called a fistula, and we also call it a *zuchem* (or *zychem*, the meaning is uncertain and may require deeper historical context).
And a fistula, truthfully, is a hardened,  white, knotted swelling, without pain, and which has a concavity like the concavity of a bird's claw; and for this reason it is called a knotted ulcer, that is, a claw.
And in some men it is moist, which discharges pus; and very quickly the moisture is excised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes it is thin, and sometimes it is coarse, and sometimes it is subtle.
I have already shown all those causes, and their signs, and their cure with medicines, in the division (of the text).
And it is necessary that I make known to you the treatment of fistula, which is done by means of surgery with the hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all the members of the body; for there are fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the diaphragm, or the intestine, or the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the joints composed by composition, such as those of the hand and foot; and those which cause damage near the principal member and similarly.
