Therefore, that one of these fistulas which is according to this manner is among the diseases of difficult healing, and does not receive a cure, and dealing with it is laborious and requires knowledge.
And if there is any of the fistulas whose depth does not reach those places that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and you wish to ascertain its extent, then take a probe of lead or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, and search for its end with it.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a thin lead probe, because lead, with the lightness of its body, is easily bent and follows the tortuous path.
And if the fistula has many winding passages, it is not possible for you to determine its extent with a probe.
Then, inject a clyster into the opening(s), because the liquid by which you inject it will go through the other openings and cleanse them.
And then inquire carefully, in whatever way possible, to know if there are bones or nerves present, and whether the depth of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a fistula with many openings, and consider all this with the utmost of your strength, with your questioning, by which you question the patient, and by the identification of pain through your compression with your hand over the area, and similar indications and accidental causes of the abscess.
And when you have considered all these causes with true judgment, then proceed to treatment with confidence:  that is, consider then whether the fistula is superficial and near, or in a healthy place, far from a joint, or nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places that I have mentioned to you.
Then you dry the fistula as described before, and remove what is in it of corrupted matter and flesh, and that which has grown in the opening, of flesh or skin or the like, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then you must incise it as deeply as possible, and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh; and then apply cauteries wrapped in sharp medicines, and introduce them into the depth of the fistula, which you have grasped with an instrument.
And do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotten in the depth of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then you must investigate it and dry it as we have recounted, if you do not prohibit it because of some vein or nerve or principal member as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that will cause flesh to grow until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not achieved the complete eradication of the corruption; and then uncover it again, and meticulously perform the scraping and cleansing of it with your utmost strength, and then restore it.
And so if it is healed, good; but if not, then know that that corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which you have not reached, and then for you in that case there is nothing to do but to leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to remove it, then you remove it with fine forceps which are suitable for this.
And if there are many pieces of bone, then meticulously remove all of them, and do not leave any behind with your best effort.
But if some matter of humidity opposes you, and there is no fear or error in their incision, then incise those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not ready to remove the bones at this time, then apply something to the wound that will cause it to suppurate, and leave it until that reaches the area around those bones, flesh, and ligaments, and watch the wound so that it does not heal, and keep constricted the incision that you made in your operation.
Then, if you are afraid, place on it a cloth soaked in crushed rue with oil, or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in one of the green ointments; for when those ligaments suppurate, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and extraction is easier.
But if it is a large bone, such as the bone of the leg or thigh, or similar to those, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape that with a rasp only on the surface until that blackness and corruption is removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that and incise all the way to where the corruption has reached, and then treat that until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use this as an example and help in your operation.
There was a man young in age, around thirty years, who suffered pain in his thigh, and the internal parts swelled with matter inside the body until the matter reached the thigh, and it was the matter of an abscess, a large abscess, and there was no external cause for it.
And then time was extended with that because of the error of the physicians until the abscess was opened, and much matter flowed from it, and its healing was delayed until the leg was fistulated, and many orifices were made in it, all of which discharged pus and the body's humors.
And then many physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no wisdom in them but in the art, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those orifices in great quantity; and already the body of that man was all weakened and his color was jaundiced.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and the probe reached the bone.
And then I examined all the orifices and found them to extend to all parts of the leg.
And then I considered and incised one of those orifices until I uncovered a part of the bone, and I found it corrupted, and it was already earthy and corrupt and blackened; and a part of the leg was already corrupted as far as the marrow.
And then I sawed away what was exposed and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I thought that in the bone there was only that corruption which I excised and sawed, and I had already eradicated it.
And then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicine for a long time, and it is not consolidated.
And then I again iterated and uncovered the bone a second time, over the first uncovering, and found continuing corruption in the bone.
And I sawed away what appeared to me of that corruption, and then I was perforated making his cure and restoration, and it is not restored.
And then I uncovered him again and did not cease to incise the bone little by little, particularly, and forcibly [tried to] restore him, and it was not restored until I incised about a palm's breadth of it, and that I extracted with its marrow, and then I restored that with medicines, and it is lightly consolidated and healed.
And further iteration in the operation on him and section of him is not necessary except for the disposition of his frailty and for the smallness of his tolerance and his fear of death, for he suffered fainting spells at all hours due to excessive evacuation.
And therefore he is healed by a complete healing, and nothing is in place of the hard bone flesh; and his disposition in his body is made good and his strength returned; and he is converted to his dispositions and suffered no impediment in walking nor harm in any way.
But if there is a prominent bone in the place of the flesh already removed, then you must saw that off in this manner:  that you take a ligature and bind it tightly around the extremity of that prominent bone, and command someone to extend it upwards; and place that woolen ligature thicker than the first ligature; and then bind that above the flesh that is below the bone, and extend its extremity so that you draw the flesh to the parts below, and you uncover the flesh above the place which you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
And place wood or a board decently under the bone, for when you do this, the saw is not prohibited from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be done on the corrupt place little by little, for if corruption is in the concavity of the bone and does not appear in its outward appearance to the senses, then force the sawing of it another time.
And if the bone is corrupt and not prominent, but one part is continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle or in part of it, then you uncover the flesh from all parts completely; and then place wood underneath, and then saw it from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing is continuous with the other part.
And let the sawing be according to the location of the corruption, little by little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupt joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no reason for it except engineering (or ingenuity/skill).
And if the corruption is in the chest of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, you must inquire into the corruption in whatever way appears to you, and you must open it, and clean it according to any disposition which is possible for you, and with every tool, let it be rectified for you, when it does not harm you in vein or nerve.
And know that the incisors and saws for incising these bones are many according to the placement of the bones, and the preparation of them, and the thickness of them, and the subtlety and the size and the smallness of them, and the hardness and their rarity; therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument convenient to that operation.
And know that this operation signifies to you the type of instrument of which you are in need; because with you there is great study and long and extensive knowledge of the ways of this art, and testimony of the ways of illnesses, for then you already have for yourself that which is convenient in the way of instruments for every illness.
And I am providing you, at the end of this chapter, the number of instruments which you will place as examples with which you will proceed to your prohibition which you will test on the others.  (The "prohibition" likely refers to a surgical procedure or treatment.) Shape/Type of saw:

Concerning the incision of extremities and the sawing of bones.
Extremities may become diseased either by an intrinsic or extrinsic cause, and if you have treated that corruption with remedies and the treatment is ineffective, or you see the corruption spreading through the limb without being caused by anything, then you must incise that limb up to the point where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may escape from death or a greater harm resulting from the limb's deterioration.
And the sign of this happening is that the limb turns black as if fire has already burned it, or the blackness spreads until the decay reaches the limb, affecting it and spreading throughout the body; therefore, treat it with an incision.
Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the impact of some poisonous thing, such as a sea scorpion, viper, or shrew, and similar creatures.
And if the corruption or injury is in the extremity of a finger, then you should incise the finger and not give space to the corruption so that it may spread to the hand.
And similarly, if it affects the hand, then you should incise it from the base of the palm, and not allow the corruption to spread so that it takes hold in the forearm.
And if it affects the arm, then incise the arm up to the elbow joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond and you see it around the elbow, then in that case is the death of the patient; and to administer anything else beyond that in treatment is a waste of effort.
And similarly with the foot: when corruption affects a toe, then you should incise it up to one of the ankle bones.
And if it takes hold in the instep of the foot, then you incise the whole foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then you incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is nothing for him but dismissal, and to leave the patient to death.
And the manner of incising a limb or cauterizing it is that you tighten a ligature on the part below that which you wish to incise, and tighten another ligature above the area; and let an assistant extend a ligature to the parts below, and let another assistant extend a ligature above; and you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures, spreading it out until all the flesh is uncovered; and then let it be incised or cauterized.
And it is necessary that linen cloths be placed on all parts so that it does not touch the healthy area, and that the patient experiences a properly adjusted lesion and a hot abscess.
But if you encounter a flow of blood while operating, then you should carefully cauterize the affected area, or apply some blood-stanching powders to it; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and care for it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have recounted to you, which is that a blackness occurred in his foot with a burn that resembled a fire burn.
And that corruption initially occurred in his toe until it spread to the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself, because he saw the cure going to the limb with such vehemence that he felt great pain and burning, and he himself incised that foot up to the joint, and it was healed.
For a long time, he suffered that same corruption in the index finger of his hand.
And then he came to me, and I tried to repel that excess with what I applied to his hand in terms of medicine, namely the purification of his body; and the excess was not repelled and began to spread to another finger until it reached the hand.
And he asked me to incise his hand, but I did not want to do so, hoping for the repulsion of that excess, and even more so because I feared death from the incision of his hand, because the patient's strength was failing.
And after he left me, he returned to his land, and it came to my attention that that man prepared himself and incised his whole hand, and he was healed.
And this narrative is not added except to be helpful in understanding this illness, and to be a sign by which it can be recognized and according to which it should be treated.
Concerning the treatment of sinuses and the quality of clysterization (enema) with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in fleshy members and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it forms a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and the pus comes out, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is over it is thin like a fine cloth, and it has not reached further corruption, and it does not penetrate to the bone or to the nerves or ligaments, it is called a sinus, and it is not called a fistula.
However, if the space is prolonged until the corruption penetrates any member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And its treatment is to incise that skin entirely as it is swollen, and especially when it is thinned and made like a cloth, and make sure it does not adhere to the place through its corruption.
But if you fear that the skin will adhere and that that corruption has not reached further consequence, and the skin has some thickness, then you treat that with a clyster (enema).
And it is that you consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the arm that is there has a foul odor, then clysterize that with an Egyptian medicine which is of oil and sharp wine and resin, taking equal parts, and let them be mixed in a vessel, and let them be cooked over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
Then you take what is necessary from it and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize the sinus with this, and compress its orifice, and leave the medicine in it for the amount of two hours.
And then extract that with a syringe; and do this for as many days until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with ash water, when this medicine is not present to you; and it is that you take the ashes of vines, and put water over them; and then strain that and clysterize with it until you are confident that the sinus is then clean.
And if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then clysterize him with water and honey and to which crushed resin is mixed, and clysterize that with wine and honey mixed, for the property of honey is to cleanse and wash, and the wine makes it adhere to the sinus, and especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are confident that the sinus is now clean, then clysterize it with something that causes the flesh to grow there.
And it is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or that you clysterize it with other medicines and ointments that have the properties which I have already written in the treatise of ointments.
And if the orifice of the sinus is narrow, so that the nozzle of the clyster is not received, then you should widen it little by little with a small iron instrument, and place in it a tent wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment until it is widened.
And similarly, if the orifice is wide, then you should approximate its lips with sutures and leave a sufficient opening for the clyster to enter without addition.
And if the orifice from which the pus flows is situated higher up, then dry it in the lowest part of it so that the pus flows downwards, for when the pus is trapped in the depth of the sinus, flesh will not be generated there.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus down to the lowest bone as you wish, then strive to prepare the limb with a preparation by which the pus flows lightly as it is convenient for you and so that no pus is retained there.
But the one over the orifice of the sinus, you should change that every hour so that what is collected there of the pus comes out. And so that you know if what flows from the pus is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And with this, you should examine the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And as your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter expelled at the opening of the sinus is already adhering, or tends to adhere, then loosen it the next day, or the third day; and when you loosen the dressing, then change the cloth for another and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus a subtle poison spreads nearby, then do not be surprised by its adhesion, for its healing is approaching; because there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and others in whom the opposite occurs.
And if undigested pus has issued from the sinus for many days, then know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you should adjust to drying medications; and if the medications are moist in their substance and in their virtue, they are dry like diafinicon ointment, when the superfluous addition of zegi is adjusted to it.
And the best remedy with which it is cured, is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and add old, well-aged wine to it, equal in its substance, and mix this well together and use it.
And it resembles that action, when there is no pus, diafinicon ointment, honey cooked until thickened, and use it; or take myrrh, aloes, and incense, grind everything and powder it over the honey while it is on the fire, then apply this on a cloth and bind it over the sinus; and apply it with the honey that you have cooked until thickened, and powder it on a cloth and bind it.
And sometimes aristolochia longa and roots of lily of the valley (and it is lily of the valley) and flour of barley and centaurea are administered with it, and use these simples or mixed; grind those, sift them, and powder them over the honey which has been mentioned; and use it.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in its depth, and it has already inquired into the bones or other hard parts or ligaments; then you treat it with fistula treatment, as described above.
Concerning the treatment of paronychia, and of injured nails, and of added finger incisions, and of sectioning.
Paronychia is an abscess which arises under the nail of the finger or toe of the hand or foot, and easily arises in other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its treatment is less valued, or is abscessed from a hot abscess, or is corrupt, and throws out pus until it corrodes the root of the nail, and easily corrupts the whole of it, and easily its corruption penetrates to the bone until there is a foul odor to it, and the extremity of the finger becomes swollen, and its color becomes dark.
And therefore when you have this with what we have said in the division and do not succeed in your treatment, then it is necessary that you incise with iron all the superfluity that remains of the nail and then cauterize the wound near to it, for a hot cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail, once empty, already compress the flesh at the inner parts, and begin to prick and hurt it, then it is necessary that you place a fine thread under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and lift it up and incise that flesh easily, and place upon what remains of the flesh corrosive medicines until it is all consumed; and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption already penetrates the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone and extract it, for a bone which is corrupt is never healed.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption going into the finger, then you incise it at one of the joints, as its treatment is prescribed, and then treat it until it is healed.
But if the nail suffers from a blow or friction, and there is vehement pain in it, then you must first bleed the patient, and then cut the nail with a sharp instrument with a transverse cut from above downwards, and take care that the cut does not reach the flesh which is under the nail, for you will cause the patient vehement pain, and it will be the cause of the growth of superfluous flesh in that place; and then treat that place with what reduces pain.
But a finger, which is born in the hands of some men, is very quickly fleshy all over, and very quickly has bones, or very quickly has nails.
And some of them are born at the root of the joint, or their growth is in some of the joints of the fingers. But that which is born in some joint of the finger does not hurt, and that which is born in the joints is very quickly painful.
And of those that are born fleshy, the section is easy, and it is that you should incise that one below its root with a broad instrument.
But of that which is born at the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of its excision.
But in that which is born on a finger between the joints, it is necessary that you first incise its flesh with a circular incision down to the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then treat that until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to the fingers between their knuckles, either by the birth of a man born thus, or by consolidation of a wound, or by burning with fire, or similarly, then it is necessary that you sever that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in rose oil so that they are not lightly consolidated, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until it is consolidated as necessary.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you cut that consolidation according to how it is formed and suitable to the shape of the limb.
Of incisions of vices, there are various kinds.
Vices are various, and they are swollen, thick veins full of melancholic superfluities which appear in many parts of the body, and most often in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and burden-bearers.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilica vein.
But the treatment of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they be incised and that black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be elevated and entirely extracted.
The incision is done in this manner:  first bathe the leg with very hot water until the thick, feculent blood is resolved from the leg. Then bind the patient's leg from the highest part of the thigh down to below the knee with a bandage. Then incise the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision. Then elevate the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and from above downwards, until the quantity of blood you desire and which seems sufficient is drawn out, and which will remove the patient's ailment.
Then bind it up, and instruct the patient to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this harms the patient.
But their elevation is according to this manner: you who shave the patient's leg, if there is much hair on it, and then put him in a bath and bathe his leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins are swollen; or, if a bath is not available to you, let him perform vigorous exercise until the limb is warmed.
Then incise the vein in the right place along the length of the vein, either behind the knee, or lower down behind the ankle.
Then open the vein with an instrument and scrape the vein on all sides until it bleeds, and it is from its appearance that you see this redness turning to blackness.
And then, when it has bled, from the vein you see that which is white, like a string.
Then you place a leech below it so that it is elevated and is drawn to the vein, and suspend it with a golden or silver hook.
Then make another incision near that incision three fingers' breadth away, and then scrape the vein from the part above the vein until it bleeds.
Then raise it and incise it in the rear section towards the heel, and then extract it and raise it until it comes out through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision that is above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until all is extracted; and then incise it.
And if it does not obey you to extract and elevate it, then insert a double-ended strong thread needle and tie it and extract it, or insert a leech below it and move your hand around until it is drawn out. And be careful that it is not incised; for if it is broken, it is made difficult for you to elevate it and it has become harmful to the patient because of that damage.
And when it is all expelled, then place on the wound site wool soaked in rose wine and un-rose-scented olive oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the intestine is constricted, having tortuosity where there is a twisting in the parts, and it is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary that you incise it along all its tortuous parts and at the places where it is twisted, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate the whole thing.
And beware during your incision that you do not cut the vein and that you do not injure it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in this.
The form of the incision with which the intestine is cut:
It is not sharpened like hooks, nor is it of a sharp point so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of a thick, flexible nature, for if it were thin, it would cut the vein because of its thinness; therefore it has superfluous thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Al-Ategi in Arabia, and in the hot and arid lands of India, of little humidity, and in which neither grass nor trees grow.
And it is easily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from under the skin, just as serpents, worms, scabies, and maggots are generated between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of its coming is that in the leg a vehement inflammation occurs; and then the area is blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blister, which resembles the root of a plant.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one to two drachms, and tie it, leaving the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as something of it appears outwardly, wrap it in lead and tie it. And if the protrusion is prolonged, then you incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not incise its root until it has emerged, for if you incise it, it is retracted and enters the flesh, causing an abscess and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulceration.
Therefore it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is entirely expelled and no part of it remains in the body.
And from this vein, in some men, it has issued to a length of five palms and fingers, and it has even reached me that one issued of twenty palms. (Note:  A palm is an old unit of length, approximately 4 inches).
And if it is incised at the time of its treatment, then introduce a probe into its opening and perforate it with a long hole through the body, until all that is in the matter is evacuated, and treat the putrefaction of the place for some days with medicines; and then cure it by the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many orifices come to it, and a branch issues from each orifice.
And therefore cure it in the way we have said in the division and with what has been said above.
Concerning the incision (or cutting) above worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine sickness.
This illness is called in some lands among us bovine illness because it often happens to cattle, and it is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and it goes throughout the body, rising and falling, and it is shown by the feeling of its movement from member to member until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is by the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms and serpents and earthworms and ascarids happen in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice, for when it goes into the body and is raised to the head, and has reached the eye, then very quickly it enters and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
And therefore, when you wish to cure this and extract it, then it is done only because of its movement and appearance to the senses, and then it is necessary that you tighten what is above it and what is below it with strong binding and constriction, and then dry it out and extract it.
And if it is deeply embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then you place a cautery with fire on the place until it dies, and the greatest cause that is feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the place as we have said.
But if you see it has reached the head near the eye, then tighten above the eyebrow with a strong binding, and then dry it out, and extract it; and it is necessary that the patient use purification of their body with medicines evacuating bad putrid humors, and guard against foods generating putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the disease nafir (or a similar ailment).
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain which comes in certain limbs, and then is moved from limb to limb.
And I have seen it as I shall tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small swelling in the basilic vein of the arm, and after it had remained for an hour, I saw that swelling moving in the manner of a worm climbing upwards, but more lightly than a worm, as if it were quicksilver running from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and fixed itself in the muscle, and remained there for about an hour.
And then it ran to the other side until it reached the other arm.
And then I realised that it was moving throughout her body as I had seen; and I was very astonished by its movement and its speed from limb to limb. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in this manner, however I have seen many who find pain moving from one limb to another. But I did not see it with my eyes as I did in the woman.
And I thought that this was so because the woman was old, with a dry body and exposed veins, and in this case that wind-like movement was apparent to the senses.
And it is necessary that it does not appear, following this treatment, in those who are delicate and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press on that place lightly and dry it until that constricted wind-like ailment is expelled, and cauterize the area.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes, then treat it with rubbing of the body and with things that expel winds, and drive them away with foul-smelling pills and pills of scammony and similar things.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the places in the body where they lodge, for their diversity is such that there are those with large points, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones, that is, solid ones; and those that have three angles, and those that have barbs; and those that have feathers.
And of the signs of the brain, when an arrow lodges there and pierces the bone, and emerges from the skull which is over the brain, for it causes sudden violent pain, and scotoma (blurred vision), and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of mind, and vomiting of bile, and speech is quickly cut off and the voice is destroyed, and there emerges from the wound a white moisture similar to pus, and from it something like the watery substance of flesh.
And therefore, if these signs appear to you, then abstain from the cure and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is already out.
But the signs of the arrow when it lodges in the heart, are that it is near the left nipple, and one feels as if it were fixed in something hard, and it is not in an empty space; and very quickly the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and there follows coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and syncope; and therefore know that death approaches without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are the emission of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels near the neck are swollen, and the patient's color changes, and he breathes with great difficulty, and desires the intake of cold air.
And if the arrow lodges in the pleura, which is in the chest, the pain then is near the lower ribs, and great difficulty in breathing is caused with violent pain and sighing, and not all the muscles of the limbs are affected.
And if the arrow lodges in the stomach, then very quickly some undigested food comes from the wound.
And if the arrow lodges in the abdomen, and is fixed there, some of the wound will discharge pus, or intestinal tissue, or intestine which is ruptured, and there is no hope of its cure without extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow lodges in the bladder, and urine is expelled, and some matter proceeds from it to the external parts, and severe pain quickly comes upon the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members, such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the armpits, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the hip, and the leg and similar members, are saved according to the best dispositions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you that I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may thereby understand your own treatment.
Which is the reason that, in a man an arrow lodged in the tear duct of his eye at the root of the nose, and I extracted it from the other side, below his ear, and he is healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which lodged in him in the corner of his eye, below the eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden, and I found only a small point with which it adhered to its shaft; and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a lion head, which had no barbs.
And the Jew is healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and this is the kind that has barbs; and then I incised it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and then I carefully worked on it, and then I extracted it, and the Christian is healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought that he would die from it; and therefore, since thirty days had passed and no change had occurred in his condition, I incised over the arrow and extracted it, and he is healed, and no harm befell him.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow lodged in his back, and the wound healed over him, and after about seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman in whom an arrow lodged in her belly, and the wound healed within her, and her condition did not change in any respect, and the woman found no impediment in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow lodged in his face, and the wound healed and the arrow broke, finding no great pain there, and many similar cases.
And I extracted the arrow from the king's prince's man which had fallen into the middle of his nose and had already declined a little to the right side, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his cure, about three days after the arrow's lodging.
And then I found the wound from the arrow very narrow; and then I investigated it with a thin probe, and did not feel it; but he felt a prickling under his right ear, and then I suspected that this prickling was from the tip of the arrow.
And I plastered the place with a plaster which has attractive and digestive virtue, trusting that the place would suppurate so that the location of the arrow would appear to me, so that I could cut into it.
And no significant event happened in the area indicating that the arrow had reached that place; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no event happened; and the wound was consolidated meanwhile, and the patient desisted from its extraction for many days, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and reported this to me.
And then I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it was opened, and with the probe I tested it, and found the thin point of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I adjusted the application of the sharp medicine to the wound until the tip of the arrow appeared to me visibly, and I spent time doing this for a period of four months.
And then, since the wound was enlarged, and it became possible for me to insert forceps into it, and remove it, and I did not succeed in doing so, then I did not cease to refine and devise methods on it, until one day I managed to grasp it with specially made forceps, as the shape of those will be shown at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I restored the wound.
And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, but I restored it and the wound was consolidated, and the patient is healed with complete health, and he suffered no harm whatsoever.
And I recount to you the method of extraction of certain arrows so that you may apply this rule and meaning to that which has not been said, for the parts of this art and its definitions are not comprehended with narration, nor does this book contain them.
And skilled artisans compare the small with the great; and that which is present with that which is absent; and they find new operations and new instruments for the removal of foreign bodies from the art when it occurs.
Therefore I say that arrows are not expelled from the limbs into which they are fixed except in two ways: either by traction from the place where they are embedded; or by pushing from the opposite side.
And that which is expelled from the place where it entered, is an arrow that has penetrated a fleshy place, and it is pulled and expelled.
And if it does not yield to extraction at the time of the injury, then you should leave it for a few days until the flesh surrounding it softens, and its extraction and removal are eased.
Similarly, if it is embedded in bone and does not yield to extraction, then leave it for a few days and repeat the attempt at extraction and movement each day, and it will eventually be expelled.
And if it still does not yield to extraction after a few days, then you must perforate the bone around the arrow on all sides with a thin drill until the area around the arrow is widened, and then extract it.
But if the arrow is hidden within the body and is beyond feeling, then you should search for it with a probe; and if you feel it, then extract it with instruments suitable for its removal.
And if you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the wound and the length of the projectile, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, then you should enlarge the wound so that it is possible to extract the arrow.
And if it is held by barbs, then you should free the flesh in which it is embedded from all sides with all the skill you can muster; and work on this, if you cannot manage the removal of the flesh by breaking off the barbs and twisting them until they yield.
And when you undertake the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then use twisting motions of your hand with forceps in all directions until it yields; and proceed with utmost delicacy so that the arrow is not broken and its extraction is not made difficult for you.
And if you cannot manage it at that time, then leave it for a few days until the parts surrounding it soften, and then try again, for it will then be easier. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then administer the treatment we have described in its chapter.
And be careful about cutting a vein or nerve, or tendon, and use all possible skill to remove the arrow; and do this with ease, calmness, slowness, and firmness, as I have recommended to you.
And therefore, counteract the arrow so that the patient is positioned according to the figure showing the arrow's placement, for this is more helpful. But if this is not possible for you, then administer what is possible according to the figures (illustrations).
But if the arrows are protruding from the opposite side, or if any part is already projecting outwards, or if the tip of the arrow causes swelling at the end of the deep wound, sometimes even protruding; then you should cut above it; and the incision should be as large as the forceps can grasp; and then pull it out, for it will then be easily removed.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then you twist the blade in the opposite direction until the arrowhead is embedded in the bone, and widen it yourself, and then extract it.
And if not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is in it, then push it through with him/it (referring to the shaft or a tool).
But if the shaft has fallen off and you want to administer pressure, then insert, if it is blunt, a concave instrument so that its concavity fits into the tail of the arrow and then push it through.
But if the arrowhead is concave, then you push it through with a strong instrument entering it, because the arrowhead is thereby relieved.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you remove all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so, and then treat it with what is appropriate.
And if the arrow falls into the foot, or into the belly, or into the bladder, or into the side, and is close enough that you can touch it with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut around it, then cut around it and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and extract it, and then dress the wound if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the forceps with which arrows are pulled out:
Let their ends be like a bird's beak, which are made like a saw when they grasp the arrow so that they do not let go of it.
And of these, many types are made, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and the tightness of it.
like a ray, so that its entry into the concave arrow is eased and is pushed with that.
Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which the customary number is thirty, of which sixteen are in the head; that is, two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called *alhasisain*; two veins which are in the two apparent temples; and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes which are called *aspicientz*; and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead, and the vein which is at the tip of the nose; and two veins which are at the neck; and two veins which are in the upper lip; and two veins which are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins which are called *algelic* —; and there are two veins which are under the tongue. But the veins which are phlebotomized in the arms are five; for of these there is one cephalic, and it is located on the wild side; and the people call it "the vein of the head", and it is deep, and it is a middle vein, and is composed of a branch of the basilic, and of another branch of the cephalic; and the people name that one "vein of the whole body", and the basilic is a vein which is located on the inner side of the arm, and it is called "citillar", and the common people name that one "vein of the belly"; and the *funis* of the arm, and it is located on the forearm, and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the elbow by manifest appearance; and *salvatella*, and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger, and there are two branches in it.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins; one of these is that which is below the bend of the knee on the outer side; and the second is the saphena, and its location is below the leg on the inner side; and the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg on the outer side; and in the leg there are other veins similar to these.
The benefit of opening the veins near the ears is for chronic catarrh, migraine, *sahafati* (a term needing further investigation for accurate translation), and old, bad head ulcers.
And the method of bleeding those veins is as I recommend to you:  that the head of the patient be raised, and the patient's head be rubbed at the occiput in the area of both veins with a wide cloth, with good friction; then the patient should tighten their neck with a bandage until the two veins and their locations appear near the ears in two depressed areas of the head; and feel for them with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those veins with your finger, then bleed them with a lancet.
Then you take the lancet knife, which is sharp, and you insert it under the vein in the incision until the blood flows out. Then raise your hand with the vein and the incision to the upper parts and incise the vein with a sharp incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two fingers placed together, or thereabouts; and let blood flow out as much as you wish.
Then bind it with powders and leave it until it is healed.
And I have already described at the beginning of the book the incision of these and their cauterization.
But of the two arterial veins that are in the temples, bloodletting is beneficial for chronic and sudden severe migraine, persistent ophthalmia, and a flow of acute superfluities effused into the eyes.
And the way to perform phlebotomy on these is as I have recounted to you:  constrict the patient's neck tightly until the two veins appear palpable by clear manifestation and their pulse is felt beneath your finger; and then you bleed with a lancet.
Then you raise the skin above the vein at the upper part with your index finger, and insert the phlebotomy lancet underneath, and raise the vein upwards; and incise it just as you did the other two veins; and let the blood flow according to your need.
Then loosen the constriction on the patient, and place your finger on the vein for an hour, and then apply a bandage and powders to it, and bind it tightly above with a strong constriction, and leave it until it is healed.
And already the recollection of them, and of their incision, and the drawing [of blood] has been preceded in the beginning of the book.
Therefore, the benefit of phlebotomy of the frontal vein is comparable to phlebotomy of the cephalic vein for ancient diseases of the face, and for migraine, and redness, and fetid ulcers.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is as I recounted to you; that is, that the patient should constrict his neck with a bandage until the vein appears; and then take the instrument which is named a fossorium, of which this is the form:
Place the prominent point which is at the head of the fossorium upon that vein and strike it with a hammer or with some other thing in the manner of it, and let the blood flow according to the quantity which you wish; and then loosen the perforation of the patient and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a lateral phlebotomy, however it is not fitting that the phlebotomy be of acute extremity like the other phlebotomies, rather it should be of blunt extremity, and perform the phlebotomy across, because the bone is near and would easily break there if the phlebotomy were subtle [i.e., finely pointed].
But the benefit of the two veins which are at the lacrimal ducts of the two eyes is for scabies of the eyes, for redness and swelling in the eyes, and for diseases of the face.
And the manner of the phlebotomy is that the patient should constrict his neck with a bandage, and then phlebotomize those veins, and you should be standing over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made across rather than lengthwise, a little with a small phlebotomy tool, because in that place there is not much flesh; and if the phlebotomy tool is sharp, its point will be broken very easily.
And then let blood flow as much as is necessary for you, and place cotton over it, and bind it for a night, and then unbind it.
But phlebotomy of the vein of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and violent headache, and for diseases of the face and migraine and redness that happens in the face, and especially when it is chronic.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is that the patient should constrict his neck and then you hold his nose with your left hand, and take a long, thin phlebotomy tool and insert it into the middle of the nasal bridge in a straight line, because the vein is not apparent to the touch here, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
See that you move your hand with the small phlebotomy tool little by little and let blood flow according to your need; and then bind it for a night, because it is lightly restored.
But the benefit of phlebotomy of the two jugular veins is for the constriction of the throat and the beginning of leprosy and many diseases and impetigo and bad corrosive ulcers, as well as herpes.
And the manner of phlebotomy of those (veins) is that the patient should tighten under each one on his neck with a bandage; and the surgeon should stand over the patient's head, and the patient should be sitting on a stool.
And then phlebotomize the vein lengthwise with a wide phlebotomy tool little by little; and then extract blood according to a moderate quantity or according to what the necessity of the situation requires; and then do likewise with the other vein.
And then untie the bandage and tighten the two veins with a moderate constriction so that the patient is not suffocated, and leave it until morning because the wound is healed.
And the benefit of phlebotomy of the alveolar veins is good for small abscesses and pustules of the mouth and corruption of the gums and bad ulcers and fissures, and for bad ulcers that are in the nose and around it.

But if you are in doubt, and you do not know which of those (veins) it is, then you should make an incision in the larger of those, and in the most apparent of those.
And do the same for the two veins that are in the upper lips.
But of the two veins that are under the tongue, the phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is more beneficial for affections that are in the throat, for disease of the uvula, and for diseases of the mouth.
And the manner of phlebotomizing these is that you hold the patient between your hands facing the sun, and elevate his tongue, and look under the tongue, on one side of it is a vein and on the other side a vein, and the color of each is tending towards blackness.
And then phlebotomize each; and be careful not to overuse your hand in phlebotomizing these, because under these are arteries.
And the veins that are phlebotomized in the neck are those which are present in all men, and their phlebotomy is done in two ways; either one punctures with a myrtle- or olive-shaped fleam of a thinning point; or one cuts with a lancet-shaped fleam, and it is called incision, and this is its form:
Let its width be like this, and it is suitable for opening concave, full, apparently large veins, and those which contain thick, turbid blood.
And this fleam is of lesser width and of a more subtle point, and is suitable for phlebotomy of fine veins that contain thin, citrine blood.
But the benefit of phlebotomy of the basilica vein, which is one of three veins: because it draws out blood from illnesses that are below the throat and neck, and from those that affect the feet and abdomen.
And it is necessary for the phlebotomist, when performing this phlebotomy, that he carefully guards this and be very careful in this, because beneath it is an artery, and if he errs and is inaccurate in the puncture of the fleam, he will incise that artery and cause a flow of blood, so that it is necessary that he not phlebotomize this with a sharp fleam, rather it should be cut with a lancet.
And if the basilica vein does not appear manifestly, then it is necessary that you leave that one and that you move to another, or that you seek some branch of it; or that you phlebotomize in its place at the end of the arm, for that is manifest; and incise that with a lancet-fleam as we have said.
But if you wish phlebotomy in the same place, then before the constriction of the arm you should touch the area until you know the location of the pulse, and then mark it with ink; and then tie the arm and incise the vein with a concave incision with a lancet-fleam as we have said; and be careful that the incision does not fall within the space of the artery's location.
And when you see, as you tighten the ligature, inflation in the place that you have marked with ink, then that inflation is inflation of the artery, and therefore avoid that.
And if you see the blood flowing in the phlebotomy like the urine of a child, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is blood from the artery; and then you observe and place your finger on it for a long time; and then remove your finger, and if the blood is interrupted, for many times it is interrupted, then you tighten the arm and leave it.
And watch the patient for their mucus, and be mindful of them and do not harm them for some days until they are healed.
But if the blood is not interrupted, and you see that you do not have a remedy for this, then you incise the artery, if it appears to you; because its extremity is constricted, and the blood is interrupted; or that you take the bark of a fig tree, and dry it, and take one half and bind it over the area of the vein with a descending constriction with a ligature and with powders until the next day.
And then if the blood is interrupted, it is good; and if not, then treat it with that which was previously mentioned in the recollection of the place with the powders that interrupt the flow of blood.
And the interruption of their blood is difficult in many conditions because of the small size of the wound and the possibility of ligating the arm.
But from the dark vein, phlebotomy is helpful to draw blood from the superior part of the head and the inferior part of the body; for this reason, because it is composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic, as we have said.
And it behooves the phlebotomist to be very careful in the phlebotomy of this, because below it is a nerve; and if he errs in the puncture of the phlebotomy and finds the nerve, stupor will occur to him, and its healing is difficult, and perhaps it will never be completely healed. And this nerve is often visible; and if it is hidden from some men and is subtle and not shown, then it is necessary that you use the phlebotomy with which you perform phlebotomy with a slanted incision, and avoid the nerve with your skill.
And if the vein is between two nerves, then you should open the vein lengthwise.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is that it draws blood from the head and helps with diseases of the eyes.
And it is proper in this case, if you want the phlebotomist to puncture with an olive-shaped phlebotomy tool or a myrtle-shaped phlebotomy tool; because it is the safest of all veins, for neither artery nor nerve lies beneath it.
However, it is very quickly inflamed in some men, when it is not phlebotomized at the first puncture, but that inflammation does no harm.
The first of these is that you should know that phlebotomy is administered only for the preservation of health and for its hope and caution against the onset of diseases, and that phlebotomy should be performed in one of the veins that are in the elbow, that is: cephalic, median, and basilic; and that phlebotomy should be performed at the beginning of spring, on an empty stomach in spring and autumn, and in winter near mealtime, when signs of repletion appear.
And let phlebotomy be performed on the third day or on the first, after three hours have passed since the day.
But phlebotomy is administered when it provokes convenience and necessity for it, whether by night or by day, at any hour and at any time.
And it is necessary that a child should not be phlebotomized until he is fourteen years old, and a pregnant woman should not be phlebotomized except from the fourth month until the seventh, and old women who are over sixty years old should not be phlebotomized.
And if anyone wishes to be phlebotomized in any manner whatsoever, it is necessary that he cleanse his bowels before phlebotomy with a clyster of honey, if he has much retained excrement in them, so that the veins do not draw the superfluities from the bowels into the phlebotomy, which are putrid, and that they may not harm the principal members.
And let not patients be phlebotomized who have nausea,  fever, or diarrhea, nor those recovering from illness or fever until these things have subsided, nor those suffering from dropsy; and furthermore, let phlebotomy be avoided in cases of colic, vomiting, and abdominal pain, and during excessive sexual intercourse, labor, exercise, fasting, or vigils, and in any circumstance that weakens vital force, whether bodily or animal.
And consider carefully beforehand, concerning the thinning of humors, if the blood is thick, with food and drink, and with medicines, if this is possible for him.
And let him enter a bath, if no cause forbids it, or let him be exercised by some activity so that the blood may be thinned.
And perform his phlebotomy in the middle of the day, that is, around the third hour, as we have said.
And perhaps you should ensure that the patient's feet are guarded on that day from all harmful accidents, both animal, such as anxiety, anger, fear, and from all bodily accidents, such as excessive labor and exertion, sexual intercourse, and the like.
And at his bedside, let there be present things whose use is customary among men, with aromatics and fragrances and with balms and things similar to them, according to what is possible.
And let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion that is higher than the cushion of the one who is to be phlebotomized.
Then, let the phlebotomist extend his arm and rub it with his hand two or three times; and then tighten the ligature with a strap and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, for when the constriction is not equal, or the constriction is excessive, then it prevents the flow of blood; or if it is too loose, then it prevents the blood from flowing out.
And near the constriction, it is necessary that the one who is to be phlebotomized rub his hands together until the veins are swollen and clearly visible.
Then let the phlebotomist anoint his fleam with a little old oil; and then place the index finger of his left hand on the vein, below the place where he wants to perform the phlebotomy, so that the vein moves slightly, and the percussion is stopped because some veins are like cords that flee from the phlebotomy; and those that are full of wind, when you place the fleam on them, contract to avoid the phlebotomy and then the phlebotomist is unsuccessful; and the fleam does not open the vein, and if it opens, it is constricted.
Therefore, the phlebotomist must be skilled and attentive to all these details.
Then apply the fleam; but if the vein is opened on the first attempt, that is good; otherwise, repeat the process a little below or above that spot with haste, if the area is not inflamed.
And if the area is inflamed, or the patient is frightened, then leave it for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage, for it will quickly draw a hot abscess, nor should they enter a bath.
Then repeat the phlebotomy if necessary.
And if the opening is small and the blood flows slowly, and you fear that you will not obtain the desired quantity of blood, then repeat the phlebotomy in that same incision, straightening and slightly enlarging the opening, and do this quickly before the area becomes inflamed, because in many men the area becomes inflamed with even a small opening.
But if you see that it is already inflamed, then do not repeat the procedure there, for it will not help; and apply some crushed olive leaves to the area, for it relieves the body of blood. And olive oil is better in this instance than other oils.
Similarly, administer crushed olive leaves in all phlebotomies with which you open veins to prevent blood clotting;  and use theriac and sagapenum (when applied to the area) to do this, because they thin the blood and break up clots when it is thick.
And if a large abscess forms at the site of the phlebotomy—which happens often, especially in someone who has been bled only once or where the vein opening was small—then take a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water, and apply it tightly to the area for an hour, for this will dissolve it.
And it is necessary to do this immediately after the bleeding from that vein is complete, or from another vein.
And if some blackness or greenness remains at the site for a few days, do not worry; and if you wish, apply something made of dissolved aloes and myrrh, or something made from mint juice, or calamint, or something similar.
And abscesses and swellings often occur after phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
Then place your hand on it, and if you find softness under your compression, then that swelling is malignant and bad; and then be careful not to apply anything of what we have said, because blood will quickly flow from the artery.
And make a plaster with something that has styptic properties, so that the area is hardened, and then treat it with other treatments until it is healed.
And it is necessary to extract blood from everyone according to the quantity of their strength and according to the dominant color of the blood as it appears.
For if the blood is black, then you let it flow until it is red; and similarly, if you see it thick, then you let it flow until it is thinned; and similarly, if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for someone who is full and strong and needs bloodletting, that the phlebotomy of the vein be widened and the phlebotomy be wide.
And in someone who is weak, the opposite should be done, and it is necessary to extract blood in several times, and that the opening be narrow.
And the best way to administer phlebotomy is that the incision be transverse, not a puncture.
And this manner of phlebotomy is safe from excessive bleeding and the incision of nerves, and is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy according to latitude and longitude (i.e., a lengthwise cut).
And to whom syncope is accustomed to happen after phlebotomy, it is necessary that you cure that before phlebotomy with a little bread infused in pomegranate wine or simple warmed oxymel; and extract the blood in three or four times.
And if there is coldness of complexion, then take before phlebotomy bread infused in syrup of myrrh or in syrup of honey aromatized with spices such as pepper, cinnamon, ginger, and the like, or in an aromatized and very fragrant syrup.
And if syncope happens after phlebotomy, and the cause of it is the loss of much blood, then it is necessary that he take to drink meat broth and a very subtly fragrant syrup; and use aromatherapy with rosewater, and let his feet be bathed with it.
And use the other remedies that we have mentioned in the division in the chapter on syncope caused by evacuation (of fluids).
And whoever wants to bleed his arm and dissolve his blood a second time, it is necessary for him, for whom phlebotomy is for the purpose of evacuation, and whose strength is weak, that he evacuate the blood little by little, that is, according to the quantity of strength on continuous days.
But whoever wants to bleed his arm and shed his blood a second time, and his body is strong, then let him do this after seven or nine hours from the first phlebotomy.
But whoever wants to draw blood from his body contrary to the part to which it declines (i.e., to a different part of the body), it is necessary that he be bled on the second or third day.
But that body in which there is much already heated blood, and it is sharp and causes fever to increase, it is necessary that blood be drawn from it at once, and that a large quantity be drawn, and that the opening be widened until syncope occurs, provided that you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy; and that you place your hand on his pulse below the flow of blood, so that death does not result instead of syncope, for this often happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligent.
And it is not necessary, when you want to untie the arm and evacuate the blood a second time, since the opening of the vein is already closed and the blood's exit is made difficult, that you press on it with vehemence and that it be bent slightly extended, for this is very bad.
Having left it, let it be bled a second time; or remove this with the sharpness of the phlebotomy instrument that is coagulated blood in the opening of the vein; or place on it theriac alfarach, or some sage, and compress it with light pressure until the blood has come out.
And if the vein is abscessed, then leave it and do not touch it until the abscess is settled (healed).
And if necessity compels you to evacuate the blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then you bleed it in that place or in the other arm in another vein.
And the vein called "rope of the arm" is phlebotomized in place of the cephalic or basilic, when they are not found or are hidden, for it is composed of those (veins).
And the quality of his phlebotomy is that the patient should put his hand in warm water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears manifestly.

And let the incision be wide, and let your phlebotomy, with which you phlebotomize, be above the joint of the hand a little.
And if the flow of blood is prohibited (stopped), then you return the hand to the warm water and let the blood run into the water until you achieve your need (desired amount).
But if you are in the summer days, then you are excused from returning the hand to the warm water, and the same is prescribed for placing the blood bowl in warm water in winter time.
And the phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all the other veins, because beneath it there is no pulsating vein, nor nerves.
And phlebotomy of the little vein of the right hand is beneficial for liver diseases.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that you constrict the arm with a ligature or with your hand, after you have put it in warm water, until the vein is swollen and clearly visible; and then phlebotomize it transversely; and if you incise it completely, that will not harm in any way.

Therefore, phlebotomize the largest branch below the leg near the heel, because it is better and healthier.

Concerning cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cups are made of horn, or of brass, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And for cups that are administered with scarification and bloodletting, there are fourteen places on the body, of which one, where cups are placed, is the occiput, and that is the back part of the head; and the middle place on either side of the neck together; and cups on the chin below the jaw, below the mouth; and cups on the two shoulder blades; and cups on the hollow of the elbow (or armpit); and cups on the two thighs; and cups on the two ankles; and cups on the two hips.
And cupping does not draw blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not diminish strength as it does in phlebotomy; nor should cupping be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity calls for cupping because of illness or a continuing condition, administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at any time.
Therefore, when you see any of these conditions, and especially if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we order that cupping be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it is applied, it relieves the heaviness in the head and that which affects the eyes; but this should be done after evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not permitted to use it on someone with a cold complexion, or in whom there is a catarrh, for it causes great harm to such a person.
And therefore, it is not advisable to use this on the elderly, and those whose heads are afflicted with cold illnesses.
But to the one on whom this is continuously applied, forgetfulness is caused; and therefore, you must instruct the cupper to place his hand lightly near the cupping device below, to prevent the onset of forgetfulness.
But cupping with alkehel (likely a specific type of cupping or substance used in cupping) is in the place of phlebotomy of the dark vein and the basilic vein: and therefore it benefits asthma, and the constriction of the throat, and the tearing of the throat, and cough, and repletion (likely referring to congestion or fullness).
And it is necessary that the alkehel cupping be applied gently, for if it is placed too forcefully, it generates weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And cupping with alhagdahan (likely another specific substance or technique) benefits accidental pains in the head, ophthalmia (eye inflammation), migraine, suffocation, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper not to press his hand during scarification lest he injure the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin benefits canker sores in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar diseases of the mouth, and stands in place of phlebotomy of the arteries that are in the two lips.
But cupping that is done on the two shoulder blades benefits against caphatan (a condition or disease, the nature of which is unknown from this context), which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two inner parts of the ankles benefits similarly to phlebotomy of three veins, that is, the basilic, median cubital (fusca - likely a translation variant or regional name), and cephalic veins, because it draws blood from all those smaller veins that are in the flesh, and those small veins from the other larger veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed too deeply in scarification, because the area is sparsely fleshed, and underneath are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cupping glass with which one cups over the buttocks and it benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and deeper ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that it should be a large cupping glass, and that it be of brass, because the area requires strong suction; and thin cupping glasses are quickly torn and should be scarified with scarification.
But cupping of the legs is gentler for evident depletion, for it draws blood from the whole body, and it benefits old pains of the joints and of the womb (uterus), and of the bladder; and it provokes menstruation, and benefits boils and swellings; and it stands in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and of the two femoral veins.
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause syncope in many men.
And of the cupping of the femoral veins, the benefit is similar to the benefit of cupping of the two legs.
And the quality of the placement of the cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass initially empty and draw by moderate suction, and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them lightly, and remove them carefully, and allow the humors to come to the place by equal distribution. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place redden and swell and the appearance of the redness of blood.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and consider the disposition of the body.
Because that man who is of tender flesh and rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once and no more, lest the place be ulcerated.
And command the cupper to enlarge the scarification and to make it a little deeper, and temper the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary that you scarify twice, for in the first time the way of thinning is opened to the blood and to its watery part. But in the second [scarification], to the scrutiny acquired from the extraction of thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then let a third scarification be made so that it achieves the desired result.
Finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a small amount, one scarification is sufficient for us.
And if we want extraction of much blood, we make many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that we scarify with a deep scarification.
And the term scarification in which there is the depth of the elbow only.  (This likely refers to the depth of the cut, using the elbow as a reference point for depth, not an actual cut at the elbow.)
And if the one whose elbow is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, it is necessary that the places for the cupping glasses be anointed with lenitive, aperitive, and resolvent ointments; if it is in summer, such as aloe oil, or violet oil, or sweet almond oil, or poppy seed oil; and if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or blood oil, and similar ones.
And if the superfluity is thick and cold, then let the oil be amaranth oil, or balsamita oil, or aloe oil, or anise oil, and similar ones.
But if the patient has wide pores and tender flesh, then the use of oil should be prohibited, and for such a person the areas for cupping should be washed with vinegar or cold water of solanum, or with poppy water, or with bordelages water, and similar ones.
And the one whose blood is of much humidity, wash his cupping areas with sharp vinegar, or with myrtle and sumac water, and similar ones.
And for the one whose superfluities are thick, wash his cupping areas with old wine, or with water of sanquis (possibly a type of herb, perhaps synonymous with majorana/marjoram), or with a decoction of anise, or of chamomile, and similar ones.
And the cupping should be performed before a bath; rather, it should be administered about an hour or two before the bath, and he should sleep after the cupping.
What is it that the person to be cupped or bled should do before cupping?
It is necessary to consider at the beginning:  if the person to be cupped or bled is choleric and has sharp blood and inflammation, then he should take cooling things, such as sour pomegranates, and endive in sharp vinegar and lettuce, and chickpea flour (or a chickpea-based preparation), and julep, and similar things.
And give him foods such as chicken, and meats of domestic birds, and foods seasoned with sour things, and the like.
And for one whose complexion is cold, he should drink syrup of almonds, or syrup of chickpea flour and sumac, and very fragrant wine drunk between autumn and winter; and he should be advised to eat and drink sparingly, and give him foods such as chicken, hempseed,  peas, small birds, and pigeons.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting, his drink should be greater than his food.
And it is necessary that at certain times some men be given theriac alpharac, or medicines of musk, that is, nutmeg, or celadine before cupping or before bloodletting or afterwards, so that the principal members are comforted and the blood is thinned; and they should not take those things heated.
But the cuppings that are without scarification should be cuppings that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen and gout, and on the navel, in the places of the knees, and the bladder region, for these members do not tolerate scarification on them.
