And it is not desired for these [cuppings] except the attraction of blood from a member, just as we place a cupping glass on the breast in a disease of nasal bleeding; and we use it so that cold, invisible windiness is dissolved from the member, just as we place it on the belly and navel; for it rarifies the member and warms it and relieves the pain because it resolves windiness.
And it is placed on boils when they occur with obstruction or inflammation, for by the virtue of strong attraction it quickly opens the obstruction and eradicates the inflammation from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed on the liver and spleen because of windiness enclosed in them.
And these cuppings are administered empty with suction alone; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in diseases of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and is larger; that is, hot water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been cooked.
Method of administering a cupping glass with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, as we have formed it; and let the length of the cupping glass be half a palm; and let it be on the side of him, in the place that is in the circumference, half of it, let the hole be small according to the quantity that an awl enters it.
Let it be made of bronze, copper, or citrus [wood?], with thick edges, smooth, even, and polished so that it does not injure the limb by its placement upon it.
And let there be a transverse bar of bronze or iron in the middle of it, where a candle with fire is placed.
And let this cupping glass be larger than the one we have described; and the smaller one smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cupping glasses for infants and thin people are different from the cupping glasses for young people and those of large bodies.
And the method of placing this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to extend the wick with a decent wood fire before a small wax candle, and place that on the middle of the transverse bar in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around the lower part, so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then it is placed on the patient's limb, and the finger is placed over the hole we mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glasses and do not need to remove the finger and let the vapor escape through that hole, then the cupping glass is immediately dissolved (i.e., the suction released).
And then ignite the wick according to its manner, and repeat this if you need to.
But in cupping that is administered in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse bar or hole, and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and it is placed on the limb alone.
And this is its form: And this cupping glass, the larger it is, the better it is, because it can receive a greater quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leech's are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs in which the placement of cupping glasses is not possible, because of their smallness, such as the finger and gums and similar things; or on a limb devoid of flesh, such as the finger and nose and things like them.
And the quality of using those leeches is that you should find leeches that are in clean fresh waters free of putrefaction.
And then let them be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and so that no substance remains in their bellies; and then let the body be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and with cupping; and then rub the patient's limb until it reddens; and then they should be placed upon it.
And when they are full and fall off.
And if it is possible to draw blood from the area with a cupping glass, it is even more beneficial.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and then with warm water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged near the application of the leeches, and it weeps (or oozes), then you dip a linen cloth in cold water, and place that on top until the weeping stops.
And if the bleeding is excessive, then sprinkle on it crushed vitriol, or gallnuts and similar styptic substances, until the bleeding is stopped; or place halved, scorched beans on the area and leave them there until the beans stick to the area, and the bleeding is stopped.
And it is necessary, if needed, to reapply leeches, when it is possible to change those (already applied).
And if leeches are prohibited or unavailable, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the area with a needle until blood comes out; and then apply (leeches), for they readily attach themselves when they sense any blood.
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle on them a little crushed aloes, or salt, or ash, and they will fall off immediately.
Thus begins the prologue of the third part: On Restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and accidental dislocations in the bones.
O, son, know that foolish physicians and commoners boast of this chapter, those who never looked at the books of the Ancients nor read a single letter of them.
And for this reason, this practice is kept private by the learned in our land; and I have never forgotten this in any way; and I have not applied this which I have learned except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and study on their understanding, until I extracted the knowledge from them.
And then I applied it to experiment and study throughout the length of my life.
And I have described in this chapter all that my knowledge contains and upon which my experience has been applied; since I have abbreviated it for you and freed it from lengthy occupation; and I have abbreviated it to the point of brevity; and I have shown it to the point of clarity; and I have formed in it many forms of instruments that are administered in it, as is done by the addition of explanation, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter One.
Summary and collection of the essentials of bone fractures, those which must be prefaced before we begin the recollection of broken and diverse members, each one individually. Therefore, I will say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters on the understanding of which the artisan is forced to stand at the beginning and on the truth of those; that is, you and those who are ready to learn or teach this noble art, others of you.
And therefore I say that when a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall happens to someone, then you should begin with phlebotomy or purging, or both together, if no cause prohibits it, such as weakness of strength, or if the one to whom it happens is a child, or an old person in decrepitude, or if the time is one of intense heat or intense cold.
And then let him be content with a diet of cool herbs and the meat of birds and rabbits; and we prohibit fatty meats and filling foods and any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are certain of a hot abscess, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to their former regimen, with which they are accustomed.
And so, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be fed with nutritious foods, very nourishing and thick and heavy, in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat boiled in water, and rice, and heads [possibly referring to specific cuts of meat], and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and similar things, and with this regimen the coagulation of the fracture is made more easily and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in their first youth and in the decrepit, cannot be rejoined and consolidated according to their nature because of the desiccation of their limbs.
However, nature adjusts and applies to the broken bone from every part some substance similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres and binds it until it is completely conjoined and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, like fire at the beginning, until nothing prevents it from its work.
And for this reason it is necessary that you give the patient foods in which there is heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other members are different from each other.
And I come with a recollection of every type of fracture, explained in its own distinct chapter.
Many diversities of fracture in itself. And the types of bone fracture are diversified in themselves; for there is an arched fracture, besides those that happen in the stars; and sometimes a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the shaft and splinters and additions proceeding and not proceeding, as is done in a quill when it is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And to each type belongs its own specific treatment or torment in its restoration, as will be recalled distinctly in its proper place.
Many signs of bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: its crookedness, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and that which we perceive when we grasp it with our hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor is anything felt by your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here.
Perhaps there is a crookedness or small fissure; then you should not move it with your hand and with complete compression.  Rather, place on it some medicine, of which mention will be made later at the appropriate moment, which is suitable for the location; and then let it be held tightly by a subtle bandage.
And know that if the place is curved and not split in two, or otherwise damaged in such a way that it has not lost its connection with its corresponding part, then you must begin at once to rectify it before an abscess develops there from the injury.
And if a hot abscess develops there, then leave it for some days until the abscess subsides; and then dress it according to whatever light and skillful method you can.
And know that the restoration and healing of [the injured area, understood from the context] is easier than of a bone in which a wound has occurred.
And then bind it as the later instructions will indicate.
But if the bone is injured, then extension of that broken limb is necessary from two sides, if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two cords, or with one cord and your hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb in a dressing that follows its natural shape, while the broken parts of the limb are extended; then you must carefully return those [displaced] parts to their places in whatever way you can.
And be careful, according to your strength, that you do not cause pain or injury to the patient through your operation; and carefully, according to your ability, apply one bone to its companion bone according to their best alignment.
And it is necessary that at that time you skillfully touch that [area] with your hand, and if you see anything amiss there, correct it and adjust it to the best of your ability.
And avoid excessive extension and compression, as many foolish physicians do, who often cause a hot abscess by their operation, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen happen through their operations many times.
And the quality of the binding of the quieted limb is that which I have recounted; so that you may know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their largeness and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the finger and the wrist and similar, then the wrapping should be of very fine soft cloth.
But for those that are large, such as the thigh or the leg, the wrapping should be broad and firm, because a broad bandage connects the large limb and holds it on all sides with even binding, so that no blemish enters it.
Then begin, after your equalization evacuation, to place a bandage suitable for this, over the place of the fracture with three or four wrappings according to what the limb requires; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
And then come to the uppermost part of the fracture site, and tighten less than you did your binding with which you bound the fracture site. And then extend yourself with the wrapping of the fracture site a little, and soften the binding little by little, until you take a large portion of the site.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with a wrapping, and then come with the wrappings to the lower part of the fracture; and let your work be in the tightening of the wrapping and in softening it as we have said in the first wrapping above.
And then place between the wrappings some lion's wool or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture is padded, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not place anything there.
And then place another bandage over it, and then carefully apply well-made splints over these bandages immediately, if there is no inflammation or hot abscess in the place, and then place on it what dries the abscess and removes the inflammation; and leave it for some days; and then you tighten the splints on it.
And let the splints be of half a cane, shaved and well-formed wisely; or let them be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint that is placed over the fracture be a little thicker and smoother than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to what is suitable for the limb in size; and then tighten over the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then tie it over with thread neatly as we have said of tightening, and it is so that your tightening is greater according to the place of the fracture, and the farther it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the practice of some fools placing their threads of twisted rope, it is a great error, when the tightening is not even.
And similarly, very fine threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve with them what you want by tightening, and it is not necessary that there be less than one finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient is hurt by the ends of the splints near the binding in those places, then place under those some carded flax or wool so that it does not injure him.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a disruption occurs in the bone, and I will return to this in that chapter according to its particular nature.
And know that not every broken limb is immediately splinted on the first day; the reason is that, when the limb is large, you should not apply splints until approximately five or seven days or more, according to your assessment of the risk of a hot abscess forming.
And this is the method of the plasters with which the Ancients made restorations, which were applied to fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
A method of common plaster, a shortened version for the restoration of fractures, suitable for many complex situations and especially for women and children, because it is something that neither heat nor cold overcomes: it is to take some mill dust, which is the pith of flour that settles on the walls of the mill from the movement of the millstones.
And make it like this, except that you sift it with egg white, and make its consistency neither thick nor thin; and use it in this way.
Another method of plaster for restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take of asafoetida, of labdanum, of acacia, of elecampane, and wild pomegranate root, of each one dram (x); of myrrh and aloes, half a dram (v); of aloes wood, two drams (xx); all should be crushed, sifted, and mixed with aloe juice or egg white if the patient's constitution is hot; then use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores slightly broken bones, and is beneficial to many people.
Another method of plaster which helps fractures and sprains: take wild pomegranate root and marshmallow root, one dram (x); of myrrh and aloes, half a dram (v); of acacia, six drams (vi); of Armenian bole, two drams (xx).
All should be crushed in a mortar with much grinding, and sifted, and mixed with water or egg white and applied.
A method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and if pain occurs in them, and for fissures and fractures: take unwashed wool and submerge it in sour wine and boiled oil and apply it to the affected area.
But this plaster does not have restorative virtue; it rather draws out hot abscesses and expels pain.
Another method of plaster that restores a broken bone: take leaves of the wild fig and leaves of wild poppy; crush everything and make a plaster from them.
A shortened method of plaster administered for the restoration of bone fracture and when you want resolution of the remaining abscess: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile, and violet flowers, oat flour, of all equal parts; all should be crushed and mixed with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then mix it with moist coriander water or with water and use it.
Another method of plaster, stronger in resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of a limb: take roots of marshmallow and flaxseed, and pomegranate, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, all in equal parts; all should be crushed and mixed with willow water or with fresh water or with wine, all this according to the quality of the limb and the restoration of its color.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration, but rather a cerate made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommends that it be of medium consistency, neither too thick nor too thin.
But in Wales it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb during its restoration, and the things which cause desiccation with a little heat, such as myrrh, incense, and similar things to those which we have mentioned.
Method for a plaster that relieves injury and pain: take millet, bran, shaved human hair or bird feathers, and marshmallow, and salt, in equal parts; they should be crushed and sifted and mixed, and a plaster should be made.
But the amount of time the bandage should remain and when it should be loosened, is that you should consider whether pain or itching has occurred, or if the bone is broken or dislocated; then you should not unbind it for many days. And if vehement pain, itching, or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should carefully loosen or remove it at that time, and remove the plaster from it.
And take linen cloth and a moist sea sponge, and immerse it in lukewarm water, and with it wash the area until the itching ceases and the pain is soothed; and then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place over it clean wool soaked in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it on for a night until you are sure of a hot abscess, and that the inflammation of the limb has subsided, and its pain is eased.
And then repeat that subtle binding and small plaster, and do not tighten it as much as your first constriction, but subtly with that until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, redness, pain, inflammation, and all the symptoms have completely subsided, and you need a plaster and binding, then you should repeat it as you did in the beginning, equally.
And if none of the conditions we have mentioned occur in the limb, then do not loosen it until after three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days, depending on what appears to you upon loosening the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh over it.
And then adjust the binding more than in your first binding, and adjust it to the thickening of the patient's flesh, as his recovery proceeds.
But if you see the fracture site drying out and becoming thinner than it should, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it; therefore, bathe it with lukewarm water every time you loosen it; and do this every three days; and loosen the binding a little, and by this operation, nourishment will return to the limb, and it will heal easily.
But what foolish restorers of a fractured limb do, when it is not properly set initially, and is set crookedly, is an error according to their practice, and a great risk, and if it were straightforward, the Ancients would have magnified this in their books and would have operated with this.
And I have not seen anyone of them do this, wherefore it is more reasonable that you do not do this.
Chapter Two: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow from a sword, and the blow breaks the entire bone into splinters, which are below the bone, just as it does in a stick, and for that reason this type is called *ascial* (axial?); or the sword cuts a part of the bone, and cuts its flesh and does not penetrate to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called *levatio absoluta* (complete elevation?).
And the wound from these two fractures is either large or small, and there is fracture which is comminution or attrition; and its cause is a blow with a blunt object or a fall on a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the periosteum which is below the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the wound from these two fractures is either large or small.
And there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone, as fine as a hair, and for this reason this type is called a hairline fracture.
And there is a fracture that is caused either by a fall or by a blow with a stone and similar things, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter the inner parts, and it is made in a concave place, just as happens to the sides of a ram when a blow is struck and a part of it is driven inwards.
And this happens especially in the bodies of moist bones, such as the skulls of infants.
And in all these types of comminuted fractures, the pieces are like separated stars. And I will come with the repositioning of all of this in its place.
And we will know all these types of fracture by detection of those and by the inquiry of their state, with probes and removal of corrupt flesh which is upon them.
And a hairline fracture is known because it is discovered on the bone, and is cleaned, and examined with a probe, for the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider at the beginning the accidents of the patient, and then if you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification concerning fear, such as vomiting of bile, extension, and loss of consciousness, and loss of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and swelling of the eyes and redness of them, and similar to them of the accidents in the major dispositions, without doubt it is not healed.
And if you see no unusual accidents, you expect health, then you begin his treatment.
The reason is that if the injured person has come to you at the beginning as injured, and this is in the winter days, then it is necessary that you study the setting of the bone before the fourteenth day, according to all disposition.
And if it is in the summer days, then it is necessary that you take care to set the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone is corrupted by the panniculus (flesh), and that the accidents that we have recounted occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the panniculus covering the brain, and is with small particles and attrition, then it is necessary that you incise the comminuted and atrophied part, as I shall recount to you.
And it is that you shave the head of the injured patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as much as possible according to the shape of the wound, and that it is light on the patient.
And if you encounter upon detection of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then you oppose it with what is necessary, and it is that you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess is sedated, and you are sure of the flow of blood; and then begin the encirclement of the bone and its ablation.
And this should be according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with a narrow incisor, and this is its form:  And then administer another incisor, this one, a little wider than that one; and this is its form:
And then administer yet another, wider incisor than the second.
And it is necessary that you have a number of different incisors, of which some are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and be sharpened at the ends, and be of Indian or tempered iron.
And use them easily in percussion on the incisor so that you do not strike the head and injure it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary that you perforate its circumference before you administer the incisors with the drills, which are called non-penetrating drills.
And they are not named thus except because those do not reach the limit of the skull bone next to it, because the drill has a round end or edge on that which is below its sharp head, similar to a small edge or circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills a large number, each of which suits the thickness of each bone, so that you have present for all parts of the skull an end which is sharp, the quantity in length and shortness being according to the quantity of the thickness of that part.
And this is the form of three types of drills: The method of perforating the circumference of the broken bone is that you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone is penetrated; and then move the drill to another place, and place what is between each hole the amount of the thickness of a ray or thereabouts.
And then incise with incisors that which is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as you can on that end for lightness, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other instrument, using tools which you will have prepared for this, such as thin pincers and forceps.
And you must take great care that the incisor does not touch any part of the brain.
And so when you have encircled the bone by perforating it, and it is separated from the brain if it is connected to it, and you have extracted it, you must scrape and smooth its roughness with another instrument similar to an incisor, but it must be thinner and more delicate than the other incisors.
And if some small and splintered bone fragments remain, then remove them easily with the instruments you have; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments which we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is easier, far from frightening, which Gualia described and praised greatly; and this is his account: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin by uncovering the part of the bone at the place where the fracture is most severe and most evident, until, when you have uncovered that part and have placed under it the end of this slow and gentle incisor, of which this is the form:
And it is so that the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is divided in two lengthwise, like a wedge; so that the lenticular part is applied to the brain with its face, the sharp part cutting into the bone.
And then strike the incisor on one side with a small mallet until it cuts through all the bone easily as it is turned, and you are certain of the periosteum that no accident which you fear occurs in any way, even if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, even if he were sleeping.
Then, if any matter attached to the bone remains in the periosteum in some places of the bone, cut it away with the lenticular end of that incisor, and it will be removed easily, for it is removed without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the periosteum, and that which is separated or removed from the bone is its surface, and roughness and small splinters remain, then it is necessary to scrape away that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps, of which you have prepared a number of different sizes so that it is possible for you to apply in each place the most suitable and most appropriate of them according to what your operation requires for that broken bone and its shape.
And it is necessary that you apply in your scraping, with which you scrape the bone initially, the broadest of those rasps, and then use progressively the more subtle ones, and do not cease doing this continuously until you come to apply the finest and most subtle of all of them.
But in each of the other small, hair-like fractures and subtle fractures, it is necessary to administer care according to what is required for its correction.
And it is a matter that is not hidden from all those who have studied this art. And I stand by what we have written and shown concerning large fractures.
And if the panniculus (flesh) is retracted when you extract the bone, then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and submerge that in rose oil, and place that over the opening of the wound; and then take another doubled or tripled cloth and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place that over the thin cloth, and do this as gently as you can so that you do not hurt the flesh; and then apply a broad bandage over that, and do not tighten it except to the extent that it holds the wound only.
And leave it with this dressing for one or two days until you are sure of a hot abscess; and then untie it and then administer medicines with which there is superfluous dryness, such as flaxseed, barley flour, and powder of lentils and birthwort, and similar things to those.
And it is necessary that you make a powder from these medicines to be sprinkled on the wound, once they are dry; and universally, apply to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not bite (or irritate).
And take care, according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment is clean; and that there is no dirt from ointment or anything else; nor allow any virulence to gather there; for when it is applied to the panniculus of the brain, it corrupts and poisons it, and thus causes great harm to the patient.
And it affects the brain, as the bone is cleaned, and especially when its care is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, if the patient suffers the accidents that we have mentioned, know that it is pernicious without any doubt.
And if there is blackness not from the medicine that is placed on top, but it is in virtue of that medicine that produces that blackness, then you should take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil, and with these, moisten a cloth, and then place it on the head; and then treat it with the appropriate healing remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two main parts together, or one of those sometimes, because it has two bones, because the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and only suffers from abrasion, crookedness, and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should insert your little finger into his nose, and that you align that fracture inside with your index finger, and with the thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with ease, and be careful that your operation does not cause the patient pain.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it must be set with the end of a rod which has a small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same thing.
And take care with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then after seven or ten days you should treat the resulting abscess with cautery.
And then insert into the end of the nose a small roll of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or place two small wedges, if the fracture is in two parts, and let the wedge be of such a size that it fills the opening of the nose.
And I say that some of the ancient restorers say that the wedges should be soaked in a certain type of plaster and changed every day, but I have not seen this; rather it is necessary that the roll be soaked in egg white mixed with powdered barley; and then leave the wedge until the bone is set and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a wedge, a goose feather, wrapped in linen cloth, is inserted into the nose; and  let it be if you have a more severe nasal fracture and the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, use wedges.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, plaster the nose with cerate or cotton soaked in sour wine and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary to plaster that area externally with sesame flour and cooked linseed powder mixed with egg white; and then place linen cloth over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then it is necessary to incise over them, and extract them with instruments suitable for this; and then sew the incision, and treat it with what causes granulation and consolidation, and with plasters suitable for this.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then it is necessary to treat it with wedges and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, observe then if the fracture is only external and is not broken in two, and is bent only at the inner parts, then its diagnosis is easy.
And it is necessary, therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and push with that the hump of the fracture inwards, easily to the outer parts, with your other hand from the outside of the bone; with this adjust it suitably so.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from two sides according to the straightness until its equalization is possible.
And if there is concussion of teeth with disruption, then bind those which you hope will remain with gold or silver wire or silk; and then place over the broken jaw a cerate, and then place over it a doubled cloth, and place over the cloth a well-made large splint, or a piece of sole leather equal to the length of the jaw; and then tie that on top according to how the bandage is prepared for you and how the application suits, until it is healed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him food of sorbitious drinks.  (Note: "sorbitious" likely refers to a type of easily digestible liquid or broth.)
And if you estimate that the form is already altered in some way, then you should attend to it and untie it on the third day; and then rectify what is already altered and plaster it with pulverized barley, with egg white, or with bean flour, after you have removed the cerate from it, and place wool over the plaster.
And then, when this plaster is attached and fitted, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it; do not untie it until it is healed and the fracture is firm; for many times this fracture is firm in three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its sedation until it is healed, and that abscess is softened.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of the bone is separated or splintered; then you should carefully remove those splinters with whatever is suitable for that in removing them from the wound.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with a fleam according to your need.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then sew the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters that is suitable for it and that will cause it to heal, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Often the clavicle is broken in the front part above the muscle, and this fracture is done in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is so that splinters are present, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is so that the fracture is accompanied by a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the splint and aligns the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
Then bind the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correctly formed, and there is neither too much prominence nor concavity.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's neck a roll of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your needs; and extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the roll with your hand until you bind the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outwards, because it has reached the sternum, then the patient should be lying supine on their neck, and you should place a moderately sized cushion on the muscle.
And the assistant presses the muscle downwards until the clavicle bone that is on the sternum above is elevated, and then you straighten the fracture and bind it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are loose, then you should make an incision over it and extract those splinters easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you carefully make an incision with one of the scalpels that you have prepared for this, after having placed under the clavicle the instrument that serves the bandage; and this instrument is of wood or iron, of which this is the shape:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size or smallness of the bone.
But let its length be as is possible for you in the operation; let it have two ends like a spoon, and one end be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and there is a risk of a hot abscess, then you join the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's incision.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then dip a cloth in sour wine and rose oil and wine, and place it on that.
And above all, ensure the splint is not misplaced over the broken bone; and for this reason it is necessary that you see the patient each day, so that when the bandage is loosened, or you see that the splint has shifted, then you correct this and tighten the bandage.
And then place the patient's pillow under their neck, and place a small cushion under their scrotum at night on top of their pillow; so that with this the injured area is elevated, and thus the fractured collarbone is elevated by the muscle; or let their arm be tied to their neck.
And do not untie the bandage unless an abscess or swelling occurs at the site, until twelve days have passed.
And then renew the plaster if you see the need, and adjust the binding and restore it, and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone is healed and consolidated.
And it is strengthened and healed, according to the case, in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken on its lateral side, and not shattered, only the edges are broken.
And so when the location of the break — and it is broken in the middle of the location — is not known except by touch, then, according to what the form of the fracture is, force the preparation of it and reduce it according to its natural form in every way that is possible for you.
Then place on the area powdered finely ground egg white and lion's wool; then place a poultice of folded cloth; and then place over it a smoothly-ironed linen cloth of fine quality, according to the size of the entire shoulder blade or a little more.
But if the linen is concave in any place on the shoulder blade, then you fill that concavity with lion's wool, and then smooth the linen to make it even.
Then bind it above with a long bandage with a decent constriction and firmness of the linen so that it is not moved from its place.
And examine the bandage each day; and every time that it is loosened, bind it again and tighten it, and smooth the linen if it has been moved from its place.
And have the patient lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 days or in 25.
And so when this number is completed, then you dissolve the bandage, and you should know for certain that this is of the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if it proceeds from the location of the fractured bone and pushes down under the skin, then you incise over it, and remove it, and proceed as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle regarding the cauterization of the abscess if it occurs here.
Chapter 7.
On the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this heals slowly; but its extremities heal much faster because they are scarred and comminuted.
And of its accidents, when the middle of the foot is broken, it inclines towards the inner parts and causes great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, strong fever, and vomiting blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is evident and not hidden from feeling.
And its restoration is that the patient should be supine on his back, and a cushion placed between his shoulders; and then press on his muscles, and adjust his bones with hands from both sides, and be meticulous in the operation and in the alignment in every way possible and appropriate, until the bone's shape is restored as it should be.
Then apply a plaster and lint, and place over it a thin splint of willow or hazel, or something similar in lightness, after you have wrapped it in cloth; and be careful in binding it over the broken bone so that it is not moved; and apply the bandage in a circular fashion to the back sometimes, and tighten it with a decent constriction; and check the bandage frequently, and tighten it when it is loosened.
And if necessity requires loosening of the bandage because of corrosion or itching, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the area, then you should examine and unbind it and remove the plaster and rectify what has occurred by appropriate treatment; and reapply the plaster, if you see fit; and adjust the bandage until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
On the restoration of the ribs when they are fractured.
Know that fracture of the ribs occurs only in the thicker parts, that is, the two; and in the extremities, from the front part, no crushing occurs because they are cartilaginous. And knowledge of it is not hidden from the feeling or investigation of it through the fingers.
And the restoration should be thus: equalize the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until it is joined as needed, and then plaster and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if it is necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs is inclined towards the inner parts, then the patient suffers vehement pain and a stabbing sensation, like the stabbing pain that occurs in pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane; and it causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting much blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And already the Ancients were ingenious in that with many inventions.
And of them it is said: it is necessary that you give the patient foods that produce inflation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we should avoid this so that it does not lightly cause a hot abscess, if it does not already exist; and if it already exists, then it already helps with that.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed on top and that it should draw with force; and this is similar to reasoning, but however, it is feared that the cupping glass will draw superfluities to the place because of the weakness.
And some of them said: the place should be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandage is even as it wraps that according to roundness; and then the patient should be treated with a pleurisy cure of food and medicine.
And if a vehement cause constricts the patient which he does not suffer, and the bone pierces the skin with a harmful puncture, threatening the patient, then it is necessary that we incise the place, uncover the broken rib, and then place on it an instrument that preserves the  ciph (likely referring to a specific surgical instrument or technique, perhaps related to bone setting), the recollection of which is previously mentioned, and that we incise the bone easily, and extract it.
And then we should join the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an internal abscess occurs, then you should take and mix powders in rose oil and apply it to the place, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And let him be supine on the side on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the spine and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and then it leads to their attrition, and similarly to the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider this: for if you see his hands already softened, stupefied, and dead, and he cannot move them, nor extend nor flex them, and when you constrain them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and he feels no pain in them, then know that he is not healed, and in most dispositions it is mortal.
And if he moves those and feels constriction in them with the fingers and puncture, then know that the marrow of the bone is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if he shows in the vertebrae of the neck something similar to that and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider his feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, what happened to the hands happens to them, and moreover when he lies on his back, he experiences flatulence and defecation without his will, and sometimes he is lying on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then know that it is mortal, and then do not work on his cure.
But if none of those things happen, the cause is better and lighter.
And the treatment for him is that you perform a cauterization of the hot abscess, that you place on the affected or reddened vertebra  pure olive oil, or with cooked egg yolks; apply this several times a day until the hot abscess is calmed.
And then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and bind it tightly; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and not to sleep on the part where he feels pain until he is healed.
And if it happens that because of the attrition of the bone splinters or any cause that it is already separated from it, then it is necessary that you cut it at the joint and that you remove that bone; and then join the two lips of the wound with sutures if it is large; and then treat it with some incarnative ointment until it is healed.
But if the posterior part of the hip bone is broken, and it is the end of the hip joint, then it is necessary that you insert the index finger of your left hand into the rectum, and that you align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by second intention; and then apply a plaster and bandage, if you have them, and then bind it.
And if you feel broken splinters in it, then you should cut into that area, and remove them, and treat the wound with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the hip.
Hip bones are rarely broken, and if they are broken, then their fracture is only a communication in the extremities of those bones, and they are incised lengthwise and incline towards the inner parts; and the patient experiences pain and throbbing in the area, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is the shape of it.
And if the fracture is only in its extremity, then you set it according to what is prepared for you by equal setting until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or inclines towards the lower parts, then you make the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. And then when you have set it, place a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or whalebone over that, and bind it tightly so that you do not fear a puncture of the fracture or movement of the splint.
And fill the concavity with something that fills it until it takes the binding according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease the extension of it and its restoration until the hot abscess is settled, and place upon it that which settles it as has been previously described; and then return to the restoration of it, and bind it as is necessary.
And if it happens in the bones that they are pierced or comminuted (shattered), then you extract them.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the collarbone (clavicle).
The collarbone is that which is between the ribcage and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a wooden splint of medium thickness according to this form:
And let two bandages be tied at both ends, and then let it be suspended from an elevated place.
And seat the patient on a stool; and then lay his broken arm over the splint until it reaches his wrist, in the middle of which the curve of the splint should be attached.
And then suspend some heavy object from it, or let the assistant pull it downwards; and then let the physician set the fracture with his hands, until the fracture is properly reset.
And the other method is that the patient be laid supine on his back, and suspend the arm from his neck with a bandage; and then command two assistants that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other hold what is below it; and each of them pull in his direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then bind the fracture with a bandage, and over that with another bandage, and let each of the two assistants pull in opposite directions.
And so if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the wrist and the other below the fracture near the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed on that spot and on that elbow.
Swallow it easily and without effort, and then, when it is swallowed as needed and applied with proper application, bind it unless a hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day, and place on it soiled wool soaked in sour wine and rose oil until the abscess subsides, and then you bind it.
And the way to bind it is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place your open hand on its muscle.
And place the cloth and bind the arm to the support so that the arm is in a steady position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And it is necessary that you place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then bind over the splints with the bandage which I described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is necessary that your bandage over the place of the fracture be tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And so if you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you proceed.
And if you fear a hot abscess, then you leave the bandage and splints in place until the seventh day, as we have said.
And then consider the bandage in all parts for three days so that itching or inflammation does not occur in the area; or it is forbidden for food to reach the limb due to the tightness of the bandage; and therefore rectify all this as we have said.
But if you are confident that none of this will happen, then do not untie the bandage until three days or more.
And let the patient's sleeping position be on their side, and their hands should be on their stomach.
And place over the injured area a poultice full of wool evenly, and check every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandage is not softened; therefore rectify all this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have previously mentioned, in such a way that the food is light at the beginning until the [condition/wound] begins to tighten; and then it is necessary to thicken his food.
For it is customary for the injured area and his leg to be bandaged for forty days; and then it is necessary that they be unbound and that a bath be administered and it be medicated with ointments which rectify this.
And if the fracture is badly bruised, then you should not untie the bandage and splints from that until one month or until two months.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the restoration of a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils; of which one is small, and it is that which is next to the little finger, and the other is large and it is that which is placed below the little finger, on the lower side.
And very easily the larger focile is broken alone, or the smaller, and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small focile is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the focile is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the small focile above, then it is convenient for the physician, during its restoration, to apply slight extension easily until it is set.
But if the large focile is what is broken, then it is necessary to apply more forceful extension.
And if both fociles are broken together, then you must apply the extension much more strongly.
And it is necessary that you position your hand during its restoration and extension, stretched out on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be on the upper parts, higher than all the other fingers, and the little finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient should be lying on a sloping chair.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at his height or level, so that the patient does not suffer strain.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extend it from above similarly.
Then the physician should manipulate the bone until he reduces it to the best possible position.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are splinters, then you must forcefully reduce all pieces to their place with your skill.
And if a separated splinter appears in it that punctures the skin and there is no hope in its restoration, then you should saw it off and remove it according to the method which we have described above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already dealt with that in a separate chapter, and therefore take the treatment from there.
And if an abscess occurs near the beginning of your reduction, then you should line a cloth with a cerate made of rose oil and white wax, and it should be of medium consistency between thick and thin, and bind the cloth tightly over it with a subtle binding until the abscess has subsided; and then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from powdered millet with egg white, and then apply poultices; and let the poultice that is placed over the fracture be thicker and stronger.
And know that the number of poultices for the arm is increased in more serious cases, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
Then apply your binding over the site of the fracture more firmly and more tightly, and if the binding is closer to the parts above the fracture or to the parts below it, apply the binding a little more loosely, as was explained at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist cloths, and not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is bound should be of medium thickness, neither too thin nor too thick, as we have already recommended.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. And if any condition occurs which requires correction, such as itching which affects the limb, then you must bathe the limb with warm water until that itching ceases.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the binding.
And if the binding is now softened and the bone is now healed and similar to the others, then correct all that with your skill.
And consider further if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb by an excessive tightness of the binding, for then it is necessary that you loosen it a little and leave it so for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient does not experience any of the conditions we have mentioned, then it is not necessary that the binding be loosened for about twenty days or thereabouts.
And then let the patient's hand be suspended by its own power of agitated movement, and make the patient sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or it is quickly restored in twenty-eight days, and all this depends on the constitution and the disposition of the vital force.
Chapter 13.
On the restoration of a fracture of the wrist and fingers.
Know that fractures rarely occur in the wrist and finger joints; rather, they usually suffer attrition.
And when a fracture or attrition occurs in the hand, then the patient should lie down and place a cushion in front of him to support it, then place his hand on that extended surface.
Then the assistant extends the broken bones, and the physician aligns them until they are joined with a good connection.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and lint, unless a hot abscess occurs; then apply a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and wrap it in linen.
But if the fracture is in the inner parts of the palm, then make a kind of ball of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold it with his fractured palm; then it should be firmly bound with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather with cloth, in which there should be lightness, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and tighten it as needed.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a bandage above and another below on the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the splint, as the hand is held in place between the fingers with ligatures.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be bandaged as needed and then bound to the palm; and if you wish, place a small, straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the index, or in the ring finger, or in the little finger, then it should be bandaged and bound to the finger next to it; or all should be bound together continuously, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and near that, the disposition of a hot abscess and oppose — with your operation — what is necessary when any of these things occur, according to the repeated narration of it.
Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and is revealed to the senses according to how it is turned towards the front and the more superior part.
And its restoration is done thus: a bandage is tightened above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on their belly and face.
And then the physician extends the whole bandage to its part according to its quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the thigh, then let the bandage be close to the knee on the side of the patella, so that the extension falls upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the knee, then let the bandage be made close to the knee, and let the extension be to the parts below.
And then the physician joins the bone with both his hands until he reduces it to its natural appearance and the bone is joined by a good conjunction; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs there, then you leave it for a few days until the abscess has matured, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made thus:  You should wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, and double it two or three times, removing any excess; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And place a long strip of cloth between the thigh and the leg near the knee on the lower parts, and let the ends of the cloth come from the upper part of both sides, and then wrap around the leg and thigh that which is superfluous from the bandage.
And then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage is secure.
And then begin with a firm bandage in the middle over the fracture site with three or four wraps, and the further you move away from the fracture site, the looser, lighter, and softer your bandage should be.
And then extend the two ends of the cloth to the lower parts until they reach the ankle, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not loosened from its place.
And then leave the bandage on, provided that there is no corruption, hot abscess, swelling, or similar conditions affecting the limb; and if any of these conditions should occur, then loosen the bandage, and rectify the situation completely, as we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone in the bone that protrudes, then you must reposition it as best you can; and if not, cover it and keep it in place, and treat it with what has been previously mentioned until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened further after the splints are applied, as we have described in the treatment of the arm.
However, in the restoration done in this manner, the patient does not experience lameness; and if it is restored alone, without the other leg being supported, then it is necessary that he will limp from that leg always.
And know that the thigh is bound for forty days, or a little more or a little less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other conditions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap heals slowly, but very often causes attrition (wear and tear/friction).
But if a fracture occurs, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a wound, and all of it is accompanied by sensation.
And its restoration is such that what is broken of its parts should be aligned with said parts until it is joined and united as much as possible by equalization, ease and fabrication (i.e., setting the bone).
Then apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you need one; and bind it tightly above with a suitable bandage.
And then consider all the precautions that we have recommended in other fractures, such as hot poultices and similar things, so that every accident is countered with what is suitable for him until he is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is large and is called by the name of the leg; and the other is thin, and is called the fibula.
And whatever kinds of fractures happen to the two bones of the arm, the same happens to the two bones of the leg, and therefore its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and its operation is one and the same.
And if the two bones are broken together, the leg is injured in all its parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is injured in the front parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it below, and it is one of those things which are not easily treated by you.
Then administer extension, setting, and bandaging of the splints according to the same method.
However, if the leg fracture is large, having many splinters, the extension should be less and lighter, and relieve it with your skill.
And in the leg there is some cause for surgery similar to the arm, and when you align the splints and complete your operation, then you will take pieces of pine wood which are placed under the parts which are placed on the soles, and these are made suitably from palm branches; choose those of small thickness, and let them not be very slender, and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the very bottom.
Then wrap around each [splint] a cloth in two layers according to its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side, and they should be from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
Then bind the two splints in three places: at the two ends and in the middle, for with this fastening the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and remains in a good position.
And now a small wooden splint has been provided, according to the length of the leg; and let the leg be placed on it so that it retains that position.
And especially it is necessary that you do this in a fracture, and when with this there is a proper wound.
Then examine the leg each day and study it carefully, and watch it for abscesses and inflammation or other similar problems; and if any of these problems occur, then apply to it what is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the talus and the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the whole talus does not often occur; but fractures of the bones of the foot do occur sometimes; and fractures of the toes occur rarely, and what happens to them is more often attrition during mishaps.
And if a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and sometimes those bones are elevated above each other, then the patient should place his foot on the ground prepared in this way as before.
Then place one foot on what is elevated of those bones, and then press down on them, and squeeze, and pull them until they return to their places.
Then apply a plaster and lint on top, and place under the sole of the foot a small board to which two flat pieces are attached.
Then bind that with the foot with a descending bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and with other things of which you have need.
And when three or four days have passed for him, then you untie the bandage, for you will find the bones joined, or whether they are fractured or dislocated, and the operation on them is as you know.
But if someone says they are fractured, then restore them, and pull them as I have recounted to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint on the fractured finger according to the length of the finger, and let it be a little longer than it; and place under the foot that board which I have recounted to you, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
But if what is fractured of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its size wrapped in linen cloth, and bind the board to the lowest part of the foot, and let it rest on one of the ends of the board outside the sole of the foot so that it holds it with good retention.
And it is convenient for you that you do not forget that you consider all the things which I have said to you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have mentioned; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is appropriate.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the woman's hip and of the pubic bone and of the man's penis.
When the hip of the woman is fractured, then make her lie down in a low bed, and gently incline her to one side, and let her be held from behind.
And then the midwife fills the hip with cotton, and it is made in her hip like a ball; and then the woman is covered, and her leg is raised little by little, because all this is useless until the hip joint is like a ball and the fractured bone returns [to its place].
And then place powders on her leg, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton easily and then she urinates, and reduce and return it according to the manner in which you set it at the beginning, and then return to the first powdering.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and attach a reed tube to its opening; and introduce the whole bladder into her hip, and then inflate it through the tube until the bladder is inflated inside the hip, for this will return the fracture [to its place].
And then it is filled with cotton and remains for several days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and healing that which we have recommended for the hip bone.
And that which is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures which occur rarely, especially for one who studies and uses and understands this my book well; for this fracture indicates to you the best methods for its restoration and repair, and therefore understand well.
But in the case of a broken penis, take a hen's gizzard and place the penis inside it; and then place a cloth bandage over it, and let it be wrapped, and then let it remain for three days or so, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractured bones when there is a wound.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with a wound, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar to those, that you carefully attend to and immediately phlebotomize him if the conditions for phlebotomy indicate it as we have said above.
And if the wound flows with blood, then you must attend to its retention so that you pulverize crushed egg whites over it, if no other cause is present to you.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that very day, and do not prolong it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you should leave its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, uncovered, then you must carefully reduce it and align it with your hands easily and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the alignment of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands with this instrument; and it is an instrument which is made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight fingers, and the width of which is according to the size of the wound; and for this it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of these, according to the quantity needed for this in the treatment of every kind of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it be of little thickness so that it is not bent by the compression on it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp end which has a margin at its ends; and let the upper part of it be of increasing thickness, and from the middle of it to the ends.
And this is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a small spatula.
Because it is necessary that its sharp end be placed raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And push that in at once until the bone returns, and it is set with some equalization.
And then strive to equalize the ends of the fracture between them, and if the broken end of the bone is thin, and does not receive the instrument well, then you must incise the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone entirely with what we have recounted, then incise it with whatever is suitable from among the incisors which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient finds vehement and persistent pain, then know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and then do this because you will confer great benefit to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then submerge a cloth in black styptic wine, especially if it is summer, and do not place on the wound cerate or anything else containing oil, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption there.
And then administer stitches at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you incise the opening with scissors and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not constrict the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish physicians have done this, and caused death or a corrosive and suppurating illness, that is, gangrene, in their patients. And if your constriction is light, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some bad accidents which we have recounted, and you find pain in the place, then it is not fitting that you place stitches there, but apply a bandage of hard cloths instead of stitches and bind it with those.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to fester, then you remove the cloth which you had placed on it with wine, and then administer poultices, ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it behooves you to unbind the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and closed, and the limb should be positioned in such a way that the pus flows down from it to the lower parts easily.
If many days pass and the wound is neither consolidated nor is the pus removed from it, then know that here are signs of small [insects/worms], for which you must examine the wound with a probe. And of those signs, those that are separated, remove and extract them; those which are not separated and which impede the limb and cause pain, you are compelled to incise them as far as is possible for you.
And if a swelling or ulcer occurs in the wound, that is, a stye, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary that you treat each of these with what is appropriate for it from the treatment which has been described previously in its chapter.
And this is what my discourse should focus on, and you should pay attention to this point: when a large bone is fractured, such as the bone of the thigh and the hip and similar large bones, and you should not attempt its traction or extraction, because many times death results from this; rather, leave it until it festers, for it will fall away by itself quite quickly within twenty or thirty days; and then, if you deem it necessary, treat the wound, otherwise leave it.
Chapter 20.
Chapter 20: On the treatment of swelling that occurs at the site of a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs at the site of a healing fracture, especially if it is near the joints, and it deforms the shape of the limb; and it quickly prevents the limb from its natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and apply medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, incense, myrrh, cerecloth, and acacia, and similar substances;  take some or all of these, and mix them with astringent wine, or egg white, or sour wine, and apply this to the swelling on a piece of linen, and bind it tightly.
And leave the bandage on without loosening it for many days; then unbind it and reapply another, until the swelling is reduced; or apply a lead plate tightly, because lead has the property of reducing anything swollen in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already hardened and become constricted, and necessity demands its removal carefully, then incise it in the most suitable place, and cut away the excess and prominent part, or scrape it with a sharp instrument until it is reduced; and treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Chapter 21. Of the restoration of a fracture when it is set and the limb remains thin and weak, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is set, and afterward the limb remains thin and weak, this is caused by many things: one of which is an excessive loosening of the ligaments and their binding not done as it should be; or by excessive tightness of the bandages so that nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb; or by excessive and superfluous embrocation (application of a medicated liquid); or by excessive movement not at the proper time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and their weakness.
And the cure for this is to give the patient nourishing food and make their heart cheerful so that their blood is increased; and administer a bath; and instill joy and merriment in them.
