And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws much nourishment to it, or continue embrocation with warm water, until nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Chapter 22: Of the treatment of fractured bones when they are set crookedly and the proper operation is prevented.
When, in a limb already set, there is near healing from crookedness and swelling at the fractured bone and node, and this causes deformity of the limb, but nevertheless the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not accept the word of one who thinks that limb is newly fractured.
But there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land; and this operation is most blameworthy, leading to great fear, the sign of which is deadly.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed with lukewarm water in which are boiled emollient herbs, such as mallow leaves and roots, melilot, and similar ones; and a poultice should be made with a softening poultice, such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of mallow root should be prepared and mixed with goose grease or olive oil, and applied as a poultice; or ripe figs should be taken and crushed with pigeon dung, and similar things among those medicines called consolidating remedies.
And sometimes it is resolved with gentle, frequent rubbing with moistened hands, and movement of the limb in all directions should be administered at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become hardened and turned to bone, and recourse to treatment with iron is necessary, then the most prominent part should be removed and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and that which superfluously swells from the node of the bone itself should be incised with fine scalpels, and relief should be provided with care and diligence.
Chapter 23.
Concerning discourse on dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints from their place, whereby movement is prevented, the shape of the limb is distorted, and great pain and injury are caused to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs to someone, then it is necessary to immediately strive that it be reduced, and not delay that reduction, for if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and quickly, and thereby the reduction of the dislocation is made difficult.
And for this reason, it should not be moved or stretched at the time of inflammation, for many times it causes spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, then you should attend to the phlebotomy of the patient, and let it be left until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated so that what is remembered returns to its place.
And I have now ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what preceded in fracture; from the most superior part of the body to the most inferior part.
Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated, it is not delayed; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are removed from their places by slight movement, and are slightly softened; or they are dislocated by complete and total dislocation, until they are softened at the joint, until the patient's saliva flows and they cannot hold it nor close their mouth, and their tongue is entangled in their speech.
But when their dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns by itself with little effort.
But if the dislocation is complete and total, then it is necessary that reduction be administered to it lightly, and not be delayed at all.
And it is that the attendant holds the head of the patient, and the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocation of the jaw inside their mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he inserts both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands are outside with which he pulls; and he commands the patient to relax their jaws and free them from any movement; and the physician pulls the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if its reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer evaporation with warm water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easy and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And then when they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place on it powders of cloth with cerate, and let it be made of wax and rose oil; and then let it be bound easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their side, and their head be between two pillows, so that it does not move to the right or left; and do not force them to chew anything; before their food, let it be very soft, until when the pains are relieved and the jaws are healed, then let them eat what they want.
And this should be administered easily, and do not work on opening the mouth during eating and drinking and speaking, until the jaw is healed and healthy.
And if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and are not returned to their places, then many times fevers and persistent thirst occur, and the patient's belly is easily distended, and they easily vomit pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is dangerous; and many times he to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and of the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated inwards by its connection with the ribs; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is apparent to the touch.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. Then constrict the area with your palm with firm compression, for it will return.
Then place a plaster and powders on that and bind it tightly.
But the extremity of that which is the elbow and is continuous with it, is not dislocated on any day; then it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as is appropriate to you.
Then place a plaster, and powders, and a bandage on it, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until it is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that a muscle is dislocated in only three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated from the upper part of the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated to the rear parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the front parts by the nerves.
And mostly it is dislocated and comes out from the lower parts below the shoulder, and especially in those whose flesh is little, it is easily dislocated; but in those in whom there is much flesh, it comes out with difficulty.
And it quickly happens to some men through a blow or a fall, and their muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is believed that it is dislocated because of this.
Then it is necessary that you prove this before you are completely certain of it, and then you proceed with its cure.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts below the shoulder; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, because you find a manifest difference between them; and you find in the head of the muscle a concavity below the shoulder near the touching of the head of the muscle like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it through all movements.
If it is dislocated near the feet or above, and you then find a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easily done when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises his hand to the upper parts; and then places his hands below his shoulder, and raises the joint strongly up to its place, and the minister raises his hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it below because it returns lightly.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and from the beginning that the dislocation happened, many days have passed, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and an embrocation be administered that softens and lubricates, such as boiled roots of althea, and fenugreek, and melilot in water, and it should be administered.
And then the patient should lie supine on his back and a wool cushion be placed under his shoulder, which should be equally between softness and firmness, and then the physician places his heel on the cushion, and raises the head of the muscle with force, and pulls the patient's arm so that it is not pulled downwards, and another minister holds the patient's head so that it is not pulled downwards, because then it immediately returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this manner: and it is that you have present a man who is taller than the patient, and stands on that side, and inserts his shoulder under the patient's shoulder and raises his shoulder upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another minister pulls the patient's arm from the lower parts of his belly. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down, because the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced thus according to another method: and it is that a long pole is fixed in the ground, the head of which is round in shape, like a pestle, which is neither thick nor thin, and then it is placed under the patient; after being placed on the head of the pole soft cloths, and the patient is standing along the length of the pole, and then his arm is extended downwards on the other side; and his body is extended on the other side with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if its reduction is difficult for all the reasons we have mentioned, then use this treatment: and it is that you take a stick the length of which is two cubits, and its width is four fingers, and its thickness is reduced at the end for the shoulder according to this form:
And then soft cloths are tied over the round head so that the stick does not injure the patient.
And then it is placed under the patient's shoulder, and the hand or arm is extended over the stick down to the lower parts, and the stick is tied above the shoulder and arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a step of a ladder according to its width, and the hand is extended to the lower parts, and the rest of the body is suspended on the other side, for the joint enters immediately.
And when its entry is completed in any way possible for you, then you must place under the shoulder a ball of wool of equal quantity; and then apply a plaster prepared from powdered comfrey and egg white over the entire muscle, so that it is wrapped over it, and then tighten it below the shoulder with a decent bandage; and the bandage should be wrapped over the plaster, and his hand should be bound to his neck, and it should be left, and his hand should not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's diet be light, until the limb is strengthened, for this is more conducive to his healing.
And then the diet should be increased after seven or five days, and some movement should be exercised; and so if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity that affects it or another cause, then in that case cautery with three punctures should be administered, as previously described in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is untied and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and bandage are reapplied several times, and it is not firm and hangs loose and cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then know that his nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are severed or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
Concerning the dislocation that occurs between the foot and the ankle or the back parts, the reduction is done with impulse and extension with the hands, until it returns [to its place].
And let another bandage be applied to that [area], and care for it until it is healed.
And if during healing heaviness occurs in the limb and slowness in its movement, then let the patient use baths many times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its former natural state.
Chapter 27.
Concerning the treatment of a hip dislocation.
Know that the hip joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and mostly to the front rather than to the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is apparent to sight or touch, according to whatever manner it is dislocated. And when you adjust the dislocated hip with the sound [healthy] one, this is manifestly clear to you, for the joint is concave and he cannot bend his leg, nor touch with that [leg] his thigh.
And it is necessary that you attend to it, and reduce the dislocation quickly before a hot abscess forms there, for if a hot abscess forms there, its reduction is difficult, and it is not easily healed completely; and especially when it is in the back parts, for it is the worst of all other types of dislocation, and most painful, and many times it results in death from it.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that his leg be extended with two hands, and his thigh be extended, and the physician's hands be on the hip and below it, and he should pull the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front parts, then return it thus: bend the leg with your hand, until you strike the hip with the root of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous and very strong extension.
And it is that two assistants extend the arm, and hold it firmly, two assistants so that it is not moved during the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, after it is wrapped in a long doubled cloth over his hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint that hand with some oil so that this may aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with vigorous force until it returns to its place.
And after the reduction, it is necessary that he be placed on a plaster which has styptic and drying properties with egg white, and be bound tightly with a descending bandage, and the arm be suspended from the neck, and left like that for a few days.
And afterwards it should be unbound; and then if the joint is set in its place, then you untie his bandage and leave it.
And if you see the joint is not well tightened, then you repeat the plaster and bandage, and leave it for a few days until it is tightened; and afterwards unbind it.
But if the patient experiences pain after the reduction of the joint, and his condition is in movement (i.e., the pain is ongoing), then administer moist heat in a bath and gentle friction, and compression, until he is relieved; or place on the joint alleviative salves, that is, something very fatty; and then it should be bandaged, and left on the bone day and night. And afterwards remove that, and put it in a bath; and when it is sufficiently softened, then you rub the joint with temperate friction, and afterwards repeat the application of the alleviative salve, that is, very fatty ointment, once, and a second time, and a third. with the aid of a bath, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place on it a warm, moist ox bladder, tied tightly over it; and do this several times, for it is moistening and helps to reduce the inflammation.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the wrist, that is, of the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
Sometimes the wrist is dislocated, and the reduction of its dislocation is easy, unlike other joints. However, it is necessary that there be speed in its reduction at the time it is dislocated, before the area becomes inflamed or a hot abscess forms in it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's wrist joint on a table and the assistant extends the patient's hand, and the physician places his hand or palm on the prominence of the joint, and pulls it until it returns to its place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already facing the belly of the hand; then the patient should place the back of their hand on a table during extension and reduction.  And if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of that hand should be on the table with the palm facing down, so that the physician's hand can fall upon that prominence of the joint.
And therefore, if it returns immediately, it is good; but if not, then constrict it with a poultice to reduce the inflammation and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, you will not endure or be able to reduce it once more days have passed.
However, if the joint remains in its distorted position and causes no harm to the patient except that the hand is weakened and cannot grasp anything, then know that the nerves are pinched or crushed.  Then there is no remedy except to constrict it with a cautery near the injury, and quickly apply some dressing.
Therefore, when the bone is reset, then apply the poultice we have described, and let it remain and constrict for five days; and then let it be untied and left, and let the patient's hand be exercised.
But if its movement is impeded and some severity occurs, then soothe it with warm water and friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in their entirety.
And when one of those fingers is dislocated towards the back of the palm or the belly of the hand, then stretch the finger and gently push the dislocation back with your thumbs until it returns to its place.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it downwards from the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is straightened on that same day.
And when it is night, bind it according to that description, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for some days until it is tight (or strong/stable).
And do similarly if it is dislocated on the palm of the hand, and bind it to that part and perform your first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts (of the body).
Chapter 30.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and many vertebrae are displaced from their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death threatens the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excrement is expelled involuntarily, and they cannot retain it; and many times some of their limbs are paralyzed, or their two legs, or their two arms, or one of these; or one vertebra is displaced from its place, for many times it is displaced and many times the displacement affects four parts.
Therefore, it is already displaced in the back parts and is called yellowness (jaundice - likely a misdiagnosis or symptom overlap).
But the treatment is that you consider if the yellowness (jaundice) comes from consumption (tuberculosis, wasting disease), because then there is no cure for it, nor is there any complete healing.
But in that which comes from a fall or a blow or something similar, the Ancients described the methods of cure with long discourse, of which the most returns with profit; but I have already abridged it,  whose brevity excuses the excess,  due to my proximity to my intended explanation, with which I have exposed and formed this from various instruments with which I will show and explain this.
And of these, only that which occurs in the two [sides/feet] properly, with what I have recounted, is cured.
And it is so that the patient should be laid on his back against a wall, and a soft mat should be placed under him so that his feet are not hurt.
And then a straight pole should be placed, fixed in the earth at the head end of the bed near his head, and another pole near his feet at the other end of the bed.
And the assistant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another assistant should hold the other one in the same way.
Then wrap a strong linen bandage around the patient's head and neck; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole which is by his head, and tie it there; and then tighten another bandage around his hips, thighs, and legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole which is by his feet.
And then each assistant should extend the bandage to its respective pole, and the two poles and the bandages should not be removed from their places where they are fixed, but nevertheless they should not be fastened as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with firmness until it returns, or place a board on it, and then adjust his feet on the board until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, then take a board whose width or length is about three braccia, and make a hole in the wall which we have said is near the patient, into which the end of the board fits. And then place half the board on the spine, and the physician place his feet on the other end, and press firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, use a revolving drill turned by hand; and you should place in the ground, near the patient's head and behind the buttocks, two poles, whose length is one braccio, and near the head in the back of the buttocks two more poles; and let the distance between each pole be one palm, and let there be a hole made in each pole through which the drill passes, and let each be firmly fixed in the ground so that it is not moved.
And let there be a round pole on which the drill is mounted, and let it be rotated in the two holes of the two poles.
And at its end is a hole into which the drill is fitted, the length of which is one palm including that which is rotated; and in the other two poles is something similar to that.
And then tighten the ligatures that you have tied to the patient's feet in the hole which is near their head, and the ligatures that you have tied to their thighs in the hole which is near their feet.
And then near each drill let there be a person who turns the drill with their hand, and the physician manipulates the vertebra as we have said.
And then, once the vertebra is returned and the area is manipulated, it is necessary that you apply a drying plaster with egg white, and then lint; and then place over the plaster a piece of linen cloth, the width of which should be three fingers or thereabouts, and the length should be sufficient to cover the area of the swelling, and over the part of the affected vertebra.
And then it should be bound with the ligature which we have mentioned, and the patient should be given light nourishment until they are healed.
And if any swelling remains in the area at the end of the healing process, then medicines should be administered that soften and smooth, with the application of the linen cloth for a long time, which we have recommended; and a lead plate is then applied to that area.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the area behind the vertebrae of the back, and it is caused by that dislocation; and that is a bone which already protrudes, so that no attempt should be made to treat it with this medicine, for it will very quickly cause death.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint are subject only to dislocation, and that which happens to them does not happen to other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and less often forwards or backwards.
And a sign of its dislocation inwards is that, when you compare the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg is longer and the knee more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot without pain, and it is in that place that the pain is sometimes accompanied by a hot abscess, because the head of the femur has already reached there.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens outwards is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And a sign of dislocation forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but nevertheless cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he forces himself to walk, he cannot do so forwards; and his urine is retained, and his groin is inflamed, and he walks on his heel.
And a sign of dislocation backwards is that he cannot extend his knee, nor can he bend it before bending his hip, and his leg is shorter than the other, and his groin is softened, and the head of the femur is towards that place.
But the reduction of these types of dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and longstanding, that it has already become established, and if you attempt reduction and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not entirely curable; and then no presumption should be made in this matter.
But in the case where the dislocation is recent, and is one of the four types of dislocation, then take care that you return the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, for it quickly returns and does not need further treatment.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare strong assistants and extend his leg from the lower part, either with your hands, or with a bandage which is tied above the knee; and another assistant extend his hand from above in such a way that he places his hands under his armpits; and then tighten a strong bandage around the root of the thigh, and let another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be from the inside of the groin to the area of the clavicle, or from the back to the area of the spine.
And let the extension of all these be at once until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four (types of dislocation).
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type [of dislocation] that which we will say of its proper cure.
In the reduction of the hip itself, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inside, the patient should be supine on his sound side; and then place the ligatures on the root of the thigh in the space between the head of the thigh and the place which is below the groin; and then extend the ligature from the groin to the highest part of the clavicle.
And then another strong assistant grasps his arms, and pulls the thick part of the affected thigh and extends it outwards with a vigorous extension, because then it returns to its place.
And this type is of easier cure, with which this member is reduced, than the other types.
But if it is dislocated upwards and does not yield to treatment with this type of cure at all, then it is necessary that both feet of the patient be tied together with a strong, light ligature, above both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each (ligature) be the quantity of four digits, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two digits.
And then the patient should be suspended by the head from a beam, and let him be inside the house; and let his height from the ground be the quantity of two arms' lengths.
And then order a strong young man to reduce the head of the thigh, and let another young man suspend the patient, and the other young man, reducing the thigh with forceful pulling, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is a dislocation outwards, it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, as we have recounted in the case of the cold abscess; and let the ligature be tightened on the affected leg specifically and on his feet.
And then two beams should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; and then let adjusted beams be placed in the middle of the elevated part on which wet cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the beam is between his thighs, so that during the extension to the lower parts it is not pulled.
And then each assistant extends on his side, and the physician with his hand guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the anterior parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narration on an elevation; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the dislocated joint of the patient, and then grasps that with the other hand, and with that grasp places that which is extended to the lower parts at the joint of the knee.
And if the dislocation is in the posterior parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended to the lower parts, and he is elevated above the ground; instead, it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for the elevation if his hip is dislocated to the outside, according to what we have said about his lying on two hips; and he is on his face and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, there is little difference.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board on the place where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as necessary — and the sign of his reduction is not hidden from you, and that is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those equal and the patient stretches his leg and extends it without difficulty, then know that the limb is returned as necessary — and then you adjust the two thighs and apply a plaster and bind it so that the hip is not moved in any of the parts; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
And then untie the bandage and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then know that the dislocation is now healed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and plaster that, and bind it according to your first binding, and leave it for three days.
And then untie it, and have him delay walking on it for a few days, until it is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of knee dislocation.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, and inwards, and downwards, more towards the back; and it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to bring their leg to their thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its types is that you make the patient lie down straight, so that they extend their leg, if they can; adhere your two hands to the front of the knee, and place their foot between your feet; and then make the patient's knee adhere to your palms, and hold those between your fingers above the knee with strength, and another assistant extend their foot until the knee returns to its place.
And a sign of its reduction is that their leg adheres to the thigh easily and without effort.
And then plaster that and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days, until they are healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction is difficult for you, then administer strong extension with the bandages of which the use in the treatment of the hip was described previously.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the talus (ankle bone).
The talus is sometimes displaced by a slight movement, and sometimes dislocated completely, and its dislocation is made either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the talus swells, protruding towards the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its slight displacement is gentle reduction, and this is done by extending it easily with the hands and gently pulling it until it returns to its place.
But in treating it when it is completely dislocated, you must have the patient lie down straight, and have a strong and capable assistant hold him from behind at the back of his thigh in the middle of the leg.
Then you, with your right hand, take the foot from its uppermost part, and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot at the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left hand, twice, and then extend it with your left; and gently push the foot with your right hand down the leg without effort.
And do this twice, as we have described.
Then push the foot against the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
Then it will return in one or two tries, according to this method, and if you see the foot set, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, because that will return it.
But if the reduction is forbidden to you by what we have recounted, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and tighten a firmly fixed rope on the ground that lies between their thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth around them so that it does not injure the patient.
Then let the patient's attendant hold their thighs, and let another attendant extend the foot with their hands or with a bandage that is tied around the ankle; and then let each attendant extend against the extension of their pair, and let the rope remain between the patient's thighs, holding it, so that their body is not pulled downwards beyond its extension.
Then let the physician set the dislocation with his hands, and let another attendant hold the healthy thigh below, because the dislocation returns easily.
And so when the dislocation is returned and the success of its reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And it is necessary that you fear or guard against the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over it is not tight and strongly injures them.
Then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is loosened, tighten it.
Then unbind it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be loosened and corrupted again, and consequently that it will not receive a cure.
And if a hot abscess occurs to him, then it is necessary that you administer to its evacuation that which we described previously, in another place of treatment and embrocation, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocations of the toes.
It is necessary that what is dislocated be healed with little extension without effort; and this is not difficult, rather it is light/easy.
But if the dislocation is in some bone of the big toe, then it is necessary that you make the patient put his foot on the ground or on a table, and be positioned as if he were walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on what protrudes from the joints, and then press that with your foot with force until it returns, and you see that it is healed and no prominence appears in the place.
And then place under the sole of his foot a board that covers the entire foot, to which two ends are attached, and then tighten it with a decent and firm bandage for three days; and then untie it and have him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its recovery is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that are made with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When any of those causes occur, and you engage in its treatment and restoration, death often ensues.
And therefore, one should not presume to treat it, except someone skilled in the art, of long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not rash or presumptuous; and who uses, at the beginning, only medicines that reduce hot abscesses, and leaves the patient to divine judgment, except for the one in whom you expect salvation from perdition, with alleviation of the illness, and in whom some hope appears. And then strive immediately to reduce it at the beginning of the cause, before a hot abscess develops.
And then, if the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen that reduces hot abscesses and treat the wound with what is appropriate for it, of desiccative ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and the shattered bone pieces occur, then you must attempt to join them, and do in this what we have said in the simple illnesses that precede in those locations.
And guard and avoid, according to your virtue, that entry into paths of fear as my preaching has preceded you; for that is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more salutary to your true body in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
